**Implication:** Before your next big innovation challenge — a product launch, a pivot, a strategic planning session — ask whether your team has been doing the creative equivalent of daily training, or whether they're showing up cold.

**The fear of being behind has a direction — it points at a closable gap.** Karpathy's framing of AI's challenge as a 'skill issue' is significant: it categorizes the problem as learnable and actionable, not existential. The right move when feeling behind is to start, pick one thing, and run the experiment. ([source](Not Behind Enough to Panic. Too Afraid to Learn.))

**Implication:** Behind-fear, properly reframed, is one of the most powerful motivators available. The goal is not to eliminate this feeling but to channel it toward the smallest possible concrete next action.

**The most innovative people share a common instinct.** when stuck, they go seek external input rather than grinding harder on the problem. This externally-directed reflex is what separates prolific innovators from those who stall. Fresh thinking is downstream of fresh input. ([source](Episode 248: Jeremy Utley — unSILOed Podcast))

> *"The most innovative individuals have this instinct to go and seek input, that drives fresh thinking when they're stuck."*

**Implication:** When you hit a creative wall, the productive move is not to push harder but to go looking — for analogies, adjacent solutions, or unexpected perspectives. Train this as a reflex.

**History's greatest innovators — from Benjamin Franklin to Steve Jobs to Frank Lloyd Wright — shared a counterintuitive habit: they regularly walked away from their work. Napping, gardening, and mundane activities weren't distractions but essential parts of their creative process. They created space for inspiration to arrive rather than forcing it.** ([source](Episode 248: Jeremy Utley — unSILOed Podcast))

**Implication:** High-output creators should deliberately schedule downtime and disengagement as part of their innovation practice. Stepping away is not laziness — it is a cognitive strategy for allowing subconscious processing and unexpected connections.

**Jeremy advocates for expanding one's professional network beyond a small recurring team as a practical habit for becoming a better idea generator. Repeated collaboration with the same small group limits the diversity of inputs that fuel novel thinking.** ([source](Maximizing the Flow of Ideas for Your Organization))

> *"To become a better idea generator, ensure that you are working with a wide net of professionals and not the same small team every week."*

**Implication:** Idea quality is partly a function of network diversity. Leaders who want to generate better ideas should deliberately introduce new perspectives into their working environment — not just rely on trusted inner circles.

**Utley proposes a 'begun' mentality toward creativity — treating innovation as a daily practice rather than something that happens in periodic sprints or special sessions. Innovation is incorporated into how you work, not scheduled as a separate event.** ([source](Jeremy Utley Media Hub))

**Implication:** Organizations that silo innovation into quarterly offsites or dedicated 'innovation labs' are structurally limiting their creative output. The more powerful model is building micro-creative habits into everyday work rhythms.

**Utley's practical guidance for navigating the AI transition centers on four moves.** become a comfortable AI collaborator, focus on zones of intrinsic interest, value judgment over generation, and embrace the weird. Together these form a personal positioning strategy for the age of exponential ideaflow. ([source](Exponential Ideaflow: Why AI Will Surpass Human Creativity))

**Implication:** Professionals should use these four moves as a self-assessment framework: Am I collaborating with AI? Do I know what I care about? Am I developing taste? Am I willing to test the strange ideas? Weakness on any dimension is a vulnerability in the AI era.

**Utley identifies his fingers as his creative bottleneck — they slow the stream of consciousness and cause ideas to be lost before they're captured. His solution is to dictate prompts at the speed of thought rather than type them, treating voice as the primary input modality for ideation. This preserves the raw, unfiltered quality of ideas as they emerge.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas))

> *"There are no bad ideas. When you treat GenAI as a collaborator and sparring partner, not an oracle, the answer is always yes."*

**Implication:** Speed of capture matters for creative work. Creators should remove friction between thought and record — whether through voice dictation, AI transcription, or other tools — to stop losing ideas at the bottleneck of physical input.

**Utley's daily creative ritual involves hours at a standing desk, mixing dictation into ChatGPT, typed input into Claude, and physical sticky notes scattered across his workspace. This hybrid analog-digital environment reflects a deliberate choice to use multiple modalities simultaneously rather than going all-in on any single tool. The messiness is the method.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas))

> *"Not to mention furiously scribbling sticky notes that are strewn all over his desk."*

**Implication:** Deep creative work often requires a messy, multi-channel environment rather than a clean, single-tool workflow. Leaders building innovation cultures should resist over-systematizing creative processes — the friction between modalities can itself be generative.

**Utley's keynote takeaway — that everyone should block 15 minutes daily to try something they've learned — reflects his core belief that the real value of learning lies in implementation, not absorption. The blog post he develops in this session is itself designed to operationalize that insight: learning only becomes useful when someone acts on it immediately and concretely.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas))

> *"As a next step, everybody should block 15 minutes on their calendar per day to try something they've learned. I think that's the takeaway for the piece."*

**Implication:** Learning events — keynotes, courses, books — have low ROI unless followed immediately by a protected implementation window. Leaders should build '15-minute try it' rituals into team learning programs to close the knowing-doing gap.

---

## Input Quality & Creative Fuel

**What you bring to an AI model — your experience, perspective, and the inspiration you've cultivated — is the primary differentiator of output quality. Access to the model is equal; the human inputs are not.** ([source](youtube:How to Master AI Powered Creativity in Just 13 Minutes | Jeremy Utley))

> *"Everybody has the same access to the same ChatGPT. How do I get a different output than you do? It's because of what I bring to the model. And what do I bring to the model? Certainly I bring technique, but I also bring my experience. I bring my perspective. I bring all the inspiration I've gleaned from the world. That's what gets a user a differential output from a model."*

**Implication:** Investing in personal creative development, diverse experience, and rich inputs is not separate from AI strategy — it is the core of AI competitive advantage.

**Imagination is a function of unexpected input, and seniority insulates leaders from the unexpected.** The more senior you become, the more filtered your information environment, and therefore the more constrained your imagination — making deliberate exposure to unexpected sources of information a strategic leadership discipline. ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

> *"Imagination is a function of unexpected input. And often times the more senior you get in an organization, the more insulated you get from any unexpected input. Therefore your imagination gets sheltered. And so what you got to do is you've got to be deliberate about exposing yourself to unexpected sources of information."*

**Implication:** Senior leaders who want to cast bold AI visions must actively engineer serendipitous, cross-domain information exposure into their routines — their default environment will systematically starve their imagination.

**Creative output — and AI output — is a direct function of input quality and diversity.** Your imagination is bounded by your experiential and informational diet: what you read, listen to, and engage with predicts what you will be able to conceive and generate. ([source](youtube:Stanford-Jeremy-Utley-AI-brainstorming))

> *"Your imagination is a function of your experience... show me what you're inputting show me what you're listening to what you're reading I can predict with a high degree of confidence what kind of output to expect."*

**Implication:** Deliberately upgrading your information diet — podcasts, newsletters, cross-disciplinary exposure — is a direct investment in the quality of both human creativity and AI collaboration.

**Imagination is a function of unexpected input.** The more senior you become inside an organization, the more insulated you are from anything unexpected — your imagination becomes sheltered. Senior leaders must take deliberate responsibility for exposing themselves to fresh sources of inspiration. ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"We know from the research that imagination is a function of unexpected input. And the more senior you get inside of an organization, the more insulated you become from anything unexpected. Your imagination becomes sheltered. So, you've got to take responsibility to expose yourself to fresh sources of inspiration."*

**Implication:** Seniority is an imagination tax unless actively countered — leaders who want to stay creatively relevant must deliberately engineer unexpected inputs into their routines.

**AI now gives individuals the ability to explore ideas from unfamiliar fields, challenge assumptions, illuminate blind spots, and generate alternative perspectives in 10 minutes — creative capacity that would have required hours of research just a few years ago.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"AI gives you a superpower. You can explore ideas from fields you've never studied, challenge your assumptions, illuminate your blind spots, generate alternative perspectives, all in 10 minutes. The creative capacity that you can build in 10 minutes with AI today would have taken hours of research a few years ago."*

**Implication:** AI dramatically lowers the cost of cross-disciplinary creative input — practitioners who build daily AI-assisted exploration habits will compound creative advantages that were previously available only to the broadly educated or well-resourced.

**Great creative thinkers deliberately seek input from outside their own field.** Steve Jobs studied Cuisinart food processors before designing the Apple II; the curves and bevels from kitchen appliances informed his computer design. Breakthroughs rarely come from looking harder within your own domain. ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"Steve Jobs, before he designed the Apple II, spent a weekend haunting the appliance aisles at Macy's in Palo Alto. He's studying Cuisinart food processors of all things. Great thinkers deliberately look for input outside of their field. Jobs didn't get his best design ideas from other computer companies. He got them from blenders."*

**Implication:** Cross-domain input-seeking is not a distraction from creative work — it is the work; building routines that deliberately expose you to unrelated fields is a high-leverage creative habit.

**The more senior a person becomes in an organization, the more insulated they become from unexpected input, which stunts imagination. Creative output is a function of unexpected input, so rising seniority without deliberate countermeasures produces declining creative capacity.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"The more senior you get in an organization, the more insulated you get from any kind of unexpected input and therefore the more sheltered and stunted your imagination becomes. So what you've got to do is deliberately expose yourself to unexpected information."*

**Implication:** Senior leaders must actively engineer diverse, unexpected inputs into their routines or accept that their creative range will narrow as their organizational power grows.

**An idea is most usefully defined as a connection between two things you already know or are learning — not as spontaneous creation from nothing. The brain is neurologically incapable of producing new content ex nihilo; it forges unexpected connections between existing knowledge.** ([source](youtube:talks-at-google-ideaflow))

> *"An idea is a connection, very simply. It turns out our brains, neurologically speaking, are incapable of producing new content from scratch. They don't do it. Ex nihilo creation does not exist in our minds. What our brains do is they make and forge unexpected connections between things we already know or are learning."*

**Implication:** Redefining ideas as connections rather than acts of genius makes idea generation less intimidating and more actionable — the practical focus shifts from waiting for inspiration to actively accumulating and colliding diverse inputs.

**Bette Nesmith Graham invented Wite-Out by connecting two separate domains.** her daily frustration with typewriter smudges and the painter's principle that 'painters don't erase mistakes, painters paint over their mistakes.' Her breakthrough was not spontaneous genius but an unexpected connection between adjacent problems. ([source](youtube:talks-at-google-ideaflow))

> *"You've got a secretary who was constantly frustrated by her typewriter's smudges, who just learned painters paint over their mistakes. If you're a conscious being right now, you realize she invented white-out."*

**Implication:** Breakthrough innovations are almost always recombinations of existing knowledge from different domains — the practical implication is to deliberately seek diverse inputs and expose oneself to problems outside one's primary field.

**An EV engineer solved range anxiety by overhearing soldiers discuss mid-air refueling for jet fighters — connecting the military solution to her automotive problem. This illustrates how solutions from entirely unrelated domains can resolve stubborn problems when a person is actively carrying the right question.** ([source](youtube:talks-at-google-ideaflow))

> *"She said, as I was listening to their conversation, I heard they were talking about jet fighters and how they have a small fuel tank, and how, in the middle of combat, they can't scramble back to the base. They do what's known as a mid-air refueling. Remember, she's trying to solve the problem of range anxiety."*

**Implication:** Actively carrying a well-defined problem while moving through the world dramatically increases the probability of cross-domain connections — the question is the antenna that makes useful signals detectable.

**Businesses tend to focus on outputs while designers are mindful of inputs.** Seeking diverse, rich inputs — observation, conversations, field research — is what enables designers to frame interesting new questions and find non-obvious problems. ([source](stanford-webinar-design-thinking-what-is-it))

> *"Businesses seem to keep us focused on the product, output. It seems like you are saying the creative process needs more inputs... designers are seeking inputs to frame up interesting new questions."*

**Implication:** Organizations that only measure and manage outputs starve their innovation pipeline at the source; building deliberate input-gathering practices is a prerequisite for generating valuable new questions.

**Most people think of creativity in terms of output, but the real leverage point is inputs.** The volume and diversity of inputs — people, inspiration sources, cross-disciplinary exposure — directly constrain the quality and novelty of creative output. Shifting attention upstream to input cultivation is the most effective creative strategy. ([source](talk:zero-to-one-myth))

> *"When most people think of creativity, they think in terms of output. That's a mistake. Think in terms of input. What are the inputs that are influencing and affecting my thinking in terms of people, in terms of inspiration."*

**Implication:** Individuals and organizations seeking creative breakthroughs should audit and deliberately expand the diversity of their input diet before optimizing their idea-generation processes.

**An idea, neurologically, is not something created from nothing — it is a connection between two things already known.** The brain cannot generate new material from scratch; it can only combine existing knowledge in unexpected ways, similar to Lego pieces. This definition demystifies creativity and makes it universally accessible. ([source](talk:zero-to-one-myth))

**Implication:** If ideas are connections rather than creations ex nihilo, the practical path to more ideas is accumulating more and more diverse knowledge inputs — more Lego pieces — and actively seeking unexpected combinations.

**The story of Bette Nesmith Graham illustrates that breakthroughs arise from cross-domain connections: a secretary frustrated with typewriter smudges heard a painter say 'painters paint over mistakes' and invented Liquid Paper. The synthesis moment came from exposure to an unrelated domain that connected to her existing problem. No one starts at zero — they connect what they already know to what they newly encounter.** ([source](talk:zero-to-one-myth))

> *"The moment that the synthesis moment, the light bulb moment was when that painter said something unrelated to her work, but that connected to her work."*

**Implication:** Deliberately seeking exposure to domains, conversations, and contexts unrelated to your current problem is a reliable strategy for generating breakthrough connections.

**Arthur Koestler defined creativity as the collision of apparently unrelated frames of reference.** The practical challenge, then, is how to facilitate such collisions — and the answer often involves deliberately stepping away from the immediate problem rather than intensifying focus on it. Incubation, not efficiency, is where creative breakthroughs are made. ([source](talk:zero-to-one-myth))

> *"Arthur Kessler, the Hungarian philosopher, said creativity is the collision of apparently unrelated frames of reference. And the question is how do you facilitate such collisions?"*

**Implication:** Designing for creative collision — through walks, cross-domain exposure, and deliberate incubation time — is as legitimate and important a work activity as focused problem-solving.

**In his essay on surprising patterns, Utley documents that the people who produce the most generative AI outputs are not those with the most technical sophistication but those with the richest and most varied inputs — about themselves, their domain, and adjacent fields. AI amplifies what you bring to it; if what you bring is narrow, the amplification only makes the narrowness louder.** ([source](A Surprising Pattern Has Emerged in My Work — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Treat AI as a diagnostic on your own input quality. If the outputs feel generic, the problem is almost never the model — it is the poverty of the context, examples, and perspectives you are feeding it. Richer inputs produce richer amplification.

**Utley argues that the habit genius researchers most consistently identify is not output behavior — it is a pattern of consuming widely and then giving ideas time to incubate before evaluation. The creative geniuses who appear to do nothing are in fact doing the most important work: loading their associative memory with rich, varied material and allowing unconscious synthesis to operate on it.** ([source](The Habit That Built Every Genius (It Looks Like Doing Nothing) — video))

**Implication:** Reframe your reading and exploration habits as core professional responsibilities, not discretionary enrichment. The time investment in consuming broadly is the upstream cause of downstream creative output quality.

**Utley describes what he calls a 'Wonder Wander' — deliberately taking a problem out into the physical world and forcing it to collide with whatever you encounter. A stoplight, a nail salon, a hardware store display all become provocations that the brain would never generate on its own. The brain left alone with itself only recirculates what it already knows.** ([source](The Reason You Don't Have Better Ideas — video))

**Implication:** When stuck, don't push harder at the desk. Take the problem for a walk with the explicit instruction to notice what the environment makes you think of. Scheduled exposure to randomness is a genuine creative technique, not a distraction.

**Utley points to the practice of learning from anyone — not just experts in your field — as a core input strategy.** The insight is that expertise in an adjacent domain often contains structural lessons that transfer directly to your problem, but only if you are approaching the encounter with a question already in mind. A master potter and a logistics executive may be solving the same underlying problem with different vocabulary. ([source](How To Learn From Anyone — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Before any meeting, conversation, or conference, load a live problem into working memory. Then approach every person you meet as a potential unexpected source of insight on that problem. The question you carry determines what you are able to hear.

**Utley documents a recurring pattern.** breakthroughs reliably come from outside the domain in which the problem lives. The people solving your industry's hardest problems often come from adjacent fields precisely because they are not constrained by the assumptions your field treats as given. Seeking discomfort through cross-disciplinary exposure is not a soft enrichment activity — it is a structural creativity advantage. ([source](Episode 248: Jeremy Utley — unSILOed Podcast))

**Implication:** Deliberately schedule exposure to fields that feel irrelevant: attend a talk in a discipline you know nothing about, read a trade publication from an unrelated industry, invite someone with zero context on your problem into the room. Irrelevance is often the signal that maximum transfer value is present.

**Utley describes the 'what does this make me think of?' flip as his signature move for activating associative rather than evaluative thinking. When you ask 'what do I think of this?' you enter a critical posture. When you ask 'what does this make me think of?' you enter a generative posture. The linguistic micro-reframe changes the cognitive mode without any additional effort.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Unconventional Paths to Breakthroughs — video))

**Implication:** Train your team — and yourself — to default to the associative question first when encountering any new idea, problem, or input. Evaluation has its place, but it should never be the first cognitive move when the goal is creative expansion.

**Utley identifies 'productive procrastination' as a legitimate and undervalued input strategy.** The period of seemingly doing nothing — reading tangentially, wandering, letting the mind drift across unrelated domains — is when the brain is actually performing its most important connection-making work. Scheduling this kind of incubation time is not indulgence; it is an evidence-backed input practice. ([source](Jeremy Utley on The Power of Productive Procrastination — video))

**Implication:** Protect white space in your calendar not as recovery time but as input-processing time. The creative breakthroughs that feel like they arrived in the shower were built from inputs that needed time to settle and combine — which means cutting all slack from the schedule cuts the creative pipeline.

**Utley describes AI as a new kind of input multiplier.** it can expose you to angles, counterarguments, analogies from other fields, and framings you would not have encountered on your own within the time available. Used well, AI dramatically expands the effective input surface of any creative session — not by replacing human judgment but by serving as a rapid cross-domain synthesizer. ([source](Exponential Ideaflow: Why AI Will Surpass Human Creativity — video))

**Implication:** Use AI explicitly as an input-expansion tool before creative sessions, not just an output-generation tool after them. Ask it to provide perspectives from unrelated fields, historical analogies, or steelman arguments before you begin generating solutions.

**Utley's concept of 'carrying a question' into the world transforms ordinary experience into a continuous input stream.** Rather than consuming inputs passively, you pre-load a live problem into working memory before any experience — a walk, a dinner conversation, a museum visit — and your brain automatically pattern-matches everything you encounter against that problem. The question you bring determines the creative fuel you harvest. ([source](How To Learn From Anyone — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Before any scheduled experience or interaction, take thirty seconds to load your current most important question. This simple habit converts background noise into active input and dramatically increases the yield of everyday life as a creative resource.

**Utley makes the case that exposure to discomfort — deliberately seeking out contexts where you are the least knowledgeable person in the room — is not a soft leadership virtue but a structural input strategy. Discomfort signals that you are encountering genuinely new material, which is the only kind of input capable of producing genuinely new output. Comfortable inputs produce incremental ideas; uncomfortable inputs produce disruptive ones.** ([source](Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Institutionalize discomfort as a design constraint in your learning diet. If every source you consume confirms what you already believe, you are refining existing ideas rather than generating new ones. Seek out the room, the book, or the conversation that makes you feel least qualified.

**Utley notes a paradox in how people approach AI.** they want it to produce novel ideas but give it only the most familiar, generic context. The model can only recombine what it is given in the conversation window. Feeding it richer, stranger, more specific inputs — a transcript from an unrelated field, a provocative constraint, an analogous problem from history — produces proportionally richer outputs. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas — interview))

**Implication:** Treat the AI conversation window as a creative staging area, not a search bar. The quality of what you put in — the specificity, the range of reference material, the provocative framing — is the primary determinant of the quality of what comes out.

**Utley observes that the people who learn fastest are those who approach every interaction — not just formal learning contexts — as a potential input source. The pattern he has documented is that breakthrough innovators treat taxi drivers, strangers at conferences, and overheard conversations as raw material, not noise. The input diet never goes off duty for people who have made curiosity a structural habit rather than an occasional mood.** ([source](How To Learn From Anyone — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Curiosity is not a personality trait but a practice. Formalizing it — through journaling, question-carrying, or end-of-day reflection on what surprised you — converts an intermittent mood into a reliable input engine.

**Utley argues that the scarcest input in most organizations is genuine dissent and honest challenge.** Leaders who surround themselves with confirming voices are limiting their input diet to a single flavor — which produces ideas that are safe, familiar, and incremental by structural necessity. Actively engineering disagreement into your input stream is as important as diversifying industries or disciplines. ([source](Episode 259 – Practicing Innovation with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Audit not just what you read and who you follow, but whether anyone in your environment is consistently challenging your assumptions. If not, the input monoculture is a creativity liability, regardless of how much content you are consuming.

**Utley draws a direct connection between input diversity and the phenomenon he calls 'exponential ideaflow' in the AI era. When AI can rapidly synthesize inputs from across disciplines, the creative ceiling is no longer set by how broadly a single human has read — it is set by how broadly and specifically you can feed the AI. This means the human's job shifts from being a walking encyclopedia to being a skilled input curator and director.** ([source](Exponential Ideaflow: Why AI Will Surpass Human Creativity — video))

**Implication:** In an AI-augmented creative workflow, curation skill becomes more valuable than encyclopedic knowledge. Learning to identify, select, and feed high-quality, diverse inputs to an AI partner is the new creative leverage — and it is a learnable skill, not a fixed trait.

**Creativity is fundamentally a recombination process, not an act of conjuring something from nothing.** Every novel idea is a collision between existing frames of reference — which means the richness of what you produce is directly constrained by the richness of what you consume. Upgrading your inputs is therefore the most reliable lever for upgrading your outputs. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Before trying to think harder or brainstorm longer, audit your input diet. If your reading, conversations, and experiences are narrow, your ideas will be narrow — no amount of effort compensates for a thin input pool.

**Utley argues that the instruction 'think of something new' is operationally useless because it gives the brain no mechanism to work with. The more productive instruction is 'think of connections' — because connections can be engineered by deliberately varying the inputs the brain is exposed to. Reframing creativity as connection-finding transforms it from a mysterious talent into a systematic practice.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Replace vague creative directives in your team with connection prompts: 'What does this problem remind you of in another field?' or 'What would a hotel designer do here?' The mechanism matters more than the aspiration.

**Utley argues that most organizations have a generation problem masquerading as a selection problem.** They build sophisticated frameworks for evaluating and filtering ideas, but apply those frameworks to an impoverished input pipeline. A brilliant filter applied to a thin idea pool still produces mediocre results. The highest leverage point is almost always upstream of evaluation — in expanding the volume and variety of raw material entering the funnel. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Before investing in better decision frameworks or innovation scorecards, ask whether the pool of ideas being evaluated is actually large and diverse enough. If not, the selection process is irrelevant — you need a generation intervention first.

**Utley points to historical exemplars — Picasso, Edison, Linus Pauling — not primarily as proof that quantity leads to quality, but as proof that all of them were voracious, eclectic consumers of inputs across disciplines. Pauling's Nobel-winning chemistry insights were fed by physics and biology; Picasso absorbed African art and transformed cubism. Volume of output was downstream of volume and diversity of input.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Study the input habits of prolific creators, not just their output habits. The lesson from the masters is less about working harder and more about consuming more widely — which is a more actionable and accessible practice for most people.

**Utley uses the metaphor of Lego bricks to describe creative raw material.** the more pieces you have, and the more varied their shapes and colors, the more structures you can build. Someone with a limited set of identical bricks cannot produce novel constructions no matter how creative they are. The constraint is the inventory, not the builder. ([source](Jeremy Utley and Perry Klebahn | Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters | Talks at Google — video))

**Implication:** Actively expanding your 'Lego inventory' — through books outside your field, conversations with people who challenge your assumptions, and deliberate exposure to unfamiliar contexts — is a form of creative capital investment with compounding returns.

**Utley describes design thinking's core diagnostic insight.** most innovation failures are not execution failures but input failures — teams that did not talk to enough diverse people, observe enough edge cases, or immerse themselves deeply enough in the actual lived experience of the problem. Better fieldwork upstream prevents the expensive failure of beautifully executing the wrong solution. ([source](Stanford Webinar - Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care? — video))

**Implication:** Before any ideation session, ask: how many different perspectives have we actually absorbed? If the team has only talked to five customers who look like each other, the input pool is too narrow to produce non-obvious solutions, regardless of the quality of the ideation process.

**Utley consistently frames the Stanford d.school's emphasis on observational fieldwork — watching real people struggle with real problems in real contexts — as the most underrated input practice in innovation. Observation done well is not just data collection; it is a form of perspective transplant that restructures the observer's assumptions from the inside, making certain solution directions feel obviously right that would never have emerged from desk research.** ([source](Stanford Webinar - Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care? — video))

**Implication:** Replace some of your secondary research time with direct observation. One hour watching someone actually use your product, navigate your service, or work around the problem you are trying to solve generates more generative input than ten hours of survey analysis.

**Utley connects the concept of input quality directly to ideaflow measurement.** teams with high ideaflow almost always have deliberately varied input practices — they read widely, interview edge-case users, and seek out perspectives that challenge their assumptions. Teams with low ideaflow tend to have homogeneous input habits regardless of how hard they work during ideation sessions. The session itself is too late in the process to compensate for input poverty. ([source](The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Measure and manage input diversity as a leading indicator of ideaflow, just as ideaflow is a leading indicator of innovation output. Ask: over the past month, what genuinely new perspectives has this team been exposed to? The answer predicts next quarter's idea quality.

**Utley identifies a specific failure mode he sees repeatedly in corporate innovation efforts.** teams that restrict their reference points to their own industry. They study only competitors, benchmark only against peers, and hire only people with directly relevant experience. The result is the creative equivalent of inbreeding — small, incremental variations on existing forms because the entire input gene pool is too narrow. ([source](Maximizing the Flow of Ideas for Your Organization — interview))

**Implication:** Actively seek benchmark comparisons and input sources from industries structurally analogous to yours but superficially unrelated. Fast food logistics, military decision-making, film production, emergency medicine — all contain operational wisdom that transfers with high creative yield across sector boundaries.

**Utley endorses Paul Graham's practice of deliberately seeking out what unusual experts know that others don't — and doing so out of genuine interest, not conversational strategy. The distinction matters: authentic curiosity produces better conversations than performed interest. Graham's framing reinforces that this practice is about real learning, not social maneuvering.** ([source](How To Learn From Anyone))

> *"When I meet someone who knows a lot about something unusual (which includes practically everyone, if you dig deep enough), I try to learn what they know that other people don't. There are almost always surprises here... I really want to know."*

**Implication:** For innovators and leaders, developing genuine intellectual curiosity — not just conversational technique — is the foundational skill that makes learning from anyone possible and sustainable.

**Seemingly irrelevant information encountered through curious conversation can prove unexpectedly valuable later.** Utley sees this as a reason to stay open and curious even when a topic feels tangential or unrelated to your current work. Cross-domain inputs often become the seeds of breakthrough ideas. ([source](How To Learn From Anyone))

**Implication:** Builders and creators should resist filtering conversations for immediate utility. Absorbing broadly — and trusting that unexpected connections emerge over time — is a key driver of creative insight.

**The single biggest mistake people make with AI is providing insufficient context.** Most people write five-word prompts — essentially Google searches — and are disappointed by the output. Utley recommends never prompting a model without at least 400 words of context, treating the AI like a new team member who needs full onboarding, not a search engine. ([source](Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley))

> *"I would recommend never prompting a model without at least 400 words of context. Most people say, 'My average prompt is five words.' Well, that's a Google search... If you want to work with an LLM, treat it like you would treat a new team member. Imagine if we were starting a job together and I turned to you and said, 'Make social media strategy now.' That's five words. And it's absurd, right? We would never treat a human colleague that way. But we do that with AI all the time."*

**Implication:** Before prompting AI for creative or strategic work, invest time establishing context: brand voice, objectives, tone, market, constraints. The depth of your setup directly determines the usefulness of what you get back.

**To change the output of your thinking, you must attend to the inputs.** Jeremy emphasizes that the quality and variety of what you consume, encounter, and expose yourself to directly shapes the ideas you are capable of producing. Garbage in, garbage out — but also: novelty in, novelty out. ([source](The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Creators and leaders should be deliberate curators of their information diet. Changing what you read, watch, and who you talk to is a direct intervention on the quality and originality of your ideas.

**The inspiration needed to solve organizational problems often lives outside the organization.** Jeremy argues that looking inward for creative solutions is a limiting strategy, and that external perspectives, industries, and stimuli are essential sources of fresh ideas. ([source](The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Organizations should systematically build in exposure to outside industries, customers, and thinkers. Innovation dies when teams only talk to themselves.

**Inspiration is not a passive, mystical event but a disciplined practice of seeking unexpected input.** Utley argues that actively and systematically pursuing novel inputs is the foundational method for solving problems. The discipline lies in consistently going out to find what you don't already know. ([source](Episode 248: Jeremy Utley — unSILOed Podcast))

> *"Inspiration is the disciplined pursuit of unexpected input. And every one of those words matters, but being disciplined in your pursuit of input is the way to solve problems."*

**Implication:** Leaders and creators should treat inspiration as a repeatable process, not a lucky accident. Build structured routines for consuming unexpected inputs — outside your industry, domain, or comfort zone.

**Solutions to your problems often already exist somewhere in the world — just not in your industry.** If you're thoughtful about where you look, you can stumble upon novel solutions that would never appear within your own domain. Cross-industry analogical thinking is a powerful but underused innovation tool. ([source](Episode 248: Jeremy Utley — unSILOed Podcast))

> *"Just because you don't know how to solve a problem doesn't mean it hasn't been solved in the world more broadly. And a lot of times, if you're thoughtful about where you go looking, you stumble upon novel solutions that you never would've seen in your own industry."*

**Implication:** Deliberately seek out how other industries, disciplines, or domains have solved structurally similar problems. The solution to your most pressing challenge may already exist — just not where you're looking.

---

## Creative Confidence & Mindset

**Every human being has innate creative capacity, but most people don't recognize or claim it.** Unlocking others' creativity is the core work of creative education — not teaching technique but releasing latent potential. ([source](youtube:How to Master AI Powered Creativity in Just 13 Minutes | Jeremy Utley))

> *"I fully and fundamentally believe every single human being has innate creative capacity. Every single one of us. What the D.school has helped me do is unlock others. Everyone has this latent creative capacity."*

**Implication:** Creative development programs should be framed as unlocking what is already present rather than installing something new — a reframe that dramatically lowers psychological resistance.

**Creative people should not fear AI — they are about to be empowered by it in unprecedented ways.** AI removes many of the execution barriers that have historically constrained creative individuals. ([source](youtube:How to Master AI Powered Creativity in Just 13 Minutes | Jeremy Utley))

> *"Creators don't need to be afraid of AI. Creators need to dive in. They need to lean in. Creators are about to be unleashed in a way they've never been unleashed before."*

**Implication:** The narrative of AI as a threat to human creativity is backwards — for those who engage with it as a creative partner, AI represents the greatest amplification of creative capacity in history.

**People unconsciously build a quality filter that blocks bad ideas, but this filter symmetrically blocks exceptional ideas too. The wall that keeps out terrible ideas is the same wall that keeps out genius. Permissiveness in both directions is required.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"They put up a wall here, and that puts up a wall on their performance here. And so, paradoxically, one of the greatest levers you have to better ideas, better performance, better output at your work, is to let yourself have worse ideas."*

**Implication:** The act of self-censorship in idea generation is not quality control — it is creative suppression. Removing the filter on bad ideas is the most direct path to unlocking better ones.

**The reason most people don't have better ideas is not lack of intelligence, training, or talent — it is that they don't allow themselves to have worse ideas. The deficit is one of permission, not capability.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"The reason you don't have better ideas is because you don't have worse ideas."*

**Implication:** Improving idea quality does not require more skill-building or more information — it requires removing the self-imposed restriction on producing bad ideas. The fix is attitudinal, not educational.

**The AI moment represents a universal invitation to enter the 'learn-it-all' class — to model the identity of continuous learner rather than already-arrived expert. Leaders who publicly admit what they don't know and use AI to find answers in real time create more engagement and trust than those who perform omniscience.** ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

**Implication:** AI provides leaders with a practical, repeatable mechanism for modeling intellectual humility — using AI visibly to answer questions they don't know signals the learn-it-all identity more powerfully than any speech about growth mindset.

**Productive failure and 'scraping your knees' is the irreplaceable mechanism for building AI fluency — it cannot be shortcut by reading, watching, or listening. Learning to ride the 'electric bike' of AI requires actual falls.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet): How to Learn AI in 12 Minutes))

> *"You'll probably scrape your knees. That's the only way to learn how to ride the bike, or what I call the electric bike of the month."*

**Implication:** Practitioners and organizations should create psychological permission for AI missteps and failed experiments, because productive failure is the only path to genuine fluency.

**The dominant emotion most people feel when AI is mentioned is fear.** The journey from fear to effective AI use follows a predictable arc: fear → familiar → fluent → fun. Knowing this arc converts the discomfort of early AI engagement from shame into expected curriculum. ([source](youtube:Stanford-Jeremy-Utley-AI-brainstorming))

> *"The predominant emotion most folks feel when AI gets mentioned is fear and what we're trying to do is let's move people from fear to familiar to fluent to we're having fun."*

**Implication:** AI adoption programs should explicitly name and normalize the fear-to-fun progression — framing early discomfort as a predictable stage rather than evidence that AI 'isn't for them.'

**Care and personal investment are prerequisites for effective use of any innovation tool, including AI.** If you don't genuinely care about the problem you're trying to solve, no tool — AI or otherwise — will compensate for that lack of motivation. ([source](youtube:Stanford-Jeremy-Utley-AI-brainstorming))

> *"In the context of innovation if you don't care don't bother meaning there's all sorts of amazing tools around ideation and prototyping and user testing... but if you don't care about the problem we're trying to solve then don't bother using any of them."*

**Implication:** AI deployment should be anchored to problems people are genuinely motivated to solve — deploying AI against low-stakes or low-care problems will produce low engagement and reinforce the belief that AI doesn't work.

**Admitting you don't have all the answers and inviting AI in feels like weakness, but it is actually the professional standard — your responsibility is to do the best possible work, and not inviting AI in produces mediocre work by choice.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"I was a little embarrassed, to be honest, because it felt like I'm admitting I don't have all the answers. But then I realized that's not a weakness. My responsibility is to do the best possible work, and not inviting AI in would result in mediocre work."*

**Implication:** Reframing AI collaboration from 'admitting inadequacy' to 'fulfilling professional responsibility' is the psychological shift that unlocks consistent AI use among high-status knowledge workers.

**The real competitive advantage is not intelligence, connections, or hard work — it is how you think about your work.** An ordinary person who decides to think differently for 45 minutes can create systems that produce decades of leverage. ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"Adam of the National Park Service wasn't a genius. He's a great guy, but he's an ordinary person, just like you and me. He's not a programmer, he's a facilities manager who decided to think differently about his work for 45 minutes. And that decision is saving 20 years of human labor across the National Park Service every single year. The real advantage isn't intelligence, it isn't connections, it isn't even hard work. It's how you think about your work."*

**Implication:** Democratizing leverage means that the primary barrier to outsized impact is not access to resources or talent — it is adopting the mental models and practices that direct ordinary effort toward extraordinary outcomes.

**Knowing the stories of how great innovators actually worked gives people language and permission to adopt unconventional approaches themselves. Without these reference points, individuals default to conventional behavior even when it fails them creatively. Historical examples serve as cognitive licenses for different ways of working.** ([source](youtube:-Ld6D78NVLQ))

> *"I find that it's useful to know these stories because all of a sudden we start having language for the kinds of work that are required."*

**Implication:** Sharing stories of unconventional creative practice is not mere inspiration — it is the mechanism by which people gain permission and vocabulary to work differently themselves.

**Intelligence and creativity are not the same thing, and our education systems conflate them by rewarding IQ and credentials over creative practice. High-GPA students frequently struggle in design challenges while less decorated peers produce breakthrough work — because creative output depends on learned habits, not academic performance.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"You get a student, got the highest GPA, the most impressive resume, everybody expects him to crush it and they struggle in our design challenges. Meanwhile, another student maybe less polished, less decorated, produces breakthrough work. Year after year, I see the same pattern emerge."*

**Implication:** Hiring and team composition decisions based on pedigree over creative habit will reliably underperform; organizations should assess creative practice directly.

**The dominant myth of innovation — 'zero to one' — implies that a lone genius creates something from nothing.** In reality, innovation is a craft that anyone can develop through practice. Framing creativity as a genetic gift rather than a learnable skill is the core barrier preventing most people from contributing creatively. ([source](youtube:talks-at-google-ideaflow))

> *"We don't believe in zero to one people, necessarily. We believe that innovation is a craft, is an ability that you can hone with practice. And yet it remains shrouded in mystery."*

**Implication:** Organizations and individuals who treat creativity as a trainable skill rather than a fixed trait will systematically outperform those who wait for genius to appear.

**Most people's instinct when they hear '2,000 ideas' is that ideas must be big, rare, and intimidating — like tigers lurking in a jungle. This fear stems from conflating ideas with polished outputs. When ideas are redefined as simple connections, the volume target becomes approachable rather than paralyzing.** ([source](youtube:talks-at-google-ideaflow))

**Implication:** Demystifying what counts as an idea — a simple connection, a half-formed observation, a 'what if' question — is a prerequisite to building ideation volume; without this reframe, the quantity target will always feel impossible.

**Expertise is increasingly becoming a liability because past knowledge is not predictive of future success.** In rapidly changing environments, deep experience in what worked before can actively constrain one's ability to see new solutions. ([source](youtube:Stanford Instructor - Meet Jeremy Utley))

> *"I don't think expertise is increasingly becoming a liability because a knowledge of what worked in the past is not predictive of what's going to work in the future and so you can't just lean on your past experience."*

**Implication:** Organizations and individuals who prize domain expertise above beginner's mindset risk being outmaneuvered by less experienced but more adaptable thinkers.

**Effective innovation requires bringing your full self to every problem in a fresh and profound way — rather than defaulting to past templates. This demands a posture of genuine openness that expertise can erode over time.** ([source](youtube:Stanford Instructor - Meet Jeremy Utley))

> *"You actually have to believe that you can bring your full self to bear in a profound new and fresh way every time you approach a problem."*

**Implication:** Creative effectiveness is as much a belief system as a skill set — practitioners must actively maintain the conviction that fresh approaches are possible, even when experience tempts them toward familiar solutions.

**The dominant mental model of innovation — going from zero to one, from nothing to something — is a myth that disempowers people. No one actually starts from zero; breakthroughs emerge from accumulation, not from thin air. Mythologizing creativity as a rare leap from nothing prevents ordinary people from seeing themselves as capable innovators.** ([source](talk:zero-to-one-myth))

> *"I don't believe in 0 to 1 people and I think it's actually incredibly disempowering to think of oneself as not a 0 to 1 person."*

**Implication:** Reframing innovation as a cumulative, learnable process rather than a magical leap makes it accessible to everyone, not just rare geniuses.

**Utley describes a predictable progression from fear to familiar to fluent to fun when anyone takes on a new creative challenge. What most people interpret as evidence that they lack creative ability — the discomfort and awkwardness of early attempts — is actually the required first stage of a known developmental arc. Badness is curriculum, not diagnosis.** ([source](You're Not Behind (yet): How to Learn AI in 12 Minutes — video))

**Implication:** Reframe early creative struggle explicitly for teams: name the stage they are in, show the arc, and make clear that discomfort is the entry fee, not a signal to exit. This single reframe prevents most people from quitting the process before it works.

**Utley argues that scraping your knees is a necessary part of learning to ride a bike — and that this obvious truth about physical skill development is inexplicably forgotten when it comes to creative work. People expect creative fluency without the equivalent of falling off the bike, then interpret the falls as evidence they should stop rather than evidence they are learning.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: AI Ignites Human Creativity — video))

**Implication:** Build explicit failure tolerance into creative development programs — not as a platitude but as a structural feature. Teams should be able to point to their 'scraped knees' as a portfolio of evidence that they are progressing, not failing.

**Utley describes a pattern he calls productive procrastination — the seemingly idle time spent walking, showering, or staring out windows — as one of the most consistently undervalued creative behaviors. Rather than being a failure of discipline, this unfocused time is when the brain makes the associative connections that focused work cannot produce. The habit that built every genius often looks like doing nothing.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on The Power of Productive Procrastination — video))

**Implication:** Protect unscheduled, unproductive-looking time as a creative asset rather than eliminating it in the name of efficiency. Leaders who schedule every hour of their teams' days are structurally preventing the creative synthesis that only emerges in the gaps.

**Utley observes a surprising pattern in his work.** the people who show up to creativity workshops most convinced they are not creative frequently become the most engaged and productive participants once given the right conditions. The identity label 'not creative' is doing no predictive work — it is simply a story that has never been tested under the right conditions. ([source](A Surprising Pattern Has Emerged in My Work — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Do not pre-sort teams into 'creatives' and 'non-creatives' when designing innovation initiatives. The label is not a reliable predictor of contribution under proper facilitation conditions — and acting on it becomes a self-fulfilling prophecy that wastes enormous organizational talent.

**Utley links the current anxiety around AI adoption directly back to the same creative confidence dynamic he has observed for years: the fear is not of the technology but of being seen as someone who does not know what they are doing. The emotional experience of 'not creative' and 'not good at AI' are structurally identical — both are identity threats wearing the costume of capability gaps.** ([source](Not Behind Enough to Panic. Too Afraid to Learn. — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** AI adoption programs that lead with skill-building before addressing the underlying identity threat will continue to underperform. The first conversation is not 'here is how to prompt' but 'it is normal to feel exposed right now, and here is why that is a sign of engagement, not inadequacy.'

**Utley argues that the live demonstration — building something real with AI in front of a skeptical audience using their own strategic questions — is the most reliable way to shift someone's creative self-concept in relation to a new capability. Witnessing expands what a person believes is possible for themselves in a way that explanation and argument cannot replicate.** ([source](How to Master AI Powered Creativity in Just 13 Minutes | Jeremy Utley — video))

**Implication:** When introducing any new creative capability to a resistant audience, lead with a live demonstration using their real problems — not a prepared example. The moment someone sees their own question produce a surprising result, the identity shift begins. No amount of persuasion achieves what one witnessed demonstration does.

**Utley challenges the assumption that unconventional paths to creative breakthroughs are lucky accidents.** He argues that deliberately introducing yourself to diverse domains, unfamiliar disciplines, and apparently unrelated fields is itself a trainable creative practice — one that systematically increases the probability of novel connections. Serendipity is an engineerable condition. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Unconventional Paths to Breakthroughs — video))

**Implication:** Formalize cross-domain exposure as a creative practice on your team: assign reading outside your industry, bring in speakers from unrelated fields, run Wonder Wander exercises. Stop treating breakthrough ideas as gifts and start treating the conditions for them as work.

**Creative confidence is not discovered all at once — it is accumulated through a series of small, low-stakes wins that gradually rewire self-perception. Each successful creative act, however minor, produces evidence that contradicts the 'I'm not creative' story. Over time, the accumulated evidence becomes stronger than the original belief.** ([source](Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Design creative exercises and innovation sprints with early, achievable wins built in — not to be easy, but to generate the evidence trail that rewires self-perception. The sequencing of creative challenges matters as much as their content.

**Utley draws a sharp distinction between performance and practice in creative work.** Most people only show up creatively when they feel ready — which is exactly backwards. Readiness is produced by repeated low-stakes practice, not by waiting. Treating every creative session as a performance prevents the reps that would eventually make performance feel natural. ([source](Episode 259 — Practicing Innovation with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Build regular, non-evaluated creative practice into team rhythms — sessions where the goal is volume and exploration, not presentation-ready output. The absence of this practice mode is why most people feel perpetually unprepared to be creative on demand.

**The fear of judgment — specifically, the fear that an idea will be seen as stupid — is the single most reliable suppressor of creative contribution in group settings. Utley argues that most people self-censor their most interesting ideas before they leave their mouths, which means brainstorming sessions are systematically producing the safest rather than the most valuable ideas.** ([source](The Reason You Don't Have Better Ideas — video))

**Implication:** Facilitators and leaders must make the cost of withholding ideas higher than the cost of sharing bad ones. This requires explicit norm-setting, structural protections for divergent thinking, and leaders who visibly share half-formed ideas without apology.

**Utley observes that most people have been taught to evaluate ideas at the same moment they generate them — and that this simultaneous evaluation is what kills creative confidence at the root. The internal critic and the internal explorer cannot coexist in the same cognitive moment. Developing creative confidence requires learning to delay the critic, not eliminate it.** ([source](The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Teach individuals to separate their inner generative voice from their inner evaluative voice as a personal practice, not just a meeting facilitation technique. Journaling, rapid ideation exercises, and quantity-first prompts all train this internal mode-switching.

**Utley frequently addresses the experience of staring at a blank page and feeling creatively blocked, reframing it not as an absence of ideas but as a diet problem — insufficient and insufficiently diverse inputs. The blank page terror that people interpret as personal inadequacy is actually a diagnostic signal about the upstream inputs feeding the creative process.** ([source](The Reason You Don't Have Better Ideas — video))

**Implication:** When someone reports creative block, the first intervention should be an input audit, not a pep talk. Ask: what have you been reading, watching, and experiencing that is genuinely outside your normal domain? Block is usually a supply chain problem, not a personal deficiency.

**Most people never realize they are creative because they never give themselves enough attempts for a creative success to emerge. Creativity at low volume is invisible — you cannot see the signal through the noise of a handful of attempts. The people who consider themselves uncreative have usually just generated too few ideas over too short a time to encounter one that surprised them.** ([source](Episode 248: Jeremy Utley — unSILOed Podcast))

**Implication:** Reframe the minimum viable creative experiment for skeptics: commit to generating 50 ideas before deciding whether you have any. The inability to see your own creative capacity at low volume is the norm, not the exception — and the fix is throughput, not introspection.

**Utley positions the willingness to share a half-formed, imperfect idea as a form of creative courage that compounds over time. Each time someone shares an unpolished idea and survives the experience — does not get laughed out of the room — they accumulate evidence that creative risk-taking is survivable. This accumulated evidence is the actual substrate of creative confidence.** ([source](Episode 259 — Practicing Innovation with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Create low-stakes sharing rituals — half-baked idea rounds, no-evaluation idea dumps, two-minute speculation sessions — that give people repeated opportunities to share before they are ready. The goal is not the ideas; it is the evidence each person collects that sharing imperfect ideas is safe.

**Utley draws attention to the gap between people who consume knowledge about creativity and those who practice it.** Reading about brainstorming, watching talks about innovation, and discussing creativity frameworks are all forms of informed passivity. The shift to creative confidence only happens through doing — and most people are perpetually over-informed and under-practiced. ([source](Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Audit the ratio of creative consumption to creative production in your own week. If you are consuming more content about creativity than you are generating ideas, the gap is not informational — it is behavioral. The next move is always to make something, not to learn something new.

**Utley notes that genius-level creative output across history is consistently characterized not by unique talent but by a unique relationship with quantity — a willingness to produce at volume without waiting for inspiration to arrive. The creative confidence of the prolific is not confidence in the quality of any given idea but confidence in the process of generating enough ideas that quality will eventually emerge.** ([source](Jeremy Utley and Perry Klebahn | Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters | Talks at Google — video))

**Implication:** Redefine what creative confidence actually means in your organization: it is not the confidence to share only great ideas — it is the confidence to generate relentlessly and trust the process of emergence. Build that definition explicitly into how you talk about and reward creative contribution.

**Utley describes the experience of working through a high-volume ideation exercise as inherently confidence-building because it proves to participants, in real time, that they have more ideas than they believed. The exercise is not a test of creative talent — it is a demonstration that the constraint was never capacity but permission. Giving yourself permission to generate without judgment produces immediate evidence that rewires self-assessment.** ([source](Zero to One: The Myth That Keeps Individuals, Teams, and Organizations from Breakthrough Innovation — video))

**Implication:** Open every creative session with a high-volume, low-stakes warm-up exercise — 50 uses for a paper clip, 30 ways to name this product — not because those ideas are valuable but because the act of producing them is. The first job of a creative facilitator is to give people evidence about themselves.

**Utley has used the image of scraping knees while learning to ride a bike as a physical metaphor for the necessary discomfort of early creative practice. The d.school deliberately builds environments where productive failure is normalized and expected, because the alternative — protecting people from early failure — produces adults who never attempt anything they might fail at. Creative confidence is built on the bones of small, well-supported failures.** ([source](Episode 259 – Practicing Innovation with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Organizations adopting design thinking must actively protect early-stage failure from being stigmatized. If the first time a team tries a new creative method produces polished, safe work, the environment is too safe to generate genuine learning.

**The belief that creativity is a fixed trait — something you either have or don't — is itself the primary creative block.** Utley argues that this identity-level conviction, not lack of talent, is what prevents most people from contributing creatively. The problem is not absence of ability but the presence of a disabling story about ability. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Before any creative intervention, leaders and facilitators need to address the identity layer: the belief that 'I'm not a creative person' will undermine every technique and framework layered on top of it. Changing the story comes before changing the skill.

**Creativity is not a talent — it is a trainable, measurable, and improvable skill.** Utley positions this not as motivational reframing but as a factual claim: the behaviors that produce creative output can be identified, practiced, and tracked over time. The same logic he applies to brainstorming and innovation he now extends to working with AI. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Organizations should treat creative development the same way they treat sales training or technical upskilling — with structured practice, feedback loops, and measurable progression. Leaving creativity to 'the creatives' is a talent strategy, not an innovation strategy.

**Prolific creators like Picasso and Edison are not celebrated despite their volume — their masterworks are statistically downstream of their enormous output. Utley uses this pattern to reframe the anxiety around producing imperfect work: if the greats needed volume to find quality, the assumption that you should produce only polished ideas on the first attempt is itself the problem.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Use historical examples of prolific creators not as inspiration porn but as statistical arguments: more output increases the probability of breakthrough. This grounds the quantity-first approach in evidence rather than optimism, making it easier for skeptical professionals to adopt.

**The instruction 'think of something new' is functionally useless because the brain cannot execute it.** Utley's reframe is that creativity is not revelation — it is connection. Replacing 'be creative' with 'what does this make you think of?' gives the brain an executable task and immediately produces more output, changing the relationship between the person and their own creative capacity. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Replace open-ended creative prompts with connection prompts in every ideation context. 'What does this remind you of?' 'What is the opposite of this?' 'Where else have you seen this pattern?' These micro-reframes make creativity feel accessible rather than mystical.

**Utley has consistently positioned creativity not as a trait that some people have and others lack, but as a skill that can be developed through the kind of deliberate practice that d.school institutionalizes. This reframe is not motivational rhetoric — it has structural implications for how organizations hire, train, and evaluate people. If creativity is a skill, then a low creative output is a training and environment problem, not a talent problem.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Stop using 'not a creative person' as an explanation for underperformance in innovation. Instead ask: what practice environment have we built, and what deliberate reps have we given people to develop this skill?

**Most people's judgment of their own creativity was installed by an educational system that rewarded conformity and penalized wrong answers. Utley contends that the creative suppression people experience as adults is not natural — it was taught. Unlearning that judgment is the actual developmental work, not acquiring a new skill from scratch.** ([source](Stanford Webinar — Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Creative confidence programs should spend as much time on deprogramming evaluative self-judgment as on teaching generative techniques. The cage is internal, not external, and most training ignores it entirely.

**Creative confidence is not the absence of doubt — it is the willingness to act in the presence of doubt.** Utley does not promise that practice eliminates the feeling of creative vulnerability; he argues that sustained practice changes your relationship to that feeling from paralyzing to manageable. The goal is not certainty but functional tolerance for uncertainty. ([source](Stanford Webinar — Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Stop framing creative confidence programs around eliminating discomfort. Frame them around building a higher tolerance for productive uncertainty — because the discomfort of not-knowing is the permanent condition of genuinely creative work.

**The design thinking tradition Utley teaches at Stanford explicitly frames the beginner mindset — approaching a problem as if for the first time, without the constraints of expert knowledge — as a creative advantage rather than a deficit. This is a structural reframe of inexperience: what looks like a weakness in conventional professional culture is a creative asset in innovation contexts.** ([source](Stanford Webinar — Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Bring genuine beginners — not just cross-functional experts — into creative problem-solving sessions. Their lack of domain fluency is a feature, not a bug. The expert's familiarity produces incremental thinking; the beginner's ignorance produces reframing.

**Design thinking instills what Utley calls creative confidence — the belief that you are capable of generating ideas that can improve the world around you. This confidence is not the starting condition for engaging with design thinking; it is the output of repeated practice with the method. The d.school's pedagogical wager is that if you put people through enough well-facilitated creative cycles, they stop identifying as 'not creative' and start identifying as capable of change-making.** ([source](Stanford Instructor - Meet Jeremy Utley — video))

**Implication:** Design thinking training should be measured by changes in self-reported creative confidence, not just process adoption. If participants finish a training still saying 'I'm not a creative person,' the training has not achieved its primary goal.

**Beginners often attribute rich interview results to luck rather than skill, assuming they 'found a good one' rather than recognizing their own effective behaviors. This attribution error prevents them from learning and repeating what actually worked. Unpacking the specific, repeatable behaviors that led to great insights is far more valuable than chalking it up to chance.** ([source](How To Learn From Anyone))

> *"When students come back with great material, it's not useful (to them, or to the class) to assume they got lucky; but it's incredibly valuable to unpack what they did (specific, repeatable behaviors they engaged) to discover such interesting material."*

**Implication:** Leaders and coaches should resist letting teams dismiss their wins as luck. Debriefing what specific behaviors generated great outcomes builds repeatable craft and sustainable confidence.

**Accomplished beginners often exhibit an 'unhelpful humility' — they produce great work but attribute it to external luck rather than their own capability. This pattern is especially common in early design work when students return from fieldwork with unexpectedly rich material. The humility, while seemingly virtuous, actually blocks learning and skill development.** ([source](How To Learn From Anyone))

> *"I've noticed an unhelpful humility often accompanies good design work, especially when accomplished beginners. Countless times, when students come back with particularly rich material from the field, they seem even more surprised than the rest of the class: 'I guess we just found a good one...'"*

**Implication:** Educators, coaches, and team leads should actively counter false modesty in high performers. Helping people own their process — not just their outcomes — accelerates skill development and creative confidence.

**Creativity is not a rare gift reserved for a select few — it is an inherent human quality that everyone possesses.** Like any skill or muscle, it can be developed, strengthened, and refined through deliberate practice. The difference between creative and uncreative people is not innate talent but whether they exercise their creative faculties. ([source](Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley))

> *"Creativity is an inherent human quality, which everyone shares. But some people practice using their creative faculties, while others do not. It's just like any other skill or muscle. It can be developed, strengthened, and refined through practice."*

**Implication:** Leaders and teams should stop gatekeeping creative roles to 'creative types' and instead treat creativity as a trainable organizational capability that compounds with intentional practice.

**While tools and interfaces evolve, the fundamental human creative impulse has not changed.** What is genuinely new is the level of uncertainty students and professionals face about the future — including uncertainty about what jobs will even exist. This requires a shift from career planning to 'creative planning under uncertainty.' ([source](Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Leaders developing talent strategies should move away from role-specific skill planning toward cultivating adaptive creative capacity — the ability to generate novel solutions and reframe problems in rapidly shifting contexts.

**Jeremy positions creativity and innovation as capacities that can be built, nurtured, grown, and attended to regularly — not fixed traits you either have or don't. This is a direct challenge to the common belief that creative people are born, not made.** ([source](Episode 259 – Practicing Innovation with Jeremy Utley))

> *"unless you start to treat innovation or creativity as a capacity that can be built and nurtured and grown and attended to regularly, you're never going to win the medal and you're often going to pull a muscle."*

**Implication:** Leaders should stop hiring for 'creative people' as if creativity is a static trait and instead invest in systems and routines that grow the creative capacity of their entire team over time.

**Jeremy frames innovation not just as a professional skill but as a means of redemption — coming up with creative solutions that redeem broken parts of the world. This gives innovation a moral and spiritual dimension beyond business performance.** ([source](Episode 259 – Practicing Innovation with Jeremy Utley))

> *"come up with creative solutions that redeem broken parts of the world"*

**Implication:** For faith-driven entrepreneurs especially, innovation is not merely a competitive advantage but a calling — a way of participating in the repair of systems, communities, and structures that are not working as they should.

**Jeremy draws a sharp contrast between having a mindset that innovation requires ongoing practice versus those who treat it as something that happens spontaneously or only at designated events. The mindset difference — practice versus event — determines who will consistently produce innovative outcomes.** ([source](Episode 259 – Practicing Innovation with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** The most important prerequisite for becoming more innovative is a mindset shift: from 'I'll be creative when inspiration strikes or when we hold a brainstorm' to 'I am actively training my creative capacity every day.'

**Fear of replacement is self-fulfilling in a concrete, mechanistic way.** The person who believes they will be displaced stops experimenting and learning — and then eventually does fall behind, not because AI replaced them, but because they opted out of learning.
