Neither creative fluency nor AI fluency is a fixed trait — both are the product of accumulated reps and intentional reflection.** ([source](Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley — interview))

**Implication:** Design AI practice programs the way you would design any skill-development program: with deliberate repetition, reflection cycles, and increasing challenge. Treat AI fluency as a learnable craft, not a personality trait some people have and others don't.

**AI excels precisely where human cognition is weakest.** generating alternatives beyond the first idea. Unlike humans, for whom ideation consumes energy, AI can produce alternatives effortlessly using analogy, lateral thinking, and combinatorial association. This makes AI a powerful antidote to first-idea bias. ([source](Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley))

> *"If creativity is doing more than the first thing you think of, then it can push you to explore further. You can ask it, 'Give me 10 other ideas,' and it will keep going. Unlike humans, for whom ideation consumes energy, AI can generate alternatives effortlessly and quickly using analogy, lateral thinking, or combinatorial association. That's creative by definition."*

**Implication:** Teams should use AI not just to execute tasks faster but specifically to break out of cognitive closure — prompting it explicitly for divergent alternatives whenever they feel satisfied with an early solution.

**AI can help people reach 'good enough' ideas quickly, but that is about efficiency, not creativity.** The creative potential of AI is largely unrealized because humans let cognitive biases — especially the tendency to stop at 'good enough' — prevent them from pushing further. Unlocking true creativity requires the human to consciously demand more from the model. ([source](Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** The bottleneck in AI-augmented creative work is human behavior, not AI capability. Building explicit prompting habits that push past the first satisfactory output is the critical skill to develop.

**Treating AI as a teammate rather than a tool is a mindset shift with practical consequences.** The way you engage with a teammate — with curiosity, respect, and full presence — produces better outputs than treating it as a passive instrument. Utley argues that how you approach the model reflects your own humanity, and that quality of presence influences quality of output. ([source](Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Organizations should deliberately cultivate a 'teammate' mental model for AI adoption — not because AI has feelings, but because the collaborative mindset changes user behavior in ways that produce measurably better outcomes.

**AI can serve as a creativity coach by being assigned a specific expert persona and asked to evaluate ideas and generate alternatives. This is functionally similar to hiring a negotiation coach or design strategist — the key is giving the model a defined role and asking the right questions to unlock its capabilities.** ([source](Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley))

> *"The key is realizing that, if you give an AI model a specific persona – a creativity expert, say – and then ask it to evaluate an idea, it can provide meaningful feedback that pushes your thinking, as well as generate alternative suggestions. That's not fundamentally different from what we might expect from, say, a negotiation coach or a design strategist. It's a tool with vast capabilities – if you ask the right questions."*

**Implication:** Assigning AI a specific expert persona before asking for feedback is a simple but powerful prompting technique. Builders and leaders should experiment with role-based prompts to get more rigorous, actionable creative feedback.

**Jeremy is actively working on AI projects, indicating he is exploring how artificial intelligence intersects with creativity and idea generation. This positions him at the frontier of applying AI as a tool to enhance or scale human creative output.** ([source](The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** AI is not just a productivity tool — forward-thinking creativity researchers like Utley are already investigating how it can serve as a partner in the idea generation process. Leaders should be paying attention to this emerging interface.

**Most people treat AI like a vending machine — opening a blank chat window and asking a stranger to improvise — rather than onboarding it like a real teammate. The result is generic output, but the fault isn't the AI's capability; it's the absence of context. The fix isn't better prompts; it's treating the AI as a colleague who needs to be properly onboarded.** ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI))

> *"They want a chief of staff, but they keep opening a blank chat window and asking a stranger to improvise. (And then, somehow, the stranger gets blamed for sounding like a stranger.)"*

**Implication:** Leaders and creators should stop optimizing prompts and start investing in onboarding their AI with real context — who they are, how they sound, what they hate, and what good work looks like.

**A tool needs instructions, but a teammate needs context.** This distinction reframes the entire relationship with AI — moving from a transactional exchange of commands to a working collaboration built on shared understanding. The best AI users Jeremy knows aren't writing more elaborate prompts; they're building reusable context around their work. ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI))

> *"A tool needs instructions. A teammate needs context. That's the shift."*

**Implication:** Professionals should invest time once in building a reusable 'working brief' for their AI — covering identity, voice, priorities, and rules — rather than re-explaining themselves in every new session.

**Jeremy used AI to solve a task he'd been avoiding — building an FAQ — by having it read 50 call transcripts, identify recurring questions, and draft answers in his own voice. The AI didn't invent anything; it found the through-line in content he'd already created. This produced the sensation of genuine leverage, not just utility.** ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI))

> *"It read 50 transcripts, found the recurring questions, pulled my actual answers, and wrote the FAQ. In about three minutes... That was one of the first moments where I didn't just think, 'AI is useful.' I thought, 'Oh… This is what it feels like to have leverage.'"*

**Implication:** Before asking AI to generate new content, creators and professionals should first point it at their existing material — transcripts, emails, notes, past drafts — because the answers often already exist and just need to be surfaced.

**The most underrated category of AI use may be getting it to be 'appropriately boring' — handling the tasks that are easy enough that you feel like you should do them, but annoying enough that you never actually will. This isn't about AI creativity; it's about AI reliability for the mundane-but-important work that keeps piling up.** ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI))

> *"I did not need it to be creative. I needed it to be appropriately boring. (Which, now that I say it, might be an underrated category of AI use.)"*

**Implication:** High-value professionals should audit their task lists for items they've been avoiding because they're tedious — not hard — and systematically delegate those to AI rather than waiting to use AI only for glamorous or complex work.

**Once the first AI teammate is properly onboarded with core identity, voice, rules, and context, every subsequent AI teammate becomes dramatically cheaper to spin up — inheriting that foundation and only requiring role-specific additions. This creates compounding leverage rather than a series of one-off interactions.** ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI))

> *"A research teammate can inherit your identity, voice, rules, and current context. You only add the research-specific job... By the fifth one, you are not starting over anymore. That is the deeper leverage."*

**Implication:** The ROI of AI onboarding is not linear — it compounds. Investing in a reusable 'Teammate Stack' means each new AI role costs less to configure and delivers faster results, making the upfront work increasingly worthwhile over time.

**The language we use to describe our relationship with AI reveals our underlying mindset.** Asking whether you 'use' AI — as opposed to 'working with' it — parallels the distinction between using a CMO versus working with one. The framing shapes the entire quality of the collaboration. ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI))

**Implication:** Organizations and individuals should audit their language around AI — shifting from 'using AI' to 'working with AI' — because the linguistic shift signals and reinforces a fundamentally different set of behaviors, expectations, and collaboration norms.

**Jeremy and his research partner Kian Gohar conducted systematic research into how teams use generative AI for problem solving, motivated by a gap between anecdotal experience and demonstrable evidence. They wanted to move beyond hunches to make empirically grounded claims about AI's impact on team-based ideation. This represents a deliberate shift from practitioner intuition to academic rigor.** ([source](New Research: Get Better Ideas with AI))

> *"The question was, what can we demonstrate apart from kind of anecdotal observation and what kinds of claims can we make about the impact of generative AI on the way teams work when they're trying to solve problems."*

**Implication:** Practitioners and organizations adopting AI for innovation work should demand evidence-based frameworks rather than relying solely on personal anecdote or vendor claims. The field is young enough that early empirical research carries significant weight.

**Jeremy's research uncovered a specific cognitive bias that can seriously hamper AI-assisted teams from reaching their full creative potential. Crucially, this bias is described as both predictable and preventable — meaning awareness alone is a powerful antidote. The research frames this as a cautionary tale with a practical remedy.** ([source](New Research: Get Better Ideas with AI))

> *"A specific cognitive bias can seriously hamper AI assisted teams from reaching their full potential. Luckily, this bias is as preventable as it is predictable for folks who are aware of the phenomenon."*

**Implication:** Organizations deploying AI in creative or problem-solving workflows must invest in cognitive bias training alongside technical onboarding. Knowing the bias exists is already a meaningful protective factor.

**Jeremy's research with Kian Gohar was triggered by questions emerging from their professional networks when ChatGPT launched: Is ideation dead? What are the implications for creativity and innovation? These weren't abstract academic questions — they were urgent practical concerns from organizations trying to figure out how to adapt. The research was explicitly designed to answer real-world organizational questions.** ([source](New Research: Get Better Ideas with AI))

**Implication:** The most relevant AI research is being driven by pressing practitioner questions, not just academic curiosity. Organizations should cultivate relationships with researcher-practitioners who are actively embedded in real business contexts.

**Utley's current research focus is the impact of generative AI on problem solving and innovation inside large organizations. He sees AI not merely as a productivity tool but as something that fundamentally reshapes the innovation process itself.** ([source](Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Leaders should not evaluate AI adoption purely through a productivity or cost-reduction lens — its more transformative impact may be on how organizations generate, evaluate, and scale ideas.

**Utley advocates collaborating with multiple generative AI chatbots simultaneously as a way to augment human ideation.** Rather than using a single AI tool, working across several lets him develop and hone new concepts more robustly. ([source](Jeremy Utley Media Hub))

> *"Collaborating with multiple GenAI chatbots lets the Stanford professor develop and hone new concepts."*

**Implication:** Treating AI as a diverse creative team — not a single assistant — multiplies the ideation surface area. Builders should experiment with using multiple AI models in parallel for creative and strategic work.

**Utley's Harvard Business Review piece argues that Gen AI should be treated as a partner in a structured conversation, not a transactional bot. The research — captured in the 'More Chat, Less Bot' study — shows this relational approach maximizes creative effectiveness.** ([source](Jeremy Utley Media Hub))

**Implication:** The quality of your AI output depends on the quality of your conversational engagement. Teams that treat AI as a dialogue partner rather than a query engine will get richer, more useful results.

**Utley used AI role-play as a tool for preparing for difficult conversations — practicing with a chatbot before engaging in challenging discussions with family, colleagues, or friends. This surfaces AI as a low-stakes rehearsal environment for high-stakes human interactions.** ([source](Jeremy Utley Media Hub))

> *"Role-playing with an AI conversationalist can prepare you to handle difficult subjects with family, friends and colleagues"*

**Implication:** AI can serve as a safe practice space for emotional and interpersonal skill-building, not just technical or analytical tasks. Professionals can use AI to rehearse hard conversations and stress-test their communication strategies before the real moment.

**AI's decisive creative advantage is not intelligence alone — it's the elimination of experimentation bottlenecks.** Unlike humans constrained by budgets, calendars, and physical prototypes, AI can run millions of experiments in parallel with minimal resource constraints, testing not just the best ideas but virtually all ideas. ([source](Exponential Ideaflow: Why AI Will Surpass Human Creativity))

> *"unlike humans — whose rate of experimentation is often limited by budgets, calendars, or even atoms — AI doesn't face the same experimentation bottlenecks... AI can run thousands or millions of experiments in parallel, with minimal resource constraints compared to human research teams."*

**Implication:** The competitive moat is shifting from 'who has the best ideas' to 'who can test the most ideas fastest.' Organizations that integrate AI into their experimentation pipelines will compound their innovation rate exponentially over those that don't.

**AI's creative superiority over humans stems from four compounding advantages.** access to vastly more information, faster and more voluminous combinations, rapid experimental testing of those combinations, and learning from experiments at unprecedented scale. Each advantage multiplies the others. ([source](Exponential Ideaflow: Why AI Will Surpass Human Creativity))

> *"It can: 1. Access and process vastly more information 2. Make more combinations more quickly 3. Test those combinations through rapid experimentation 4. Learn from those experiments at unprecedented scale"*

**Implication:** Human creators need to identify which stages of the creative process they add irreplaceable value to, and delegate the rest to AI. Competing with AI on volume or speed of idea generation is a losing strategy.

**As AI takes over idea generation and testing, the distinctly human contribution shifts to determining which ideas matter. AI can generate and test millions of ideas, but it cannot feel the significance of solving a particular problem or care about a particular outcome. Meaning-making and prioritization remain human domains.** ([source](Exponential Ideaflow: Why AI Will Surpass Human Creativity))

> *"AI can generate and test millions of ideas. But it can't (yet) determine which ideas matter. It can't feel the significance of solving a particular problem or creating a particular experience. That remains our domain."*

**Implication:** The most valuable skill in an AI-saturated world is judgment — knowing which problems are worth solving and why. Leaders and creators should invest in developing and articulating their values and priorities, not just their technical AI skills.

**Utley sees the AI-2027 report's insight about AI researching AI as a creativity-relevant inflection point.** The self-reinforcing loop — AI improving AI research, which produces better AI, which does better research — mirrors the exponential dynamic of a pond-filling organism: the pond is half full on day 99 of a 100-day doubling cycle. ([source](Exponential Ideaflow: Why AI Will Surpass Human Creativity))

> *"AI labs are increasingly focusing their AI systems on AI research itself. This creates a self-reinforcing loop. AI systems researching AI will lead to better AI systems, which can then conduct even better AI research, faster."*

**Implication:** Leaders must internalize exponential thinking when planning for AI's creative impact. The danger is not the AI capabilities we see today but the near-future capabilities that will emerge from self-reinforcing improvement loops.

**Utley treats GenAI not as an oracle dispensing correct answers, but as a collaborator and sparring partner.** This reframe changes the entire dynamic of human-AI interaction — the posture becomes conversational and generative rather than transactional. When you approach AI this way, the answer is always yes, and no idea is too raw to explore. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas))

> *"When you treat GenAI as a collaborator and sparring partner, not an oracle, the answer is always yes."*

**Implication:** Leaders and creators should stop querying AI for 'the answer' and start using it as a thinking partner. The shift from oracle-mode to collaborator-mode unlocks far more creative value from the same tools.

**Utley runs two chatbots simultaneously — ChatGPT and Claude — feeding each one's output to the other for critique and iteration. This multi-bot architecture creates a built-in adversarial review loop, where neither AI's response goes unchallenged. The constant back-and-forth produces sharper, more refined output than any single AI session could.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas))

> *"Read the entire conversation and critique ChatGPT's approach. Be brutal, honest and don't hold back."*

**Implication:** Using multiple AI tools in dialogue with each other is a high-leverage creative strategy. Teams and individuals can manufacture the equivalent of a peer review process at zero marginal cost by routing outputs between competing AI systems.

**A core tactic in Utley's workflow is asking one AI to evaluate the other's performance — not just its output.** By prompting Claude to critique ChatGPT's 'approach to the assignment brief,' Utley surfaces meta-level weaknesses: structural issues, missing frameworks, voice misalignment. This creates a quality layer above the content layer itself. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas))

> *"Would you please read the conversation and critique ChatGPT's approach to the 'assignment brief.' Be brutal, honest and don't hold back."*

**Implication:** AI critique should operate at multiple levels — not just 'is this good content?' but 'is this the right approach?' Building a habit of meta-critique (evaluating how AI reasoned, not just what it said) produces significantly better final outputs.

**When Utley gets competing metaphor suggestions from two different AIs, he doesn't pick a winner — he asks each AI to evaluate the other's suggestions and explain which feel more aligned with his voice. This triangulation process surfaces not just the best option but the reasoning behind fit, helping Utley make a more informed creative decision.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas))

> *"I asked ChatGPT the same question and here's what it said (below) - what do you think?"*

**Implication:** When AI tools disagree or diverge in their suggestions, that divergence is itself informative. Creators should present competing AI outputs to each other and ask for comparative analysis — this extracts the latent wisdom in the disagreement rather than defaulting to the first answer.

**Utley recommends that AI be asked to 'think out loud' — to explicitly explain its reasoning when generating answers.** This transparency requirement transforms the AI from a black box producing outputs into a thinking partner whose logic can be interrogated, challenged, or built upon. It makes the reasoning itself part of the creative raw material. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas))

> *"Tell the chatbot to think out loud: Ask it to clearly explain its reasoning when giving answers."*

**Implication:** Asking AI to show its work is a high-yield prompting habit. When reasoning is visible, users can spot errors, redirect logic, and build more sophisticated follow-up prompts — turning a one-shot answer into an ongoing intellectual dialogue.

---

## Self-Literacy & AI Onboarding

**A powerful onboarding prompt — asking AI to interview you about your workflows, responsibilities, and objectives before making recommendations — unlocks highly personalized, actionable AI guidance that generic prompts cannot produce.** ([source](youtube:How to Master AI Powered Creativity in Just 13 Minutes | Jeremy Utley))

> *"Hey, you're an AI expert. I would love your help and a consultation with you to help me figure out where I can best leverage AI in my life. As an AI expert, would you please ask me questions one question at a time, until you have enough context about my workflows and responsibilities and KPIs and objectives that you could make two obvious recommendations and two non-obvious recommendations."*

**Implication:** Reversing the typical prompt dynamic — letting AI ask the questions — produces far more tailored and illuminating results than typing a direct request.

**Most people approach AI from the posture of question-asker to answer-giver.** Flipping this dynamic — asking AI what questions it needs answered, or what ten questions you should be asking — produces qualitatively superior outcomes. ([source](youtube:How to Master AI Powered Creativity in Just 13 Minutes | Jeremy Utley))

> *"The fundamental orientation a lot of people take towards AI is I'm the question asker, AI is the answer giver. But if you think about AI like a teammate, you say, hey, what are ten questions I should ask about this? Or what do you need to know from me in order to get the best response?"*

**Implication:** Letting AI drive the discovery process through its own questions surfaces context and nuance that users don't know they're withholding, dramatically improving the quality of collaboration.

**The self-literacy required to brief an AI — articulating your voice, context, intent, and standards — is itself the most important skill AI demands from its users and the one most professionals have not developed.** ([source](youtube:How to Master AI Powered Creativity in Just 13 Minutes | Jeremy Utley))

> *"I would give anything to have an assistant who understood my context and my voice and my intent well enough that I could sit in the bath and they could write my speech."*

**Implication:** The real work of AI onboarding is not learning the tool — it is developing the self-knowledge and articulacy to brief any collaborator, human or AI, with enough richness to produce excellent work.

**The reverse-prompting technique — asking AI to interview you rather than answering your questions — leverages the fact that AI response quality is a direct function of the context provided. Having AI ask questions one at a time draws out richer, more relevant context than any human's ability to self-brief upfront.** ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

**Implication:** Practitioners who struggle with 'what to put in the prompt' should invert the interaction entirely — letting AI lead the questioning session produces better context, better responses, and a more natural collaborative dynamic.

**A trained AI chief-of-staff or teammate brief — an AI loaded with personal context, voice, and working knowledge — dramatically compresses the time from zero to high-quality output in real-time high-stakes situations. Demonstrating this live, in front of skeptics, is more persuasive than any amount of explanation.** ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

> *"I've got a chief of staff that's trained on a bunch of my knowledge work. And I said, 'Hey, Claude, I'm pulling together a press announcement for Nikkei Business Press here in Tokyo, and they want a one-minute line. Would you ask me a few questions, knowing what you know about my newsletter and my podcast?' He gives me 20 hooks. I showed Heroto's son which ones you like?"*

**Implication:** Building and maintaining a persistent AI teammate brief is the infrastructure investment that makes every high-pressure creative moment faster and better — leaders who demonstrate this live change the culture around AI more effectively than any training program.

**The advice 'use AI to use AI' is the most universally applicable starting point for AI adoption.** When confused about how to start or what to prompt, the answer is to ask AI itself — to be interviewed by it, to have it compose your custom instructions, or to have it surface use cases for your specific role. ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"Use AI to use AI. So, you wonder how could I use it? Don't wonder by yourself. Open up ChatGPT and say, 'Hey, here's who I am. Here's what I'm trying to do. How could I leverage generative AI to do what I'm doing?'"*

**Implication:** Onboarding friction for new AI users collapses when they understand that the tool itself is the best guide to using the tool — removing the need for extensive training before first value is realized.

**The quality of AI outputs is a direct function of the quality and richness of inputs the user provides, including both the content of the prompt and the level of genuine care the user brings to the interaction. Lukewarm engagement produces mediocre AI responses, leading people to incorrectly conclude AI is ineffective.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet): How to Learn AI in 12 Minutes))

> *"The outputs of your collaboration with AI are a function of the inputs you bring to it. And part of your input is your prompt, but then there's also your care."*

**Implication:** People who dismiss AI as 'dumb' are often diagnosing a care and context problem in themselves, not a capability problem in the AI.

**Reverse prompting — asking AI to interview you with questions rather than crafting the perfect prompt yourself — is a high-leverage tactic for extracting rich context. The AI's questions pull out information you wouldn't have thought to volunteer, producing more relevant and personalized responses.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet): How to Learn AI in 12 Minutes))

> *"Instead of trying to craft the perfect prompt yourself, tell the AI, I want you to interview me about my role, about my challenges, about my goals. Ask me questions one at a time and wait for my answers in between until you have enough information to deliver a quality response."*

**Implication:** The burden of context provision doesn't have to fall entirely on the user — delegating the extraction process to AI through structured interviews is a more reliable path to rich context than solo prompt crafting.

**Embedding candor preferences into AI custom instructions — rather than requesting pushback ad hoc — creates a persistent collaboration posture that transforms every interaction. Telling AI you value challenge over comfort recalibrates the entire relationship rather than a single exchange.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet): How to Learn AI in 12 Minutes))

> *"I set up my custom instructions so that AI knows I value candor. I don't want to get gassed up. I want meaningful collaboration with a strategic thought partner who I expect to challenge my thinking and to push where my thinking is weak. Just add this to your custom instructions: I'm trying to stay sharp and critical. I want this interaction to improve my analytical ability."*

**Implication:** Custom instructions are the highest-leverage configuration setting most users ignore — a single candor instruction permanently upgrades every future conversation.

**Effective conversation simulation with AI requires briefing the AI on your conversation partner's specific characteristics before role-play begins — interviewing AI about the other person so it can portray them realistically rather than playing a generic difficult-person archetype.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet): How to Learn AI in 12 Minutes))

> *"Ask the AI to interview you about your conversation partner until it feels confident it can play their role in the conversation. Then have the AI have the conversation with you out loud using advanced voice mode."*

**Implication:** The quality of conversation simulation is proportional to the specificity of the role briefing — generic role-plays produce generic preparation, while detailed character briefings produce realistic practice.

**Assigning AI the role of questioner — rather than answer-generator — produces both more insightful questions and more satisfying final output. Inverting the typical dynamic unlocks a depth of thinking that the standard prompt-response loop cannot reach.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"What you'll find is when you give AI the job of asking you questions, one, it asks very insightful questions. And two, it will give you far more satisfying output."*

**Implication:** A simple tactical reframe — asking AI to interview you before drafting anything — can dramatically improve the quality of AI-assisted work without requiring any new tools or technical knowledge.

**Letting the AI ask you clarifying questions is a concrete collaboration technique that shifts the dynamic from one-way prompting to genuine dialogue. When the model asks questions, it surfaces gaps in context and forces the user to sharpen their own thinking, producing better-defined inputs and therefore better outputs.** ([source](youtube:The 10-Second Test That Shows If You're Behind on AI))

> *"Let the model ask you questions."*

**Implication:** A single practice shift — explicitly inviting the AI to ask clarifying questions before responding — can significantly improve output quality and simultaneously develop the user's self-literacy about their own problem.

**Giving AI real context — rather than sparse, search-engine-style prompts — is a foundational technique that separates effective from ineffective collaboration. Context richness directly determines output quality, and most users habitually under-brief their AI collaborator the same way they would under-brief a new hire.** ([source](youtube:The 10-Second Test That Shows If You're Behind on AI))

> *"Start giving the model real context."*

**Implication:** The single fastest horizontal improvement most professionals can make is to invest more time in briefing AI thoroughly before expecting useful output — treating it like onboarding a new colleague rather than querying a database.

**When AI produces unsatisfying output, users should take responsibility rather than blame the tool.** Insufficient or low-quality input is almost always the true cause of poor output — the user's ability to provide rich context is the actual constraint. ([source](youtube:Stanford-Jeremy-Utley-AI-brainstorming))

> *"If you are getting an undesirable or dissatisfactory output take responsibility for it and say it's because I provided insufficient input."*

**Implication:** Framing poor AI outputs as an input problem rather than a tool problem shifts the locus of improvement to where it actually lives — the quality and richness of the human's contribution.

**Custom instructions — directions that tell an LLM how to interact with you — can produce wildly different and better outputs, but most users don't know where to start. The solution is to ask the LLM itself to interview you and draft your custom instructions.** ([source](youtube:Stanford-Jeremy-Utley-AI-brainstorming))

> *"You can go to the llm and say I'd love to craft custom instructions... I have no idea how to begin would you interview me as a New York Times Reporter whose job is to craft custom instructions for professional users ask me up to 10 questions about myself."*

**Implication:** Using AI to build the instructions that shape future AI interactions is a meta-skill that compounds — the upfront investment in a good brief pays dividends in every subsequent session.

**The Stanford MBA intern mental model is a powerful reframe for AI adoption.** If you can clearly articulate which workflow a highly capable intern could take off your plate, you can deploy AI in that same role. The bottleneck is not AI capability — it is the user's ability to atomize and articulate their own workflows. ([source](youtube:AI_in_Learning_Ep1_JeremyUtley))

**Implication:** Professionals who invest time in mapping their own workflows gain an immediate and disproportionate advantage in AI deployment.

**The highest-leverage AI setup is a trained 'chief of staff' — an AI system built on your writing, podcast, book, and voice — that knows your context deeply enough to ask clarifying questions and generate context-aware outputs without you having to re-explain yourself every session.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"I pulled out my phone and with a chief of staff that's been trained on my blog post, my podcast, and my book, and my voice, and I said, 'Hey, I am in Tokyo right now with the Nikkei Business Press. They would like a 1-minute video they want to send out to their subscribers, and they're asking me for some killer material.'"*

**Implication:** Building a persistent, context-rich AI collaborator trained on your own body of work is the highest-leverage AI investment most professionals have not yet made — it converts every session from cold start to warm handoff.

**Getting better AI output requires the user to articulate explicit design parameters — specific qualities they liked and why — rather than expecting the AI to infer preferences from prior output alone. The AI cannot read your mind; it can only work with what you explicitly provide.** ([source](youtube:Jeremy_Utley_AI_Optimal_Results))

> *"I like the pun of behind the screens, the simplicity of mastering the art of innovation, the art part. I like the alliteration of Pixar Paradox... I've got to give it design parameters. It cannot read my mind."*

**Implication:** The discipline of articulating what you like and why is not optional polish — it is the core skill that separates users who get great AI output from those who get mediocre output.

**The most common complaint about AI — that outputs 'don't sound like me' — is almost always a self-literacy failure rather than an AI failure. Most people have never explicitly described their own voice, style, or standards to the AI, then blame the AI for not capturing them.** ([source](youtube:Jeremy_Utley_AI_Optimal_Results))

**Implication:** Before blaming AI for generic output, professionals should invest in developing and communicating a clear description of their voice, style, and standards — the bottleneck is self-knowledge, not AI capability.

**Most people don't realize they can explicitly tell an AI what their communication style sounds like.** This is a fundamental and underused capability that unlocks dramatically more personalized and useful output. ([source](youtube:Jeremy_Utley_AI_Optimal_Results))

> *"I didn't know I could tell it what I sound like. Yeah, of course you can."*

**Implication:** A simple shift in awareness — knowing that you can and should describe your own voice to the AI — immediately expands what users can get from the tool.

**Time invested in properly briefing and directing an AI is not overhead — it is the direct mechanism through which output quality improves. The willingness to invest that time is the single differentiating factor between users who get great AI output and those who don't.** ([source](youtube:Jeremy_Utley_AI_Optimal_Results))

> *"To get the best possible output out of a language model requires work on your part. You got to give it feedback. How would it know otherwise?... If we're willing to invest the time, we get way better output."*

**Implication:** The ROI calculus on AI should include the upfront investment in context-setting and feedback as an essential input, not a tax — users who treat this investment as worthwhile will consistently outperform those who don't.

**The real bottleneck in most AI deployments is not AI literacy — it is self-literacy.** Most professionals have never been forced to articulate their own context, voice, standards, and non-negotiables clearly enough to brief another person. AI is the first collaborator that actually demands this self-knowledge before it can be useful. ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Before investing in prompt engineering courses or AI tool subscriptions, professionals should audit what they actually know about themselves — how they communicate, what they refuse to compromise on, what they keep re-explaining — because that self-knowledge is the raw material AI needs to produce genuinely personalized output.

**Utley draws a sharp distinction between prompting AI and onboarding AI.** A tool needs instructions; a teammate needs context. Most people open a blank chat window and ask a stranger to improvise on their behalf, then blame the AI for sounding generic — when the real failure is that the AI was never given enough to work with in the first place. ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Shifting from a prompting mindset to an onboarding mindset — giving AI persistent, structured context about who you are, how you work, and what you reject — transforms sporadic outputs into a repeatable and improving collaborative relationship.

**Most people start every AI session from zero, re-explaining who they are, what they do, and what they need.** This is the equivalent of hiring a talented chief of staff and making them forget everything they learned at the end of each workday. The leverage point is building a reusable context document — an AI teammate brief — that travels into every session automatically. ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** High-leverage AI users should invest time once in building a comprehensive, reusable brief that captures their professional identity, communication style, and recurring context. This upfront investment compounds across every future session.

**Asking 'what prompt should I use?' is the wrong question — it keeps people stuck at the beginner level indefinitely.** The right question is 'what would this AI need to know if it were joining my team as a new colleague today?' Reframing from prompt-seeking to colleague-onboarding is the cognitive shift that separates occasional users from genuine power users. ([source](Jeremy Utley on The Best Way To Work With AI — video))

**Implication:** Leaders and trainers should replace prompt libraries with onboarding frameworks. Teaching people to think like a hiring manager briefing a new teammate produces more transferable and durable AI skills than collecting clever one-off prompts.

**The discomfort most people feel when trying to build an AI teammate brief is not a UX problem — it is a self-knowledge problem. Writing down your voice, your standards, your non-negotiables, and your recurring context forces a level of self-articulation that most professionals have never been required to produce. That discomfort is not a bug; it is the actual work.** ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Organizations rolling out AI tools should treat the process of building personal context documents as a professional development exercise in its own right — not as a technical onboarding step. The self-clarification work has value independent of the AI outputs it eventually enables.

**AI is the first collaborator in history that surfaces your inability to articulate yourself as a distinct, measurable problem. Every previous colleague filled in the gaps through shared context, institutional memory, or social grace. AI has none of that — and so every gap in your self-description becomes a gap in the output. The medium makes the problem visible in a way nothing else has.** ([source](AI as Your New Strategic Thought Partner with Jeremy Utley, Stanford, Ideaflow, & Beyond the Prompt — video))

**Implication:** Rather than viewing AI's generic outputs as a failure of the technology, professionals should read generic outputs as diagnostic feedback — a signal that their self-description is incomplete. Poor AI outputs are mirrors, not malfunctions.

**Utley used AI to synthesize an FAQ from dozens of pre-event call transcripts in a matter of minutes, extracting consistent answers he had given repeatedly but never organized. The key insight is not that AI is fast — it is that AI can surface what you already know but have never made explicit. The answers were already there, trapped in the pattern of his own words.** ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Before asking AI to generate new thinking, professionals should feed it their existing output — past emails, transcripts, documents, presentations — and ask it to synthesize the patterns. The self-knowledge most people need is already embedded in what they have already produced.

**Utley identifies a compounding rep gap as the primary driver of the growing divide between AI power users and everyone else. The gap is not between people who know advanced features and those who don't — it is between people who use AI daily and people who use it occasionally. Daily use builds self-literacy and AI fluency simultaneously, which is why consistency beats sophistication every time.** ([source](How to Get Ahead of 99% of People (with AI) — video))

**Implication:** The highest-ROI AI habit for most professionals is simply committing to daily use — any use — rather than waiting to learn the 'right' prompts or the 'best' tools. The self-literacy developed through daily practice is not available through any other route.

**Utley argues that what most people call 'AI fatigue' is more accurately described as the cumulative discomfort of not having clarified their own professional identity clearly enough to benefit from AI. The exhaustion is not from too much AI exposure — it is from the ambient pressure of knowing you should be able to articulate yourself and finding that you cannot.** ([source](The AI Fatigue Myth & What Leaders Can Actually Do About It — video))

**Implication:** Leaders managing AI adoption resistance should diagnose whether the resistance is really about technology overload or about the unexpressed anxiety of self-articulation. Addressing the self-literacy gap directly — through structured exercises — resolves the resistance more reliably than more AI demos or feature training.

**Building an AI teammate brief requires answering questions most professionals have never been asked.** How do you actually sound when you are at your best? What are the things you would never say? What context does every collaborator need to work with you effectively? These are not trivial questions, and the effort required to answer them is exactly proportional to how much leverage the answers create. ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** A structured self-interview — covering voice, values, non-negotiables, recurring context, and communication preferences — is one of the highest-leverage investments a knowledge worker can make in 2024. The output is not just an AI brief; it is a portable professional self-portrait.

**Utley observes that the most skilled AI users in most organizations are effectively hidden — research suggests that colleagues perceive heavy AI users as less competent and less hardworking. This creates a perverse dynamic where the people with the most self-literacy and the most refined AI workflows are the least likely to share what they know, leaving the organization's learning curve unnecessarily steep.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on How to Build an AI Culture to Lead for the Future — video))

**Implication:** Organizations trying to build AI culture need to de-stigmatize visible AI use before skills transfer can happen at scale. Norms — not tools — are the actual bottleneck, and changing norms requires leaders to model AI use publicly, including the self-literacy work that underlies it.

**Utley frames the AI teammate brief as a living document rather than a one-time setup exercise.** Just as a real colleague learns more about you over time, your AI context document should evolve as your role, voice, and priorities change. The professionals who treat it as a one-time task and forget it are leaving compounding returns on the table. ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Schedule a quarterly review of your AI teammate brief the same way you might review your professional development goals. What has changed about your role, your communication style, or your priorities? Updating the document keeps the AI relationship calibrated and avoids the drift that causes outputs to feel stale or off-brand.

**Utley points out that AI is aggressively egalitarian in one specific way.** it provides every user with exactly as much leverage as that user provides context. A junior employee with a comprehensive AI brief will consistently outperform a senior executive who starts every session cold. Self-literacy — not seniority, not salary, not access — is the democratizing variable. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Investing Time to Get Optimal Results from AI — video))

**Implication:** Organizations should recognize that AI redistributes leverage toward self-aware individuals regardless of hierarchy. This creates both an opportunity — anyone who invests in self-literacy gains disproportionate advantage — and a leadership risk, if senior leaders assume their institutional knowledge is automatically legible to AI.

**Utley regularly observes that the most common mistake professionals make with AI is treating each session as standalone rather than cumulative. Real colleague relationships build context over time — each interaction draws on everything before it. The professionals who replicate this cumulative quality in AI interactions through reusable briefs and persistent context are operating in a fundamentally different relationship with the technology than those who start fresh every time.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Where to Start with AI — video))

**Implication:** Power users should build context persistence into their AI workflow as a non-negotiable habit — using custom instructions, system prompts, or dedicated project spaces — so that every session starts from the accumulated baseline rather than from zero.

**The skill-progression arc Utley describes for AI adoption — from fear to familiar to fluent to fun — applies directly to the self-literacy work as well. The first attempt to write an AI teammate brief feels awkward and incomplete because it is; that is the expected first stage of the progression, not evidence that the exercise is failing or that the practitioner lacks the capacity to do it.** ([source](You're Not Behind (yet): How to Learn AI in 12 Minutes — video))

**Implication:** Facilitators running AI onboarding should normalize the discomfort of the first self-literacy attempt explicitly, framing it as curriculum rather than failure. A rough first brief that gets refined over time will outperform no brief every time — the goal is to start, not to start perfectly.

**Utley argues that voice is one of the hardest and most important things to make legible in an AI brief.** Most people have a recognizable voice that colleagues can identify instantly — but they have never described it in words. The exercise of translating intuitive voice into explicit principles is difficult precisely because it requires moving from tacit to explicit knowledge, which is the hardest direction of travel in any domain. ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** To capture voice for an AI brief, practitioners should start by feeding existing work — emails, presentations, past writing — to AI and asking it to describe the patterns it observes. This externalizes the voice description task, making it easier to recognize and refine rather than generate from scratch.

**Utley positions the work of building an AI context document as the highest-leverage AI skill most professionals have not developed — higher leverage than knowing advanced features, higher leverage than learning specific tool interfaces, higher leverage than prompt libraries. Context beats commands because context scales across every interaction, while commands are single-use.** ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** AI training budgets and personal learning time should be weighted heavily toward context-building and self-articulation work, not feature mastery. The skills that compound most reliably are the ones that make every future interaction better, not the ones that improve any single output.

**Utley notes that the people who produce the most impressive AI outputs are rarely the most technically sophisticated — they are the most self-aware. The quality difference between a remarkable AI output and a generic one traces back almost entirely to the richness of the human context provided, not to the cleverness of the instruction syntax.** ([source](The 5 Step Playbook for 10x Your AI Productivity | Jeremy Utley — video))

**Implication:** Organizations benchmarking AI outputs across teams should look for the self-literacy differentiator, not the prompt-engineering differentiator. High-quality outputs signal a high-quality context brief, which means the scalable intervention is helping everyone articulate their context more clearly.

**There is a measurable 10-second test Utley describes for assessing whether someone is genuinely integrated with AI or merely adjacent to it: ask them to show you something they built with AI that could not have come from a generic user. If they cannot produce it in ten seconds, they are not yet in a daily-use compounding relationship with the technology — regardless of how many tools they have access to.** ([source](The 10-Second Test That Shows If You're Behind on AI — video))

**Implication:** Leaders assessing AI adoption should move past self-reported usage surveys and toward demonstrated artifact reviews. Asking team members to surface real examples of AI-augmented work quickly reveals where genuine integration has occurred versus where adoption is nominal.

**Utley's 'show, don't tell' live demonstration principle applies with particular force to the self-literacy question.** When executives see their own strategic questions answered with genuine nuance and specificity — using a pre-built context brief — the imagination gap closes in a way that no explanation of the concept can produce. The experience of seeing your own voice reflected back accurately is uniquely convincing. ([source](How to Master AI Powered Creativity in Just 13 Minutes | Jeremy Utley — video))

**Implication:** AI champions within organizations should build and maintain their own high-quality AI briefs specifically so they can demonstrate the quality gap live — running the same question through a generic AI session and then a context-rich session to make the difference visceral and immediate.

**Utley frequently connects self-literacy to the broader principle that most people have not invested in understanding how they think — not just what they think about. The meta-cognitive dimension of professional practice — knowing your own creative process, your own decision-making patterns, your own communication defaults — is what distinguishes practitioners who can brief a colleague from those who cannot, whether that colleague is human or AI.** ([source](AI in Learning Series | Ep 1 | Jeremy Utley | How to spark learning innovation with AI — video))

**Implication:** Professional development programs should include structured meta-cognitive work — exercises that help practitioners observe and describe their own thinking patterns, not just build domain expertise. This foundation makes AI onboarding dramatically faster and more effective.

**Utley argues that the single most important question in AI onboarding is not 'what can this AI do?' but 'what do I need this AI to know about me?' The first question is product-oriented and leads to feature exploration; the second is relationship-oriented and leads to self-articulation. The framing of the entry question determines the quality of the entire practice that follows.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Where to Start with AI — video))

**Implication:** AI onboarding sessions, whether for individuals or teams, should be structured around the question 'what does your new AI colleague need to know about you?' rather than feature tours or prompt libraries. Starting with the self-knowledge question reorients the entire learning experience toward durable capability.

**Utley connects the difficulty of AI onboarding directly to the broader principle that creativity requires self-knowledge. Knowing how you think — what inputs energize you, what modes you default to, what you need to produce your best work — is the same intelligence that makes you a good AI collaborator. The two developmental paths are not separate; they reinforce each other.** ([source](Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley — interview))

**Implication:** Investments in personal creativity development — journaling, reflective practice, understanding one's own creative rhythms — have a direct return in AI collaboration quality. Practitioners who take both seriously develop a compounding advantage over those who treat them as unrelated domains.

**AI functions like a mirror.** it reflects and amplifies what the user brings to it. An HR expert who approaches a model with expert-level thinking will get expert-level output; a novice asking the same question will get far less. The model's power lies in matching and extending the user's existing knowledge, not replacing their thinking. ([source](Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley))

> *"The AI model reflects what you bring to it. If you're an HR expert, and you approach the model with expert-level thinking, you'll get an expert response... AI reveals things to you it won't reveal to someone else. That's why I say the model's power is in its ability to match and extend the user's thinking. It doesn't replace your creativity, it amplifies it."*

**Implication:** Investing in your own domain expertise and self-awareness remains essential in the AI era — the quality of AI output is a direct function of the quality of knowledge and context you bring to the conversation.

**A practical onboarding exercise for AI adoption is to ask the model to play the role of a 'ChatGPT adviser' and have it interview you about your workflows, responsibilities, and KPIs before making recommendations. This prompt is role-independent — it works for interns, managers, and executives alike — and creates a 'double delivery': it solves your immediate need while opening the door to deeper AI fluency.** ([source](Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** This prompt is a low-barrier, high-value entry point for anyone struggling to integrate AI into daily work. Leaders can share it org-wide as a concrete first step toward AI fluency without requiring training programs.

**Utley offers a specific high-value prompt for getting real value from AI.** describe your role, list your top three weekly activities, and ask the AI to interview you with 3-5 questions before offering suggestions. This approach grounds AI interaction in actual work context rather than generic demos or tutorials. ([source](Not Behind Enough to Panic. Too Afraid to Learn.))

> *"Open ChatGPT or Claude right now and paste this: 'I'm a [your role] at a [your type of company]. Here are the three things I spend most of my time on each week: [list them]. Before giving me any suggestions, interview me — ask me 3-5 questions about my work so you understand what would actually help.'"*

**Implication:** The most direct path to personal AI value is a contextual, role-specific conversation rather than general tutorials. Getting AI to ask questions before answering produces dramatically more relevant output.

**The real bottleneck to effective AI use isn't AI literacy — it's self-literacy.** Most people underuse AI because they haven't done the work of making their own context legible: who they are, how they sound, what they refuse, and what they keep explaining repeatedly. AI is simply the first colleague that actually demands this kind of self-knowledge. ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI))

**Implication:** Before blaming AI tools for poor output, individuals and teams should ask whether they've ever clearly articulated their own voice, values, standards, and working rules — because AI cannot infer what hasn't been examined.

**The first AI teammate is the hardest — not because the technology is difficult, but because setting it up forces you to notice yourself. You have to name what matters, write down your rules, listen for your own voice, and decide what good work looks like before you can ask AI to produce it. This self-examination is the real cost and the real payoff.** ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI))

> *"The first AI teammate is the hard one. Not because the technology is impossible. Because the first one forces you to notice yourself."*

**Implication:** Leaders should expect the initial investment in AI onboarding to feel uncomfortable precisely because it demands self-examination — and should treat that discomfort as a feature, not a bug, since the resulting clarity compounds across every future AI collaboration.

**Jeremy frames the key reorienting question not as 'What prompt should I use?' but as 'What would this AI need to know if it were joining my team?' This question transforms prompting from performance into preparation, and turns the chat window from a vending machine into a working relationship.** ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI))

> *"What would this AI need to know if it were joining my team? That question changes everything. It turns prompting into onboarding. It turns one-off answers into reusable leverage."*

**Implication:** Anyone serious about AI productivity should replace their default prompt-crafting ritual with a teammate-onboarding mindset — asking what a smart new hire would need to know on day one, then building that brief once and reusing it.

**Jeremy recommends a specific onboarding practice.** before any important AI session, have the AI interview you — one question at a time — to build a working brief capturing who you are, how you sound, what you hate, and what the AI should never do. The interview format draws out things you'd say out loud but never type. ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI))

> *"I want to onboard you as a teammate before I ask you to help me. Please ask me one question at a time, waiting for my answers in between. Help me create a short working brief that captures who I am, what I'm working on, how I sound, what I hate, and what you should never do."*

**Implication:** Practitioners can immediately improve AI output quality by running a one-time AI interview session to generate a reusable working brief — replacing the habit of diving straight into task prompts with a brief context-building conversation first.

**Jeremy emphasizes that telling AI what you hate — what sounds fake, what risks matter, when to challenge you — is the step most people skip when onboarding. Defining what the AI should never do is as important as defining what it should produce, because it's the guard against generic, inauthentic output.** ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI))

> *"This is the part people skip. Tell it what you hate. Tell it what sounds fake. Tell it what risks matter. Tell it when to challenge you, when to ask, and when to stop polishing and just make the thing useful."*

**Implication:** AI onboarding should include explicit negative constraints — a 'never do this' list — because AI defaults to the generic center of the distribution, and only explicit guardrails pull output toward an individual's authentic voice and standards.

**Utley's first move when starting a GenAI session is to explicitly define the role, expertise, and behavioral mandate of the chatbot. Rather than asking a generic question, he instructs the AI to act as a specific type of expert with specific tasks — editor, copywriter coach, harsh critic — before the real work begins. This role-framing shapes the quality and character of every subsequent response.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas))

> *"I want you to act as an expert newsletter copy editor with the knowledge of my background, particular interests and area of expertise."*

**Implication:** Prompt quality is upstream of output quality. The single highest-leverage prompting habit is defining who the AI is before asking it anything. Teams should build libraries of role-framing prompts tailored to their specific creative and strategic needs.

---

## AI Adoption & Overcoming Resistance

**Non-technical professionals, when given foundational AI training, can build tools that produce enormous organizational leverage — far beyond what their titles or technical backgrounds would suggest is possible.** ([source](youtube:How to Master AI Powered Creativity in Just 13 Minutes | Jeremy Utley))

> *"Adam said, if I have to replace the carpet tiles in the lodge, I have to fill out all this paperwork... And in 45 minutes, he built a tool with natural language that saves him two days of work... The National Park Service is estimating that the tool that Adam built in 45 minutes is going to save the service 7000 days of human labor this year."*

**Implication:** The ROI of basic AI training for non-technical employees is potentially massive and wildly underestimated by most organizations.

**There is a massive 'realization gap' in AI adoption.** while AI demonstrably improves speed, output volume, and quality, fewer than 10% of working professionals are actually deriving meaningful productivity gains from it. ([source](youtube:How to Master AI Powered Creativity in Just 13 Minutes | Jeremy Utley))

> *"While on the one hand, AI makes people 25% faster and 12% more work and 40% better quality, it's also true that less than 10% of working professionals are deriving meaningful productivity gains from collaboration with AI. To me, there's this enormous gap. I call it the realization gap."*

**Implication:** The problem is not AI capability — it is adoption quality. Organizations that close the realization gap will have an outsized competitive advantage.

**AI training programs should start with the individual's most dreaded or avoided work — the tasks that feel burdensome — because that is where the motivation to invest time in building an AI solution is highest and the productivity gain is most viscerally felt.** ([source](youtube:How to Master AI Powered Creativity in Just 13 Minutes | Jeremy Utley))

> *"One of the things I say is you should focus on parts of your work that you dread. Parts of your work that you don't enjoy. 'Ah, I have to do this again.'"*

**Implication:** Targeting AI adoption at pain points rather than aspirational use cases generates faster adoption, stronger motivation to learn, and more concrete ROI stories that spread adoption organically.

**What leaders call 'AI fatigue' is actually AI insecurity in disguise.** The people who feel fatigued are not exhausted by AI itself — they are exhausted by the existential pressure of not having started while everyone around them talks about it. True lead users of AI are not tired; they are exhilarated. ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

**Implication:** Leaders diagnosing 'AI fatigue' in their organizations are misreading the signal — the intervention needed is not less AI conversation but more hands-on experience to replace insecurity with competence.

**A simple live demonstration exposes the gap between claimed fatigue and actual AI experience.** When an audience of 400 all raised their hands to signal AI fatigue, but every hand dropped when asked to share their screen and show what they were doing with AI, the contradiction revealed itself instantly. Fatigue requires use; most people haven't actually used AI meaningfully. ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

**Implication:** The most powerful tool for overcoming claimed AI fatigue is a live, in-the-moment demonstration that makes the gap between narrative and reality undeniable — people cannot sustain the fatigue story once exposed.

**The real barrier to AI adoption is not lack of knowledge but fear of looking incompetent — specifically, the fear of learning something you feel you should already know. A Stanford professor's candid reply captured this precisely: the shame of being behind is more paralyzing than any capability gap.** ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

> *"Fear of learning something new that I should effing already know was not one of the choices. The reason most folks aren't taking action is because they're paralyzed. They're afraid."*

**Implication:** AI adoption programs that lead with information and training will underperform because they address the wrong barrier — the real work is reducing shame and normalizing being a beginner, not transferring knowledge.

**Not using available AI tools in professional knowledge work is professional malpractice — an abdication of responsibility to do the best possible work with every available means. Just as a cardiologist who refuses a stethoscope in favor of 'handcrafted artisanal cardiology' would face immediate referral, professionals who refuse AI must eventually face the same standard.** ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

> *"What would you do if you went to a cardiologist and she says, 'Okay, I'm going to put my ear on your chest so I can listen to your heart'? You would say, do you have a stethoscope? And what if she said no, handmade artisanal cardiology only here. You would ask for a referral. That is malpractice. Professional knowledge workers have to see not inviting AI in is malpractice."*

**Implication:** The paradigm shift needed in organizations is not permission to use AI but a professional standard that treats non-use as negligence — leaders who adopt this framing change the incentive structure around AI adoption fundamentally.

**The most important question a person can ask when exposed to an AI use case is 'Could it...?' — the open-ended wondering about whether AI could help with their own situation. What most people lack is not information about AI but a sparked imagination that generates this question.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"That is actually the most important question somebody could ask. Could it dot dot dot? The problem is for most people, they don't have a question."*

**Implication:** Creating conditions for imagination-sparking — podcasts, live demos, peer stories — is more valuable for AI adoption than instruction or information, because the bottleneck is question formation, not knowledge.

**Showing AI in action on someone's actual real-time problem — rather than describing it abstractly — is the most effective way to convert skeptics and spark adoption. Live demonstration with real stakes creates the witnessed experience that changes behavior.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"Rather than answer your question right now, which is what everybody thinks the expert is supposed to do, I'm gonna share my screen and I'm gonna pull up ChatGPT and I'm going to ask it to be my thought partner because your question right now is the most important question I've gotten today. Why on earth would I provide an artisanal handcrafted response when I have augmented intelligence available to me?"*

**Implication:** AI advocates inside organizations should prioritize live, screen-shared demonstrations on real problems over presentations and decks — the visceral 'I can see it working on my actual problem' moment is what converts.

**The greatest gains from AI often come not to technically sophisticated users but to people who don't consider themselves tech-savvy. Non-technical workers frequently have the most to gain because their manual, language-heavy processes have the highest leverage for compression.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"I think the some of the biggest gains actually come from folks who don't consider themselves techsavvy... this is a guy who, by the way, I go to tag him on LinkedIn — homeboy doesn't even have a LinkedIn account. Who's creating a tool that, if you scale it across the park service, you do a little bit of back of the envelope math, it's like 10,000 days of labor that could be saved this year alone."*

**Implication:** AI rollout strategies should prioritize frontline, non-technical workers with high-repetition paperwork or language-processing tasks — not just knowledge workers or technical staff — to capture the largest efficiency gains.

**The deeply ingrained human habit of relying on one's own intellect and instincts causes even experienced AI users to spend significant time on problems manually before thinking to use AI. Overcoming this default is a deep behavioral change, not a simple habit swap.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

**Implication:** AI adoption programs should account for the cognitive default toward self-reliance and design deliberate triggers — checklists, team norms, environmental cues — that prompt people to reach for AI earlier in their workflows.

**AI advantage accrues to consistent daily experimenters, not to the most informed consumers of AI content.** Reading articles, attending webinars, and watching videos about AI are no substitute for 10-15 minutes of daily hands-on practice. ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet): How to Learn AI in 12 Minutes))

> *"The advantage doesn't go to the people who read the most articles about AI or attend the most webinars or watch the most YouTube videos, including this one. The advantage goes to people who experiment consistently, daily."*

**Implication:** Consuming AI content can create an illusion of preparation while actually widening the gap — the only closing move is practice, not more information.

**Genuine play and personal exploration — not structured professional use cases — is often how people discover their most valuable AI applications. You will discover use cases unique to your life that no expert or article would generate for you, but only through actual experimentation.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet): How to Learn AI in 12 Minutes))

**Implication:** Personal AI use case discovery is irreducibly individual — no framework or expert list substitutes for personal experimentation, and the most valuable applications are often ones no one else would have predicted.

**The gap between current users and the top 1% of AI practitioners is not about intelligence, technical skill, or access — it is about action. This reframe makes AI mastery accessible to virtually anyone willing to take consistent small steps.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet): How to Learn AI in 12 Minutes))

> *"The gap between you and the top 1% of AI users isn't intelligence, it's not technical skill, it's not access, it's action."*

**Implication:** Framing AI fluency as an action gap rather than a talent or resource gap removes most structural excuses and places agency fully with the individual.

**Most people who talk about AI take no meaningful action with it — simply completing one AI drill puts you ahead of the vast majority of people who discuss AI without practicing. The action gap is far larger than the knowledge gap.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet): How to Learn AI in 12 Minutes))

> *"If you actually did that, and I really hope that you did, you just took more action with AI than 90% of people who talk about it. And that's the whole point of this video."*

**Implication:** In the current AI landscape, action is extraordinarily rare relative to discussion — even minimal practice immediately differentiates practitioners from the majority.

**The window of advantage for AI early movers is real but narrowing.** People are not yet behind, but continued inaction while others build daily practice habits will create compounding skill gaps that become increasingly difficult to close. ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet): How to Learn AI in 12 Minutes))

> *"You're not behind, not yet, but the window is narrowing."*

**Implication:** The urgency of starting AI practice is not about catching up today but about avoiding a compounding disadvantage that grows exponentially as daily practitioners accumulate months of experience.

**The blank-page intimidation that hobbles most AI interactions comes from importing the wrong mental model — treating AI like Google and feeling pressure to write a perfect, compressed search query. Voice input dissolves this intimidation by replacing the typed interface with natural speech.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet): How to Learn AI in 12 Minutes))

> *"The intimidation of the blank page comes from this feeling of oh, this is Google, I need the perfect staccato four-word search query. You don't. You just need to talk."*

**Implication:** Interface-induced anxiety is a significant hidden barrier to AI adoption — voice mode is not just a convenience but a psychological unblocker that lowers the activation energy for rich AI engagement.

**Frequency of AI use is the wrong metric for measuring AI progress.** What matters is not how often you open the tool, but how skillfully you use it and on what quality of work you apply it. Counting logins and daily usage creates a false sense of keeping up while masking real stagnation. ([source](youtube:The 10-Second Test That Shows If You're Behind on AI))

> *"Most people measure their AI progress by the wrong thing. They count how often they use it, whether they've opened it today. Frequency is not the important metric. Frequency will not tell you whether AI is actually changing your work output in any kind of meaningful way."*

**Implication:** Teams and individuals who optimize for frequency rather than impact will feel productive while generating no meaningful gains — the metric itself becomes a trap.

**A 2x2 matrix with axes of 'how well' (badly vs.** well) and 'where applied' (trivial vs. valuable work) reveals a professional's true AI situation more accurately than months of self-assessment. The four quadrants expose distinct failure modes and one target state, giving people an honest diagnostic in seconds. ([source](youtube:The 10-Second Test That Shows If You're Behind on AI))

> *"I like to use a simple 2 by 2 matrix with every team I work with, whether it's the students at Stanford or Fortune 500 companies. In about 10 seconds, it tells people more about their real AI situation than months of self-assessment. Not where they think they are, but where they actually are."*

**Implication:** A simple diagnostic framework cuts through self-deception about AI progress and immediately surfaces the specific upgrade needed — vertical, horizontal, or both.

**Less than 10% of working professionals are generating meaningful productivity gains from AI — some studies put this as low as 3%. The gap between this group and the rest is not the tools they use or the prompts they know; it is their orientation toward AI itself.** ([source](youtube:The 10-Second Test That Shows If You're Behind on AI))

> *"Less than 10% of working professionals are driving meaningful productivity gains from AI. And some studies show it's as low as 3%. When we look at the difference between people in that 3 to 10% and the rest, it wasn't that the tools they use or the prompts that they knew were different. It was their orientation."*

**Implication:** Prompt libraries and tool comparisons are the wrong investment — the actual leverage point is shifting how a person fundamentally relates to AI as a collaborator.

**The bottom-right trap is where well-intentioned professionals squander their AI potential.** they invest in improving collaboration quality without investing in the importance of the work they apply it to. Better technique on unimportant work still produces unimportant results. ([source](youtube:The 10-Second Test That Shows If You're Behind on AI))

> *"Most people are trying to make the horizontal move, but they aren't thinking about their vertical one. They're trying to improve the quality of the collaboration without improving the importance of the work they're working on. That's the bottom right trap, and it's where well-intentioned people will squander their true potential in this moment."*

**Implication:** AI fluency is necessary but not sufficient — professionals must simultaneously upgrade the strategic weight of the tasks they bring to AI or their investment in skill-building will yield negligible returns.

**Checking a box — logging in, staying current, being seen to use AI — is a frequency-driven behavior that conflates activity with progress. The 'am I using AI enough?' question keeps attention on superficial compliance rather than on whether AI is actually changing meaningful work output.** ([source](youtube:The 10-Second Test That Shows If You're Behind on AI))

> *"It keeps you focused on frequency and on checking a box, logging in, staying current."*

**Implication:** Organizations that measure AI adoption by login rates or usage frequency will systematically reward the wrong behaviors and miss the professionals generating real value with fewer but better interactions.

**Technology has programmed users to expect the first search result to be the best result — a conditioning that actively harms AI collaboration. Unlike Google, generative AI is non-deterministic, meaning the first output is not privileged over subsequent ones.** ([source](youtube:Stanford-Jeremy-Utley-AI-brainstorming))

**Implication:** Breaking the 'first result is best' mental model is a prerequisite for effective AI use — users need to be explicitly retrained to expect and seek variation across outputs.

**AI adoption resistance in organizations is usually not a genuine data security problem — it is fear of the unknown dressed up in the language of compliance.
