**Most organizations are unconsciously engineered to suppress ideas rather than generate them.** The structures that feel like innovation infrastructure — stage-gate processes, evaluation committees, pitch decks — are actually filtering mechanisms applied to a chronically underfilled pipeline. The real problem is never downstream selection; it is upstream starvation. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Before investing in better idea-evaluation systems, leaders should audit how many ideas are actually being generated. If the pipeline is thin, adding more sophisticated filters only guarantees mediocre outcomes faster.

**Ideaflow — the volume and velocity of ideas generated per unit of time — functions as a leading indicator of organizational creative health. Revenue, patents, and product launches are lagging indicators that tell you what already happened. Ideaflow tells you what an organization is likely to produce next. Organizations that want to predict their future innovation output should measure what their people are generating today, not what they launched last quarter.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Build ideaflow tracking into team operating rhythms. Count ideas generated per session or sprint, watch the trend, and treat a declining ideaflow number as an early warning signal — not of talent failure but of environmental suppression.

**Psychological safety is not a nice-to-have cultural amenity — it is a functional prerequisite for idea generation.** When people fear that their ideas will be judged, mocked, or used against them, they self-censor before speaking. The strangest ideas — which are statistically the most likely to be genuinely novel — are also the most socially vulnerable and therefore the first to disappear in unsafe environments. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Building psychological safety is not about making people comfortable; it is about preserving the strange ideas that conventional safety-seeking kills. Leaders should explicitly signal that weird, half-formed, and even terrible ideas are welcome and expected.

**Setting an explicitly uncomfortable idea quota — fifty ideas, a hundred ideas — changes the social contract in a brainstorming session in a way that no amount of encouragement can. When the goal is 'say something impressive,' social filtering dominates and only safe ideas survive. When the goal is 'help us hit the number,' people are liberated to contribute strange, half-formed, and unexpected ideas because the target is quantity, not quality.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Facilitators should set idea quotas that feel unreasonably high. The discomfort of the number is not a bug — it is the mechanism that overrides social self-censorship and allows unusual ideas to surface.

**Companies consistently misdiagnose their innovation problem as a selection problem when it is actually a generation problem. They build elaborate frameworks for picking winners from a thin idea pool, not realizing that a brilliant filter applied to a weak pipeline still produces mediocrity. The leverage is almost always upstream of the funnel, not in refining the criteria used to judge what enters it.** ([source](Jeremy Utley and Perry Klebahn | Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** Leaders should resist the temptation to add more rigor to the evaluation process and instead invest in dramatically expanding the number of ideas in circulation. The selection problem largely solves itself when the generative volume is high enough.

**Creativity is not a talent distributed unevenly at birth — it is a skill that can be trained, measured, and improved through deliberate practice. The organizational belief that some teams or functions are 'creative' and others are not is itself a form of institutional learned helplessness that suppresses the generative potential of the majority of the workforce.** ([source](Stanford Instructor - Meet Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Organizations that reserve creative work for designated 'creative' roles are leaving enormous generative capacity dormant. Treating creativity as trainable means every team can build ideaflow capacity with the right practice conditions and facilitation support.

**Design thinking as practiced at Stanford's d.school is not primarily a set of tools — it is a mindset shift that repositions the employee from executor to human-centered investigator. When organizations adopt the vocabulary without the mindset, they get sticky-notes theater: the forms of empathy and ideation without the underlying epistemological commitment to being genuinely uncertain about what the right answer is.** ([source](Stanford Webinar - Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** When introducing design thinking to an organization, invest disproportionately in the mindset layer — comfort with ambiguity, permission to be wrong, genuine curiosity about users — before introducing the process tools. The tools without the mindset produce performance, not transformation.

**The d.school's multi-disciplinary team model is itself a design choice premised on the insight that creative collisions between different domains produce ideas that no homogeneous team can generate. Utley has consistently pointed to cross-domain input as a driver of ideaflow, and the d.school's physical and pedagogical structure instantiates this principle by deliberately mixing engineers, business students, artists, and social scientists in the same project teams.** ([source](Jeremy Utley and Perry Klebahn | Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** When assembling innovation teams, optimize for cognitive diversity over functional expertise. The team that makes the most interesting mistakes is usually the most heterogeneous one, and interesting mistakes are the raw material of breakthroughs.

**Utley has described the d.school's approach to innovation as fundamentally optimistic about human capability — the institution is built on the belief that the skills required for meaningful creative contribution can be taught to anyone willing to practice them. This is a political stance as much as a pedagogical one: it rejects elitist models of innovation that concentrate creative authority in small, credentialed groups and redistributes creative responsibility to everyone who faces a human problem.** ([source](Special d.school mission — video))

**Implication:** Innovation programs should be designed for breadth, not just depth. The goal is not to produce a small team of expert design thinkers but to raise the baseline creative capability of every person in the organization.

**Utley envisions AI becoming a permanent equal partner in the ideation process — a trusted creative colleague who always surfaces new ideas, pushes thinking, and illuminates blind spots. Future creative teams will not just use AI as a utility but will integrate it as a full member of the ideation table.** ([source](Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley))

> *"I cannot imagine a world where AI is not an equal partner in the ideation process. It's going to sit at the table as a member of creative teams. Teams will come to see it as a creative partner, a trusted colleague who always has new ideas, who pushes your thinking, who helps you see blind spots."*

**Implication:** Organizations should begin designing team structures, meeting formats, and creative rituals that formally include AI as a participant — not an afterthought — to get ahead of this shift before it becomes standard practice.

**Jeremy critiques the over-association of innovation with events like hackathons, bootcamps, and sprints.** These events are not inherently bad, but if they are the extent of your innovation practice, you are in serious trouble. The LinkedIn badge for attending a workshop is not equivalent to actual creative capacity. ([source](Episode 259 – Practicing Innovation with Jeremy Utley))

> *"there's this over association of innovation with events. And you know, at the d school I'm all about leveraging its brands or a hackathon or a workshop. That's fine. But if those events are the extent of your practice, you're in trouble."*

**Implication:** Leaders should stop measuring innovation health by the number of workshops run or badges earned, and start measuring it by the ongoing cadence of idea generation and problem-solving activity.

**Connections between people and ideas are a critical engine of idea generation.** Jeremy highlights the importance of connections when generating more ideas, suggesting that creative output is not a solo endeavor but is amplified through networks and relationships. ([source](The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Building diverse, cross-functional relationships is not just good for culture — it is a direct investment in your idea-generating capacity. Isolated thinkers produce fewer and narrower ideas.

**Idea Flow is framed as a paradigm shift — a fundamentally different mental model for how creativity works at the enterprise level. Rather than treating idea generation as an episodic creative event, Utley repositions it as a continuous, manageable flow that can be engineered and scaled.** ([source](The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Organizations that treat creativity as an occasional brainstorm session are structurally underinvesting in innovation. Adopting an 'idea flow' operating model means making idea generation an ongoing, embedded practice.

**Dopamine is not merely a pleasure chemical — it is a neuroplasticity catalyst that allows learning to stick.** Without curiosity triggering dopamine, a person can be physically present in a training session and go through all the motions without the learning actually landing. ([source](Not Behind Enough to Panic. Too Afraid to Learn.))

> *"Dopamine is not just a feel-good chemical. Dopamine is a neuroplasticity catalyst. It's what allows the learning to stick. Without curiosity, you can be in the room, you can go through the motions, but the learning doesn't land."*

**Implication:** Attendance and compliance metrics are poor proxies for AI skill development. What matters is whether employees are in a neurological state of curiosity when they engage with new tools.

**A competent professional leader contacted Utley for a private one-on-one AI consultation because he was too self-conscious to ask questions in a group setting. The gap in AI knowledge felt like evidence of personal failing rather than a normal response to unprecedented change — illustrating how shame compounds avoidance.** ([source](Not Behind Enough to Panic. Too Afraid to Learn.))

> *"The feeling of being behind wasn't just uncomfortable for him. It felt like it said something about him. Like the gap was evidence of a personal failing, not a normal response to an unprecedented rate of change."*

**Implication:** Organizations must create psychologically safe AI learning environments — small groups, peer cohorts, or private channels — where people can ask beginner questions without status threat.

**Jeremy spent 15 years teaching at Stanford's d.school with a specialization in creativity and entrepreneurship.** His body of work bridges academic research and organizational practice — he advises organizations on building innovation capacity, not just teaching in a classroom. This practitioner-researcher hybrid role shapes how he approaches AI's impact on creative work. ([source](New Research: Get Better Ideas with AI))

> *"I've spent the last 15 years teaching at Stanford's D school, wrote what I thought would be the world's greatest book on brainstorming and idea generation."*

**Implication:** The most valuable AI-and-creativity insights will come from people who operate at the intersection of empirical research and real organizational practice — not purely from either academic or consulting silos.

**Jeremy challenges the myth that idea generation is a solo activity.** Collaborative ideation draws on a wider pool of perspectives, backgrounds, and experiences, producing ideas that no single individual could reach alone. Diverse input expands the solution space. ([source](Maximizing the Flow of Ideas for Your Organization))

> *"creating ideas with a team can create broader insights, by drawing on a wider pool of perspectives and backgrounds to create ideas that might be outside the experience of a single person."*

**Implication:** Building diverse, cross-functional ideation teams is not just a cultural good — it's a direct multiplier on the quality and range of ideas an organization can generate.

**Innovation has become simultaneously over-hyped and under-nourished as a genuine capability.** Despite enormous cultural attention, most individuals, teams, and organizations have not developed the actual practices and habits that make sustained creative output possible. ([source](Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley))

> *"Having taught the subject of innovation for the last 14+ years at Stanford, he has seen the topic become over-hyped, yet remain an under-nourished capability. This is true not only on the personal level, but also on the team and organizational levels."*

**Implication:** Organizations should audit the gap between their innovation rhetoric and their actual capability-building investments — hype without practice produces neither ideas nor results.

**Utley warns that Gen AI can actually limit a team's creativity if used incorrectly, despite its potential to enhance it.** The risk is real: defaulting to AI outputs without critical engagement can narrow rather than expand creative thinking. ([source](Jeremy Utley Media Hub))

**Implication:** Organizations must be intentional about how they integrate AI into creative workflows. Passive use of AI can converge thinking prematurely — leaders need guardrails that keep human judgment and divergent thinking in the loop.

**Many organizational leaders recognize creativity as vital for innovation but don't know how to actually cultivate it among their employees. Utley and Klebahn recommend concrete structural changes — giving employees dedicated time and space to ideate — as the practical lever.** ([source](Jeremy Utley Media Hub))

> *"Creativity is vital for innovation, but many org leaders don't know how to tap it among employees."*

**Implication:** Creativity doesn't emerge from exhortation — it requires environmental design. Leaders must build structural slack (time, psychological safety, and permission to explore) if they want to see creative output from their teams.

**Utley argues that in a world of exponential AI-driven ideaflow, human roles shift fundamentally — from generators and testers of ideas to judges of which ideas matter and why. This reframes human purpose in the creative process around meaning and values rather than production.** ([source](Exponential Ideaflow: Why AI Will Surpass Human Creativity))

> *"In a world of exponential idea flow, our role shifts from being the generators and testers of ideas to being the judges of which ideas matter and why."*

**Implication:** Organizations should restructure creative workflows so humans are deployed primarily as decision-makers and meaning-setters, with AI handling the upstream generation and testing. This is a structural change, not just a tooling change.

---

## Brainstorming & Facilitation

**The FIXIT framework provides a structured approach to AI collaboration.** Focus on a problem, Ideate individually before using AI, provide eXtended context, Interact iteratively, and undertake Team-based incubation and experimentation. This sequence short-circuits the cognitive biases that make naive AI use counterproductive. ([source](youtube:Stanford-Jeremy-Utley-AI-brainstorming))

> *"F set a focused problem I ideate as an individual before going to AI X provide the AI sufficient context I interact iteratively and then T team-based incubation and next steps for experimentation if a team will leverage this approach they're far more likely to shortcircuit some of the cognitive biases."*

**Implication:** Teams with a structured AI collaboration protocol consistently outperform teams that improvise their AI interactions — the FIXIT sequence is a minimum viable framework for capturing AI's potential while avoiding its pitfalls.

**Starting a brainstorm with a deliberate bad idea session — explicitly generating the worst possible ideas — creates psychological safety and dissolves the cognitive grooves that keep thinking rigid. Steve Jobs described thinking as a record with grooves; bad idea brainstorms shake thoughts loose from those grooves and increase the probability of unexpected creative connections.** ([source](youtube:AI_in_Learning_Ep1_JeremyUtley))

**Implication:** Facilitators should normalize — even ritualize — bad idea generation at the opening of any creative session to lower inhibition and increase the range of ideas that surface.

**Deliberately deferring the decision at the end of a brainstorm — making the explicit choice not to choose yet — exploits the Zeigarnik effect, which keeps unresolved problems active in working memory. Ideas continue developing between sessions when the mind remains open rather than closed by premature selection.** ([source](youtube:AI_in_Learning_Ep1_JeremyUtley))

> *"As a leader, one of the most important things you can do is say, 'Team, let's actually not choose yet. We're going to wait till the next meeting to make decisions.' What that does is it allows folks' working memory to remain active. It's called the Zeigarnik effect."*

**Implication:** Leaders who resist premature closure after brainstorms activate a free cognitive resource — the team's working memory — to continue generating and refining ideas between meetings.

**Astro Teller, CEO of Google X, always asks teams who bring one idea to bring five.** Teams learned not to show up with a single idea — and half the time, one of the four 'dummy ideas' they sandbagged with turned out to be better than their original favorite. Forced volume surfaces better ideas even from reluctant generators. ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"Whenever a team brings him one idea, he always says, 'Give me five.' Astro told me what happens is a team will bring their pet idea, and then they sandbag with four dummy ideas. He says, 'Half the time one of their dummy ideas is better than their pet favorite.'"*

**Implication:** Requiring volume — even when teams resist it — is a practical facilitation technique that reliably improves idea quality by expanding the search space beyond initial favorites.

**Ideas follow a normal distribution.** a small percentage are delightful, most are ordinary, and an equal small percentage are dopey. When you eliminate the variation that produces dopey ideas, you also eliminate the variation that produces delightful ideas — you cannot surgically remove one tail of the distribution. ([source](youtube:talks-at-google-ideaflow))

> *"When we get rid of the variation that produces dopey ideas, we also eliminate the variation that produces delightful ideas. And so the reality is, we're left with a choice. Do I want to get to delightful ideas? I've got to have the kind of variation and embrace the kind of collaboration that involves dopey ideas too."*

**Implication:** Organizations that filter out 'bad' ideas before generating sufficient volume are statistically eliminating their breakthrough ideas alongside the failures — psychological safety for bad ideas is a prerequisite for good ones.

**Karl Duncker's 1930s research showed that people could solve difficult problems via analogical inspiration if they were explicitly told to consider whether the analogy had bearing on their problem. Without that prompt, the analogy failed to transfer — awareness that a connection might exist is a necessary condition for making it.** ([source](youtube:talks-at-google-ideaflow))

> *"He fed them analogies that had bearing on the problem, and about 30% to 40% could solve it. But then when they were told, see if the analogy has bearing on the problem, all of a sudden, over 80% of people can solve it. What's the difference? They were told to consider the possibility of a connection."*

**Implication:** Telling people to actively look for connections — not just exposing them to diverse stimuli — is a critical facilitation and teaching move that dramatically increases creative transfer.

**The average corporate brainstorm generates only two ideas.** Design thinking is valuable precisely because it helps teams overcome the tendency to converge prematurely and instead generate a high volume of ideas. ([source](stanford-webinar-design-thinking-what-is-it))

> *"There's some research that indicates that the average corporate brainstorm generates two ideas, just two. And so what design thinking does is it helps teams overcome that tendency to settle very, very quickly."*

**Implication:** If the baseline is two ideas per brainstorm, any structured effort to increase idea volume represents a massive competitive advantage — the bar is far lower than most teams assume.

**When generating hundreds of ideas leads to default back to the familiar, the solution is not to generate fewer ideas but to shorten the time before prototyping begins — keeping wild ideas alive by making them tangible rather than letting them die in list form.** ([source](stanford-webinar-design-thinking-what-is-it))

> *"Hundreds of ideas, Jonathan, I would say shorten your brainstorming time and lead time right then and there to build some of the ideas, to make some really rough prototypes, to keep alive some of the wilder ideas. And say, we're going to suspend this sort of decision making, and we're just going to build out a couple of these and make them real."*

**Implication:** The gap between ideation and prototyping is where unconventional ideas die — compressing that gap by building rough prototypes during or immediately after the session prevents premature convergence on the familiar.

**Most people conflate the concept of an idea with the concept of a good idea, making quality inseparable from generation in their minds. This conflation is a root cause of underperformance in creative work. When someone asks 'how do I come up with ideas,' they almost always mean 'how do I come up with good ideas' — and that mental filter is precisely what slows them down.** ([source](talk:zero-to-one-myth))

> *"I help people come up with ideas. Right. But for most of us, the notion of quality is inseparable from the notion of an idea. And that's part of the problem. That's actually what stands in our way."*

**Implication:** Separating idea generation from idea evaluation — even conceptually — is a foundational shift that unlocks dramatically higher creative output.

**Ideas fall on a normal distribution.** some are delightful, most are ordinary, and some are dopey — in roughly equal proportions at the tails. You cannot surgically remove the dopey ideas without also eliminating the delightful ones. Accepting dopey ideas as a necessary condition of the process is what enables access to breakthrough ideas. ([source](talk:zero-to-one-myth))

> *"You can't get rid of only one side of the distribution. You can't do it. So your choice is, which distribution do you want? Do you want delightful ideas? You got to have dopey."*

**Implication:** Creative cultures must explicitly protect and even celebrate dopey ideas, because the willingness to be dopey is the price of admission for being delightful.

**Information transfer cannot produce imagination, and imagination must precede skill-building.** Utley argues that you cannot train someone to brainstorm effectively through explanation — they have to witness or experience a generative process working before they can reproduce it. This means the standard workshop structure (explain → practice) has the sequence backwards. ([source](The AI Fatigue Myth & What Leaders Can Actually Do About It — video; Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley — interview))

**Implication:** Design brainstorming training by leading with live demonstration rather than theory. Run a real ideation exercise with real stakes before introducing the framework that explains why it works. Skills attach to imagination that has already been sparked, not to models that have been intellectually understood.

**Utley treats AI as a brainstorming partner that dramatically expands ideaflow.** Because AI can generate dozens of candidate ideas in seconds without social risk, it removes the ego cost that makes human contributors censor themselves. Pairing AI generation with human curation — using AI to flood the funnel and people to select and refine — produces better outcomes than either working alone. ([source](New Research: Get Better Ideas with AI — podcast; Exponential Ideaflow: Why AI Will Surpass Human Creativity — essay))

**Implication:** Use AI to pre-populate brainstorm sessions with a large number of raw ideas before the human meeting begins. This lowers the barrier to participation, anchors the group at higher quantity, and gives humans something to react to and build on rather than staring at a blank whiteboard.

**Research Utley cites on AI-assisted brainstorming shows that individuals working with AI consistently outperform groups brainstorming without it in both the quantity and quality of ideas produced. This finding challenges the conventional assumption that group brainstorming is inherently superior to individual ideation and suggests the real bottleneck has always been the social dynamics of the room, not the raw cognitive capacity of the individuals.** ([source](New Research: Get Better Ideas with AI — podcast))

**Implication:** Before defaulting to a group brainstorming session, ask whether a structured individual-plus-AI generation phase followed by group synthesis would outperform the traditional meeting. The evidence suggests it often will — especially when the problem is genuinely novel and diverse perspectives are needed.

**Using AI as a brainstorming partner before a team meeting changes the starting conditions of the session.** Rather than beginning from zero with a blank whiteboard, participants can arrive having already generated a large pool of raw ideas with AI assistance. This shifts the group's role from generation under social pressure to curation and synthesis — work humans are naturally better at in group settings. ([source](New Research: Get Better Ideas with AI — podcast; Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas — interview))

**Implication:** Redesign brainstorming meetings as synthesis sessions, not generation sessions. Assign individual AI-assisted idea generation as pre-work, collect all output before the meeting, then use the group time for pattern recognition, connection-making, and prioritization — the tasks that benefit most from human judgment and conversation.

**Utley argues that building an AI brainstorming practice requires investing time upfront to get compounding returns later. Priming an AI with rich context about the problem, the constraints, the customer, and previous attempts is not overhead — it is the work. The outputs are only as generative as the inputs, and most people shortchange the input investment and then blame the outputs.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Investing Time to Get Optimal Results from AI — video; Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas — interview))

**Implication:** Treat the AI briefing document for a brainstorm the same way you would treat the briefing document for a talented human consultant: invest real time making the problem, constraints, and context legible before asking for ideas. That investment is what separates useful AI generation from generic noise.

**Productive procrastination — giving the subconscious time to work on a problem before forcing a solution — is a legitimate and underused creative tool. Utley points to the long history of creative breakthroughs that came in the shower, on a walk, or during seemingly unproductive time as evidence that the brain does its best generative work when not under direct evaluative pressure. The structured pause is a brainstorming method, not an avoidance strategy.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on The Power of Productive Procrastination — video; The Habit That Built Every Genius (It Looks Like Doing Nothing) — video))

**Implication:** Build incubation time into your team's creative process as a deliberate phase, not dead time. After an initial generative session, let the problem sit before reconvening. The second session, after subconscious processing, will reliably surface connections that the first session's intensity prevented.

**Premature judgment is the primary idea killer in group settings.** Generation and evaluation are cognitively incompatible activities — the brain cannot simultaneously produce and assess without the critical faculty overpowering the generative one. The most valuable ideas — the strange ones that eventually become breakthroughs — are disproportionately vulnerable to early evaluation because they are hardest to defend before they are fully formed. ([source](The Reason You Don't Have Better Ideas — video))

**Implication:** Train teams to recognize when they have slipped from generating mode into evaluating mode. This is not a values problem; it is a cognitive architecture problem. The facilitation intervention is naming the mode the group is in and explicitly calling it back.

**Most innovation failures are fundamentally framing failures.** Teams spend enormous energy solving the wrong problem beautifully because they move to solution generation before adequately sitting with the question. The highest-leverage skill in the innovation toolkit is crafting the right 'How Might We' prompt — or equivalent problem framing — before generating any solutions at all. ([source](Stanford Webinar - Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?; Zero to One: The Myth That Keeps Individuals, Teams, and Organizations from Breakthrough Innovation — video))

**Implication:** Build a mandatory problem-reframing phase into every brainstorming session. Before anyone generates a single solution, spend meaningful time asking: What is the real problem here? What would have to be true for this to be the wrong question? The quality of your answers is bounded by the quality of your questions.

**The skill progression for brainstorming and creative confidence follows a predictable arc.** fear → familiar → fluent → fun. When people report they are 'bad at brainstorming,' Utley reframes that as the required first stage of a known progression rather than evidence of a fixed deficit. The discomfort of early creative practice is not a signal to stop — it is the curriculum itself. ([source](Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley — podcast; Stanford Instructor - Meet Jeremy Utley — video))

**Implication:** When teams resist brainstorming exercises with 'we're not creative people,' respond by normalizing the discomfort as stage one of a progression everyone goes through. Low initial output is not the floor — it is the starting line. Sustained practice with the mechanics reliably moves people along the arc.

**Utley applies the design thinking principle of radical collaboration to brainstorming by insisting that diverse cognitive inputs — different disciplines, lived experiences, and knowledge domains — are not a nicety but a structural requirement for reaching novel ideas. Homogeneous groups recirculate shared assumptions and hit the same cognitive ceilings repeatedly. Diversity is a throughput mechanism, not a values statement.** ([source](Stanford Webinar - Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?; Special d.school mission — video))

**Implication:** When assembling a brainstorming team, optimize for cognitive diversity — people who don't share the same training, vocabulary, or default assumptions about the problem. The friction of different mental models, properly facilitated, is a feature, not a cost.

**Brainstorming at scale — within large organizations — requires that everyday employees, not just designated innovation teams, develop the habit and permission to generate ideas continuously. Utley frames this as a cultural and structural challenge: most organizations have inadvertently built systems that concentrate ideation in a small group while everyone else executes, which dramatically limits total ideaflow.** ([source](Maximizing the Flow of Ideas for Your Organization — interview; Jeremy Utley and Perry Klebahn | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** Audit where in your organization ideation is permitted to happen. If it is concentrated in a single team or meeting type, you have a structural suppression problem. Distributed ideation — where anyone at any level can surface and develop ideas — multiplies total creative output and is a prerequisite for organizational innovation health.

**Most brainstorming sessions are evaluation meetings with a permission slip.** The single highest-leverage facilitation discipline — separating divergent thinking from convergent thinking in time — is also the most rarely practiced discipline in organizations of every size. When generation and evaluation happen simultaneously, evaluation always wins, and the strangest ideas die first. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Facilitators must build a hard structural wall between the generative phase and the judgment phase. Never let a brainstorm session end without first having produced a raw, unevaluated list — and treat any evaluative comment during that phase as a facilitation failure to address in real time.

**Setting an explicit, uncomfortably high idea quota — 50 ideas, 100 ideas — fundamentally changes the social contract in a room. The implicit goal shifts from 'say something impressive' to 'help us hit the number,' which is the only condition under which strange, potentially valuable ideas survive long enough to be heard. Quotas are not gimmicks; they are the mechanism by which psychological safety actually becomes operational.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Before your next brainstorm, anchor the session to a number that feels slightly ridiculous. Watch how the energy shifts from performance to contribution. The quota is the single most underused facilitation lever available to any team leader.

**Ideaflow — the volume and velocity of idea generation per unit of time — is a leading indicator of creative health, while revenue, launches, and patents are lagging. Most organizations audit the wrong end of the pipeline. If you want to know what an organization will produce next year, measure how many ideas its people are generating today, not how many products shipped last quarter.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022); Jeremy Utley and Perry Klebahn | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** Add an ideaflow metric to your team's operating rhythm. Track ideas generated per session or per week, and watch the trend. A declining ideaflow number is an early warning system — not for lack of talent, but for a meeting structure or culture that is silently suppressing generation.

**Utley's signature micro-reframe for escaping evaluative posture is replacing 'What do I think of this idea?' with 'What does this idea make me think of?' The first question positions the thinker as judge; the second positions them as associator. This single linguistic flip can shift an entire group from premature convergence back into generative exploration.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022); Episode 259 – Practicing Innovation with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Teach this reframe to every facilitator and team lead in your organization. Post it on whiteboards. When a brainstorm stalls, it almost always means people are answering the first question when they should be answering the second. The reframe costs nothing and works immediately.

**Most companies have a generation problem, not an evaluation problem.** Organizations invest heavily in frameworks for picking winners from a thin idea pool — stage gates, scoring rubrics, selection committees — without recognizing that a brilliant filter applied to a weak pipeline still produces mediocrity. The highest-leverage intervention is almost always upstream of the funnel, not in refining the selection criteria. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022); Maximizing the Flow of Ideas for Your Organization — interview))

**Implication:** Before adding another evaluation layer to your innovation process, audit the top of the funnel. How many ideas are actually entering it? If the number is low, no downstream process improvement will compensate. Direct your investment to raw idea volume before optimizing how you sort it.

**Effective facilitation is largely about announcing and protecting the cognitive mode the group is in.** Utley describes this as 'flaring or focusing' — are we expanding the option space or narrowing it? Most brainstorming dysfunction comes not from bad ideas or bad people, but from participants applying the wrong cognitive posture at the wrong moment, often without realizing it. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022); Stanford Webinar - Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Make mode-naming a standard facilitation practice. Open each phase of a session by explicitly declaring which mode is active. Give teams permission to call out mode violations — not as policing, but as a shared language for staying coherent about what kind of thinking the moment requires.

**Social dynamics in traditional brainstorming reliably suppress the best ideas.** When a group lacks an explicit high quota, the unspoken norm is impression management — each person is auditioning rather than contributing. This self-censorship hits unconventional ideas hardest, because those ideas carry the highest social risk and the lowest immediate legibility. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022); The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Recognize that the absence of a quota is not neutral — it defaults to a performance contest that systematically filters out your most valuable raw material. High quotas are a social engineering tool that redefines what winning looks like in the room.

**Quantity is the path to quality, not the enemy of it.** Utley points to Picasso, Edison, and Linus Pauling as exemplars of this principle — their masterworks emerged from volume, not despite it. The team chasing 100 ideas reliably outperforms the team searching for the single perfect idea, because breakthroughs are statistically downstream of throughput. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022); Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Stop asking teams to bring their 'best ideas' to a session — that framing activates self-censorship before the meeting even starts. Ask them to bring as many ideas as possible. Grade the session on count, not quality, and let selection happen afterward.

**Creativity is the collision of apparently unrelated frames of reference — ideas are connections, not revelations.** The instruction 'think of something new' is operationally useless because it gives the brain nothing to work with. 'Think of connections' is operational because it points to a concrete cognitive action: finding unexpected links between things you already know. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022); Jeremy Utley on Unconventional Paths to Breakthroughs — video))

**Implication:** Replace open-ended prompts like 'be creative' or 'think outside the box' with structured collision exercises — introduce an unrelated domain, object, or constraint and ask the team to find the connection to the problem at hand. The collisions are the method, not the warm-up.

**Utley describes the Wonder Wander as a deliberate technique for forcing creative collision.** taking an unsolved problem into the physical world and intentionally colliding it with whatever you encounter. A stoplight can become a scarcity alert; a nail salon can model a checkout upsell. The brain alone with itself only recirculates what it already knows — novel input is required to produce novel output. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022); Jeremy Utley on The Power of Productive Procrastination — video))

**Implication:** Build Wonder Wanders into your team's process for stuck problems. Send people out of the building with the question in mind, not to find the answer, but to find unexpected raw material the brain would never surface on its own. Document what they notice and bring it back to the brainstorm.

**The leader's job in innovation is environmental design, not idea supply.** Leaders who try to be the best idea generator in the room become the bottleneck. Leaders who design the conditions — psychological safety, structural separation of generation from evaluation, explicit norms around volume — multiply the creative capacity of everyone around them. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022); Jeremy Utley on How to Build an AI Culture to Lead for the Future — video))

**Implication:** Shift your self-evaluation as a leader from 'Did I contribute a good idea?' to 'Did the session produce more and better ideas than it would have without my facilitation choices?' The room's output is your output. Your design decisions are your contribution.

**When forced to generate an uncomfortable number of ideas, teams inevitably run out of the obvious ones and are pushed into generative territory they would never have reached voluntarily. The discomfort of the quota is precisely the mechanism — it is the forcing function that empties the conventional idea reservoir and opens access to the genuinely novel.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022); The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Design for the moment when the room says 'we've run out of ideas' — that is not the failure point, it is the inflection point. The facilitator's job at that moment is to hold the quota and introduce a new stimulus or prompt, not to let the session end. The best ideas are almost always in the second half.

**Brainstorming sessions frequently fail because they conflate idea generation with idea advocacy.** Once a participant proposes an idea, they become its defender, and the meeting degrades into a debate about that idea rather than a search for more ideas. Structural separation of generation from evaluation eliminates the incentive to advocate and keeps the generative phase open. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022); Episode 248: Jeremy Utley — unSILOed Podcast))

**Implication:** Adopt a rule that no idea is discussed, explained, or defended during the generative phase — only recorded. This removes the social incentive to propose only ideas you can defend, which is precisely the incentive that filters out the most novel contributions.

**The ideation stage of design thinking is where the quantity-breeds-quality principle meets structured process.** The d.school trains practitioners to defer judgment explicitly and generate large volumes of ideas before evaluating any of them — a discipline that runs against most organizational instincts. Utley has pointed out that most so-called brainstorming sessions in organizations violate this principle constantly, producing what amounts to a narrow, safe subset of the possible solution space. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Separate ideation from evaluation in time and, ideally, in personnel. The person who will evaluate the ideas should not be in the room during generation, or at minimum should receive the ideas in written form before a selection meeting.

**Letting conversation partners lead the discussion in unexpected directions — following their passion rather than a fixed agenda — produces richer and more surprising insights. Utley frames this as a deliberate practice: discover their expertise, then let them run with it. Relinquishing conversational control is itself a skill.** ([source](How To Learn From Anyone))

> *"Make a game with yourself to discover what that is, and then allow them to carry the conversation in an unexpected direction, according to their passion."*

**Implication:** In interviews, leadership conversations, or creative collaborations, the instinct to stay 'on script' often limits discovery. Practiced interviewers learn when to release control and follow unexpected threads.

**Multiple perspectives are a powerful force multiplier in idea creation.** Jeremy stresses the importance of having diverse viewpoints when generating ideas, as each additional perspective introduces new angles and combinations that a homogeneous group would never surface. ([source](The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Homogeneous teams are an idea-generation liability. Leaders who build cognitively and experientially diverse teams gain a structural advantage in creative output.

**Jeremy and Kian developed a paradigm for getting the most out of team-based AI-assisted ideation, incorporating concepts like the 'Taylor Swift method,' the 'Einstein effect,' specific GPT prompts, and cognitive bias mitigation. The research wasn't just descriptive — it produced actionable, science-based frameworks for practitioners. This represents a rare combination of empirical rigor and practical application.** ([source](New Research: Get Better Ideas with AI))

> *"Jeremy and Kian have developed a whole paradigm for getting the most out of team based AI assisted ideation. It involves Taylor Swift, the Einstein effect, GPT prompts, cognitive biases."*

**Implication:** Effective AI-assisted ideation is not intuitive — it requires deliberate frameworks. Teams that adopt structured methodologies grounded in behavioral science will consistently outperform those who use AI tools ad hoc.

**Jeremy and Perry teach that unworkable or seemingly silly ideas should not be filtered out during ideation.** Even ideas that are ultimately discarded may contain seeds or components that contribute to the final solution. Stifling the flow kills those hidden contributions. ([source](Maximizing the Flow of Ideas for Your Organization))

> *"To find great ideas, you have to let the unworkable and goofy ideas flow, as well. Don't stifle the process!"*

**Implication:** Teams need explicit permission — and ideally structured facilitation — to explore ideas that feel wrong or embarrassing. The cost of filtering too early is far higher than the cost of considering too many ideas.

**Most people default to convergent thinking — making decisions, eliminating options — because it is rewarded by education, organizational incentives, and daily norms. Divergent thinking, which generates options and embraces variation, is equally critical to innovation but far less practiced.** ([source](Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Teams need explicit permission structures and facilitation practices that protect divergent thinking time — otherwise organizational gravity will collapse every creative session into premature decision-making.

**When diverging, the right question shifts from 'What do I think of this idea?' to 'What does this idea make me think of?' This reframe removes judgment from early-stage ideation and instead uses each idea as a springboard to more ideas.** ([source](Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley))

> *"If I'm diverging from trying to generate possibilities, I'm not going to say, what do I think of this idea? I'm going to say, what does this idea make me think of? Are there other things that come to mind."*

**Implication:** Facilitators and leaders can unlock dramatically more creative output simply by changing the question asked in ideation sessions — a near-zero-cost intervention with significant results.

**Utley frames the question of creative mindset in terms of 'focusing vs.** flaring' — knowing which mode you are in determines how you should show up, what questions you should ask, and how critical or generative you should be. This simple distinction makes the right behavior legible in any given moment. ([source](Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley))

> *"One very simple kind of language we'll use is just, are we focusing or are we flaring? Are we converging? Are we diverging? And that helps me know who I should be when I show up."*

**Implication:** Teams can eliminate much of their creative friction by beginning every meeting with a clear declaration of whether they are in flare mode or focus mode — this single habit changes how people listen, speak, and evaluate.

**Utley deliberately asks ChatGPT to adopt an extreme critical persona — a 'Russian supercritical judgmental editor' — to surface flaws in his own thinking before committing to a direction. By weaponizing harshness as a prompt parameter, he gets the AI to do the work of a demanding editor that most people don't have access to. This transforms GenAI into a pre-mortem machine.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas))

> *"If you're like a Russian supercritical judgmental editor and if you were to do a harsh critique of this brief before we give it to the writer, what would you say are the two glaring errors?"*

**Implication:** Most people prompt AI for validation; Utley prompts it for destruction. Leaders should routinely ask AI to find the fatal flaws in plans, pitches, and briefs before socializing them — it's a fast, low-ego form of stress-testing.

---

## Innovation Process & Problem Framing

**Productivity culture assumes progress should map linearly to time — 10% through the project should mean 10% toward the solution. But breakthrough creative work does not follow this linear logic; the most important cognitive work is often invisible and non-linear.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"This is madness to individuals who want our progress to map perfectly to our time. When we're 10% done, we should be 10% of the way done."*

**Implication:** Measuring creative work by visible linear output is a category error that systematically suppresses the conditions needed for breakthrough thinking.

**Breakthrough thinkers recognize that genuine creative insight requires a gestation period that cannot be rushed or scheduled. This is not inefficiency — it is the cognitive work of incubation, which is the necessary precondition for illumination.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

**Implication:** Organizations and individuals who refuse to budget time for gestation are systematically cutting off the supply chain of their own breakthrough ideas.

**The clearest symptom of cognitive quicksand — organizational thinking stuck in place — is the recurring decision to schedule another meeting without deploying any experiment first. Without new information generated between meetings, no new thinking is possible and the conversation will repeat.** ([source](youtube:AI_in_Learning_Ep1_JeremyUtley))

> *"The most telling symptom of cognitive quicksand is the decision to have another meeting. If there's no new information, there will not be a new conversation. The decision to have another meeting is a procrastination tactic."*

**Implication:** Organizations should treat 'let's have another meeting' as a diagnostic warning sign and require a committed experiment or information-gathering action before the next meeting is scheduled.

**Skills scale linearly — you improve a little and produce a little more.** Systems scale exponentially — you build once and the output multiplies indefinitely. Top performers invest in building systems that do the work, not just in getting better at doing the work themselves. ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"Skills are linear. You get a little better, you produce a little bit more. Systems are exponential. You build something once and it multiplies your output forever."*

**Implication:** The highest-leverage use of professional time is often not execution but system-building — the question 'could I build something that does this for me?' should precede every repetitive task.

**Established companies with long histories and strong brand identities — like Qualtrics being known as 'the survey company' — face a particular transformation challenge: their public identity lags behind their actual strategic investments. Years of investment in AI and machine learning can be invisible to the market if the product suite hasn't caught up, creating a gap between internal capability and external perception.** ([source](youtube:jjbVeFyjuxU))

**Implication:** For legacy companies undergoing AI transformation, the strategic challenge is not just building new capability but making that capability visible — both to the market and internally, so that employees understand the new identity they are being asked to help build.

**Ideas are cheap; insights are expensive.** The real competitive advantage of design thinking is building a team's consistent capacity to generate breakthrough insights — not just generating ideas. ([source](youtube:Stanford Instructor - Meet Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Organizations that conflate idea generation with insight generation are investing in the wrong output; the scarce and valuable resource is the deep understanding that makes ideas meaningful.

**The 'How Might We' or 'How Can I' question format is a precision instrument that narrows the solution space to human-centered, actionable territory. By specifying both the target person and the desired action or change, the question eliminates vague problem statements and forces clarity about what success looks like.** ([source](special_dschool_mission))

> *"how can i get a specific group or person to engage in a specific physical action or mental change — how can i get somebody to do something"*

**Implication:** Teams and individuals who invest in crafting well-formed questions before ideating will generate solutions that are more targeted and implementable than those who brainstorm from poorly defined problems.

**Design thinking makes two meaningful advances over conventional problem solving.** first, it advocates diverging before converging on solutions; second, it recognizes that problem finding is as important as problem solving, and that both deserve a diverge-then-converge discipline. ([source](stanford-webinar-design-thinking-what-is-it))

> *"Design thinking makes two meaningful advances. The first advance that we advocate is we say, hang on, before we converge on one solution, let's diverge... The other significant advance that we have made in the d.school is that we said, actually let's recognize that there's two pieces to any problem solving endeavor... problem finding."*

**Implication:** Organizations that skip problem finding and jump straight to solution convergence are systematically solving the wrong problems, no matter how good their solution-generation tools are.

**Using multiple selection criteria — rather than a single ranking — is a more powerful way to evaluate a large pool of ideas. Different criteria (budget feasibility, customer delight, competitive differentiation) surface different promising ideas and reveal a richer picture of the solution space.** ([source](stanford-webinar-design-thinking-what-is-it))

**Implication:** Teams that reduce idea selection to a single dimension — usually feasibility or cost — systematically discard ideas that would have been breakthrough on other dimensions like customer delight or differentiation.

**Human-centered design is not the same as customer-led design.** Asking customers what they want produces unreliable data because customers cannot accurately predict their own future behavior and are socially motivated to be agreeable. ([source](stanford-webinar-design-thinking-what-is-it))

> *"Human centered design is not customer led design. It's not taking instructions from the customer, far from it. And you have to be careful to craft experiments, really, is what we would say. It's an experimental mindset that give you a highly credible data."*

**Implication:** The discipline of design research is not about asking better questions in surveys — it is about designing interactions and experiments that generate behavioral data rather than stated preferences.

**Innovation is not about intense focus alone — it requires playful variation as well.** The myth that innovation is purely about disciplined subtraction ignores the generative, playful, high-volume ideation that precedes great selection decisions. Both poles are necessary: generating many dopey ideas AND ruthlessly selecting from them. ([source](talk:zero-to-one-myth))

> *"It's very important to realize that innovation is not just about intense focus. It's also about playful variation as well."*

**Implication:** Innovation frameworks and leadership models should balance the celebrated discipline of selection with the undervalued practice of prolific, playful generation.

**The four-stage creative process — preparation, incubation, illumination, validation — is well-established, but incubation is the most neglected stage in modern work culture. Because we are always on and hyper-efficiency oriented, we deprive our minds of the marination time that allows unexpected connections to form. Working differently — not harder — is the solution to creative blocks.** ([source](talk:zero-to-one-myth))

> *"Incubation is where the money gets made, so to speak. It's the most overlooked step today because we don't have any time to marinate... imagination demands space to sometimes make synthesis of these new connections."*

**Implication:** Organizations should explicitly design incubation time into creative work — protecting periods of apparent inactivity as essential stages in the innovation process.

**The second myth of innovation — that it requires 1,000% focus and relentless elimination — is incomplete without its prerequisite: first generating thousands of options to select from. Steve Jobs's essentialism was only possible because he first generated enormous volume. The essentialism quote puts emphasis on the wrong place if taken without the generative context.** ([source](talk:zero-to-one-myth))

> *"I realized I'd be putting the emphasis on the wrong place. He said, I'm always emphasizing Steve Jobs says no, but talking with you about the ideas ratio, he said, I realize that's not the first thing. The first thing is he had a thousand things to say no to."*

**Implication:** Leaders and strategists who adopt 'focus' as their primary innovation principle without first investing in prolific generation are practicing the second half of the innovation process while skipping the first.

**AI now offers a powerful new tool for problem reframing.** teams can ask AI to rewrite the same problem statement from ten different angles, at different levels of abstraction, or from different stakeholder perspectives in seconds. Utley positions this as one of AI's highest-value applications in innovation work — not generating solutions, but multiplying the number of ways a problem can be seen before any solution work begins. ([source](New Research: Get Better Ideas with AI — podcast))

**Implication:** Use AI as a problem-reframing engine before using it as an idea generator. Feed it your current problem statement and ask it to generate fifteen alternative framings, then bring those alternative framings to the human team for discussion. The diversity of framing alone often unlocks solution directions that would never have emerged from the original statement.

**When Utley demonstrates AI use with executive audiences, one of the most powerful applications he surfaces is using AI to challenge and stress-test a problem framing in real time. By feeding a draft problem statement to an AI and asking it to identify hidden assumptions, suggest alternative framings, and flag scope issues, teams can compress what would otherwise be a multi-hour problem-definition workshop into a rapid iterative exchange that sharpens the question before any human ideation begins.** ([source](Stanford University's Jeremy Utley. AI's brainstorming and problem-solving potential — video))

**Implication:** Integrate AI as a problem-statement critic into the front end of your innovation process. Before distributing a challenge brief to a team, run it through an AI conversation specifically designed to expose its weaknesses. Treat the AI's reframings not as final answers but as prompts for human deliberation about which framing is most generative.

**Utley teaches that the instinct to immediately generate solutions upon encountering a problem is the primary enemy of breakthrough innovation. The productive procrastination of remaining in the problem space — deliberately deferring solution-mode — is not laziness but a form of cognitive discipline that allows the problem to reveal its real structure before constraints are imposed.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on The Power of Productive Procrastination — video))

**Implication:** Build structured delays into innovation processes. Schedule problem-immersion sessions that have an explicit prohibition on solution proposals. The discomfort of sitting with an unsolved problem is often the signal that the reframing work is still incomplete.

**Utley frames the 'Zero to One' problem — the myth that the hardest part of innovation is the first idea — as a framing trap. Teams that romanticize the moment of initial inspiration misunderstand where leverage actually lives. The critical work is not the flash of the first idea but the disciplined refinement of the question that makes the right first idea possible.** ([source](Zero to One: The Myth That Keeps Individuals, Teams, and Organizations from Breakthrough Innovation — video))

**Implication:** Deprioritize the mythology of the eureka moment in your innovation culture. Replace it with systematic question-design rituals that treat problem framing as a team sport requiring iteration, not an individual act of inspiration requiring genius.

**Utley teaches that constraints, when properly understood, are not enemies of creativity but framers of it.** A problem with no constraints is actually a problem with no useful frame — it produces unconstrained brainstorming that generates low-signal, unactionable ideas. The most generative creative constraints are those that reflect real-world conditions while remaining flexible enough to allow unusual solutions. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Unconventional Paths to Breakthroughs — video))

**Implication:** When designing a problem statement, make constraints explicit and examine them carefully. Some constraints are real and should be honored; others are assumed and should be challenged. The act of sorting real constraints from assumed ones is itself a form of problem reframing that almost always expands the solution space.

**Utley positions the question 'what does this make me think of?' as a micro-reframing intervention that can be applied at any stage of the innovation process. The distinction between 'what do I think of this?' and 'what does this make me think of?' is not semantic — it is a shift from evaluation mode to association mode. Applied to problem framing, it transforms the analysis of an existing problem statement into generative exploration of adjacent problem territories.** ([source](The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Train facilitators and innovation leads to deploy this question pivot as a standard tool. Whenever a discussion collapses into evaluation of the current framing, redirecting with 'what does this problem make you think of?' can rapidly surface new angles, analogous challenges from other domains, and reframings that break the convergent gravity.

**Utley draws a sharp line between problems that are well-understood and under-solved versus problems that are well-funded but poorly framed. Many organizations pour resources into the latter, building sophisticated solution systems for a challenge that was never examined rigorously enough to know whether solving it would produce real value. The discipline of asking 'is this the right problem to solve?' before 'how do we solve it?' is disproportionately rare and disproportionately valuable.** ([source](Episode 248: Jeremy Utley — unSILOed Podcast))

**Implication:** Before approving resource allocation to any new innovation initiative, require the proposing team to present not just a solution concept but a documented case for why this is the right problem — including what alternative framings were considered and rejected, and what evidence of real user or market need exists.

**Utley teaches that creative breakthroughs often emerge not from generating more ideas within an existing frame but from discovering that the frame itself was the constraint. This is what distinguishes truly generative innovation from sophisticated optimization — the optimizer accepts the frame and works within it; the innovator questions whether the frame is the real problem. Developing the reflex to challenge the frame before engaging with it is a learnable and teachable skill.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Unconventional Paths to Breakthroughs — video))

**Implication:** Build a 'frame challenge' step into every innovation sprint before ideation begins. Ask the team: what if the frame we've been given is itself the problem? What frame would open up solution spaces we haven't yet considered? This single question, asked consistently, is one of the highest-return interventions available to innovation facilitators.

**Most innovation failures are not execution failures — they are framing failures.** Teams invest enormous energy solving problems with skill and diligence, only to discover they were solving the wrong problem entirely. The discipline of sitting with a problem longer, resisting the pull toward solution, and interrogating whether the question itself is correctly formed is the highest-leverage skill in the innovation toolkit. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Before any ideation session, teams should invest proportionally more time in problem definition than feels comfortable. If the problem statement can be written in thirty seconds, it probably hasn't been examined deeply enough to deserve a solution.

**Reframing is not a creative luxury — it is a systematic practice with concrete techniques.** One of the most powerful is shifting the level of abstraction in the problem statement: zoom out and the problem becomes a different challenge entirely; zoom in and what seemed like a strategic question becomes an operational one. The same underlying situation can generate radically different solution spaces depending solely on how the question is pitched. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** When a brainstorm produces only incremental ideas, the first diagnostic question is not 'do we need better people?' but 'is the framing too narrow or too broad?' Rewriting the problem statement at a higher or lower level of abstraction is the fastest way to unlock a new solution space.

**The difference between an incremental innovation and a breakthrough innovation is often traceable to the level of reframing that occurred at the question stage. Incremental innovations typically answer the question as given; breakthrough innovations reframe the question itself. Utley cites this pattern as a core insight from studying innovation at scale — the teams that produce genuinely new things almost always changed the question, not just the answer.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** When an ideation session is producing only incremental ideas, treat that as diagnostic feedback about the framing, not about the team's creativity. The solution is not to push harder for better answers — it is to return to the problem statement and ask whether a different question would open more generative territory.

**Utley describes the 'Wonder Wander' as a physical practice of problem reframing.** taking a poorly-defined problem out into the world and deliberately colliding it with unrelated stimuli to generate new angles on what the problem actually is. The value is not the random stimuli themselves but the cognitive habit of asking 'what does this tell me about my problem?' — a question that breaks the echo chamber of working entirely within one's existing knowledge. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** For problems that feel stuck or that keep generating the same class of solutions, prescribe a structured physical excursion before the next ideation session. Give team members the problem statement and instruct them to spend thirty minutes in an unfamiliar environment, actively asking how each thing they encounter reframes or illuminates the problem.

**Utley's research and teaching experience consistently point to the same upstream bottleneck.** teams have more ideation capacity than they use, but that capacity is constrained by the quality of the problem they've been given to work on. A mediocre problem statement caps the ceiling of even the best brainstorming session. Raising the quality of the challenge brief is therefore a higher-leverage investment than improving the ideation process itself. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Professionalize the creation of challenge briefs in your organization. Treat the challenge brief as a deliverable that requires iteration, peer review, and quality standards — not as a task that can be drafted in five minutes before the meeting. The hour invested in a well-crafted challenge brief multiplies the return on every hour the team subsequently spends in ideation.

**The 'How Might We' question is not a warm-up exercise or a facilitation formality — it is a precision instrument that determines the entire solution space before a single idea is generated. A narrowly framed HMW question produces incremental answers; a broadly or creatively framed one opens up entirely different solution territories. The quality of the question is a direct ceiling on the quality of the answers.** ([source](Stanford Webinar - Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Treat the construction of the HMW question as a standalone work session, not a preamble. Draft multiple versions, stress-test each one for hidden assumptions, and choose the framing that opens the most generative space rather than the one that feels safest.

**Design thinking's most underappreciated step is the one that happens before ideation.** deep empathy work that reframes what problem is actually worth solving. Utley emphasizes that d.school methodology is built on the premise that the user's stated problem is rarely the real problem — skilled innovators treat problem discovery as iterative fieldwork, not a preliminary checkbox. ([source](Stanford Webinar - Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Innovation teams should spend at least as much structured time in problem discovery as in solution generation. The impulse to skip to ideation is a bias toward action that reliably produces elegant solutions to misidentified problems.

**One of Utley's consistent observations from teaching at Stanford d.school is that most problem statements contain hidden assumptions that silently constrain the solution space. These assumptions are invisible precisely because they feel like facts rather than choices. Making assumptions explicit — writing them down and then asking 'what if this assumption were false?' — is a rapid technique for expanding the range of possible solutions.** ([source](Stanford Webinar - Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Add an assumption-surfacing step to every HMW formulation process. For each problem statement, list every assumption embedded in it, then systematically challenge each one. The most productive creative breakthroughs often come from invalidating an assumption that everyone in the room had unconsciously treated as fixed.

**Utley challenges students to treat finding what makes their conversation partner fascinating as a personal challenge, reframing the task from passive luck to active discovery. This mindset shift turns any assigned customer into a puzzle worth solving. The goal is to discover why your interviewee is the most interesting person in the room.** ([source](How To Learn From Anyone))

> *"Your goal is to discover why you're the luckiest team in the room, why your customer is the most interesting customer you could have been randomly assigned. Take it as a personal challenge to discover what makes your conversation partner the most fascinating person in the room."*

**Implication:** For researchers, designers, and leaders conducting conversations, reframing curiosity as a competitive challenge — rather than hoping for a 'good' subject — unlocks richer insights from any interaction.

**Jeremy defines innovation simply as an effective solution to a problem — not as a technology product or a startup idea.** This definition deliberately decouples innovation from the tech world and makes it universally applicable across HR, finance, operations, and any domain where problems exist. ([source](Episode 259 – Practicing Innovation with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Every professional, regardless of industry or function, is a potential innovator. Reframing innovation as effective problem-solving removes the gatekeeping that causes non-tech workers to opt out of creative thinking.

**Utley's book Ideaflow proposes a strategy not just for generating more ideas but for determining which ones are worth pursuing. The framework positions idea generation and idea evaluation as distinct, coupled disciplines. Without a method for filtering, high volume alone doesn't produce innovation.** ([source](Episode 248: Jeremy Utley — unSILOed Podcast))

**Implication:** Innovation systems need two engines: one for generating ideas at volume and one for systematically evaluating which to pursue. Building only one without the other is a structural gap most organizations have.

**When Jeremy first saw the research results come in, his immediate reaction was 'oh no' — a signal he interprets as a good sign for academic research. Surprising or uncomfortable findings that challenge your hypotheses are intellectually valuable. The data revealed something both fascinating and cautionary about how cognitive bias undermines AI-assisted teams.** ([source](New Research: Get Better Ideas with AI))

> *"When we first started seeing results flow in, my first thought was, oh no, oh no. You know, and actually it's a good sign as an academic researcher when you get the data back and you think, oh no, because then you wait a few beats. And I remember that first day, Kian and I are on the phone looking at stuff and I go, Oh, yeah."*

**Implication:** For researchers and innovators alike, data that surprises or unsettles you is often the most valuable. Build processes that surface uncomfortable results rather than confirming what you already believe.

**Jeremy distinguishes between tasks and problems.** tasks have known solutions and are simply executed, while problems are situations where the right path is unclear. For problems, the instinct to reach for a single solution is a trap — what's needed first is a volume of ideas before any evaluation begins. ([source](Maximizing the Flow of Ideas for Your Organization))

> *"Tasks are straightforward. You know how to do. The answer is apparent. It's just a matter of execution. But a problem is something that you're not exactly sure what the right way to handle it is."*

**Implication:** Leaders and teams should resist the urge to converge on a solution immediately. The first move when facing a genuine problem should always be divergence — generating many possibilities before selecting any.

**Jeremy and Perry warn against locking into solutions too early, even when something appears to be working.** Early-stage success can create a false sense of certainty that prematurely closes off exploration of potentially superior alternatives. ([source](Maximizing the Flow of Ideas for Your Organization))

> *"In the early stages of business, resist the urge to 'lock in' on something just because it is working. Continue to generate ideas and innovate."*

**Implication:** Early traction is a signal to keep exploring, not to stop. Founders and leaders should treat initial success as validation of the space, not as permission to stop generating alternatives.

**Utley and Klebahn argue that every problem is fundamentally an idea problem — and that reframing how you think about any problem is the key to unlocking success. The Ideaflow framework offers a proven strategy for routinely generating and commercializing breakthrough ideas.** ([source](Jeremy Utley Media Hub))

> *"every problem is an idea problem, and changing the way you think about any problem will unleash success"*

**Implication:** Problem-solving should always begin with a reframing step. Before jumping to solutions, leaders should ask whether they've correctly diagnosed the idea gap at the root of their challenge.

**Utley uses GenAI to workshop not just content but the brief that precedes the content — asking the AI to act as an editor giving instructions to a copywriter before a word of the actual piece is written. This upstream use of AI to sharpen the creative brief is a structural innovation: it catches conceptual errors before they propagate into hours of wasted drafting.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Prompting GenAI for New Ideas))

> *"Be my editor who's giving instructions to my copywriter exactly how to think about approaching this piece. Could you craft that set of instructions?"*

**Implication:** AI should be deployed earlier in the creative process than most teams currently use it. Investing AI cycles in brief development and problem framing — before any execution begins — dramatically reduces downstream rework and misalignment.

---

## Problem Finding & Question Design

**The most eminent creative practitioners tend to delay decisions longer than their peers, resisting the urge to act prematurely. This productive procrastination is not laziness but a deliberate tolerance for the ambiguity required before genuine insight emerges.
