Frank Lloyd Wright's approach to Fallingwater — waiting weeks before touching the drafting table — exemplifies this pattern.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"The most imminent practitioners were far more likely to delay decisions than their counterparts. They tended to just not decide. This is maddening to people who are productivity oriented."*

**Implication:** High performers should resist the cultural pressure to show linear progress and instead protect time for non-linear gestation before committing to solutions.

**An unsolved problem that gnaws persistently in your gut is not a source of anxiety to be eliminated — it is a gift.** That feeling of 'we haven't solved this right yet' is the lived experience of incubation, and it is precisely the condition from which rich ideas and illumination emerge. ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"A real gift is a problem that is gnawing in the pit of your stomach because you go, I don't think we've solved it right yet. Listen to that. That's incubation. That's where really rich ideas come from. That's where the illumination occurs."*

**Implication:** Reframing persistent creative discomfort from a problem to be solved to a signal to be honored changes the relationship to ambiguity and dramatically increases the likelihood of genuine insight.

**Innovators and great product builders share a foundational habit.** keeping a 'bug list' — a running record of things that bother them. This attentiveness to problems is not incidental; it is the generative engine that produces solutions. Since the 1960s Stanford has assigned this practice because problem-awareness is the precondition for ideation. ([source](youtube:AI_in_Learning_Ep1_JeremyUtley))

> *"Since the 1960s, we've been giving students at Stanford this assignment. Keep a bug list. Write down a list of the things that bug you. If they pay attention to things that bother them, they become the kind of person who thinks of solutions."*

**Implication:** Teams and individuals who systematically log friction points and pain observations will consistently out-ideate those who wait for inspiration.

**Small frustrations and annoyances are seeds of innovation.** The Stanford d.school's 'bug list' exercise — writing down everything that bugs you — is grounded in the principle that great products begin as someone's annoyance. Bette Nesmith Graham invented Liquid Paper from frustration with messy corrections at a typing job. ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"One of the most powerful practices we teach at the D-School is something called a bug list. Write down the things that bug you. Frustrations, annoyances, small ways in which you see the world doesn't work the way it should. Because small frustrations are the seeds of innovation. Every great product started as somebody else's annoyance."*

**Implication:** Systematically collecting and examining personal frustrations is a practical and underused problem-finding method that surfaces real, human-centered innovation opportunities.

**Effective design thinking sessions require participants to arrive with pre-formed questions rather than vague problems.** Framing challenges as 'How can I get a specific person or group to engage in a specific action or change?' focuses creative energy on human behavior and concrete outcomes from the start. ([source](special_dschool_mission))

> *"your pre-work is bring three to five simple questions to the session tomorrow... the specific format we'd like you to use for the question is how can i get a specific group or person to engage in a specific physical action or mental change"*

**Implication:** Loading participants with behaviorally-framed questions before a design thinking session dramatically increases the relevance and actionability of ideation work done in the session.

**Generating good design thinking questions begins with surveying the diverse areas of your life — work projects, relationships, meetings, customers, passions — rather than fixating on a single domain. Breadth of reflection increases the probability of surfacing a genuinely important challenge to work on.** ([source](special_dschool_mission))

> *"first thing is reflect on the various areas of your life or aspects of your life... think of the various areas of your life first"*

**Implication:** A broad personal inventory before question generation prevents participants from defaulting to obvious or surface-level problems and surfaces richer, more motivating design challenges.

**After identifying an area of life or work, the productive next step is to identify a specific struggle or uncertainty within that area. The struggle or uncertainty is the raw material that, when translated into a 'how can I get someone to do something' format, becomes a tractable design challenge.** ([source](special_dschool_mission))

> *"after you've thought of those areas of your life think of a specific struggle or uncertainty related to that area... after you've picked an area of your life pick a struggle and uncertainty"*

**Implication:** The two-step move — area then struggle — gives practitioners a repeatable method for converting vague dissatisfaction into concrete, workable design questions.

**To generate multiple questions, practitioners should cycle through multiple life or work areas rather than mining a single domain exhaustively. Each new area visited can yield its own struggle, which in turn yields another question — creating a generative loop rather than a single linear path.** ([source](special_dschool_mission))

> *"in order to generate the next question i might visit another area of my work or life and then ask myself what's a struggle or uncertainty in that area"*

**Implication:** Teaching practitioners to iterate across domains for question generation ensures they arrive at sessions with genuinely diverse challenges rather than variations on a single theme.

**The question is every bit as important as the answer.** As John Dewey said, 'A problem well put is half solved.' Much of the real work of innovation is framing a problem worth solving before ever generating solutions. ([source](stanford-webinar-design-thinking-what-is-it))

> *"John Dewey, the famous American educational reformer said, 'A problem well put is half solved.' And so a lot of the efforts that we spend in the design practice... a lot of your time is actually spent framing a problem worth solving before you ever even undertake the tools to think about how you'd solve it."*

**Implication:** Teams that invest in sharpening their problem statement before generating solutions will produce more targeted and breakthrough innovations than those who rush to ideation.

**Design thinking reframes business problems from output-focused imperatives ('increase revenue,' 'sell more') to human-centered problem statements ('our users feel fear') that open up fundamentally different and more valuable solution spaces.** ([source](stanford-webinar-design-thinking-what-is-it))

> *"Too often we sort of walk through life accepting an existing problem statement. A lot of business problem statements might be around how do we increase revenue or how do we sell more. And the reframe that happened with Bill was he learned, hey for our products, there's this emotion fear. And I'm going to actually use that to design, and ultimately design a far better product."*

**Implication:** The most valuable innovation work often happens before any solution is generated — in the reframing of the problem from a business metric to a human experience worth improving.

**Utley makes a clear distinction between problems worth solving and problems that are merely visible and urgent.** Urgency creates a gravitational pull toward the presenting problem — the symptom — rather than the underlying structure generating the symptom. Skilled innovators develop a discipline of asking 'is this the problem, or is this the evidence of a problem?' before committing resources. ([source](Episode 259 – Practicing Innovation with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** When a problem surfaces in your organization, create a structured pause before solution work begins. Ask the team: is what we're describing the root problem or a symptom? Treating symptoms with sophisticated solutions is resource-intensive and produces recurrence; addressing root causes requires more upfront framing investment but generates durable change.

**Sitting with a problem — resisting the gravitational pull toward solutions — is an act of discipline, not passivity.** Utley frames the discomfort of the open question as productive, because premature closure on a solution is one of the most common and costly mistakes teams make. The willingness to stay in the question space longer than feels comfortable is what separates genuine breakthroughs from incremental improvements. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Unconventional Paths to Breakthroughs — video))

**Implication:** Leaders should explicitly name and protect the problem-exploration phase in meetings. When someone jumps to solutions, the facilitator's job is to redirect back to the question — not because the solution is wrong, but because the question may not yet be right.

**Utley draws a sharp distinction between working on the right problem and working on the problem right.** Most organizations are extraordinarily competent at the latter — optimizing, executing, refining — and dramatically underpowered at the former. Efficiency applied to the wrong problem is not just wasteful; it actively crowds out the time and attention needed to find better problems. ([source](Episode 248: Jeremy Utley — unSILOed Podcast))

**Implication:** Build a regular organizational ritual — quarterly, at minimum — dedicated to questioning whether the problems currently on the roadmap are actually the highest-value problems. Execution excellence should never substitute for problem-selection rigor.

**Utley identifies a consistent pattern.** the teams that generate the most breakthrough ideas are not the ones with the highest IQ or the most experience — they are the ones who spent the most time upstream, in the question space, before generating solutions. Time invested in problem articulation is not a cost; it is a multiplier on everything that follows. ([source](The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** When a team feels stuck in ideation, the answer is rarely to brainstorm harder. The answer is almost always to go back upstream and ask whether the problem is framed correctly. Better questions unlock better ideas faster than any ideation technique.

**One of Utley's core teaching moves is distinguishing between a symptom and a root problem.** What teams present as the problem to be solved is frequently a surface manifestation of a deeper, more important problem. Training people to ask 'is this actually the problem, or a symptom of the problem?' is one of the highest-value interventions in any innovation curriculum. ([source](The Reason You Don't Have Better Ideas — video))

**Implication:** Add a 'five whys' or root-cause interrogation step before finalizing any problem statement. If the team cannot articulate why solving this problem matters beyond the immediate surface, they have likely not yet found the real problem worth solving.

**Utley argues that AI can be a powerful partner in the problem-finding phase — not just the solution-generation phase.** When given a rich problem context, AI can help surface adjacent framings, challenge the assumptions embedded in the original question, and generate alternative 'How Might We' statements that human teams would not naturally produce. The bottleneck is whether people think to use AI upstream, before solutions, rather than only downstream. ([source](Stanford University's Jeremy Utley. AI's brainstorming and problem-solving potential — video))

**Implication:** Experiment with using AI at the start of a project to interrogate the problem framing: ask it to identify the assumptions in your problem statement, to reframe the question from five different stakeholder perspectives, and to articulate what you might be missing. This upstream AI use is often more valuable than using AI to generate solution ideas.

**Utley frequently observes that the most innovative organizations are distinguished not by their ability to generate better solutions, but by their ability to notice better problems. Problem-noticing — the capacity to see friction, unmet needs, or embedded assumptions that others have normalized — is the upstream competency that makes everything else possible. It is also one of the most trainable skills that most organizations never deliberately develop.** ([source](Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Create structured problem-noticing practices inside your organization: customer shadowing sessions, friction logs, assumption audits. Reward people who surface a better problem, not just people who deliver a polished solution to the problem they were handed.

**Utley argues that the most dangerous moment in any innovation process is when a team mistakes confidence in their problem statement for correctness. Early confidence in a framing shuts down the exploratory posture that better framing requires. Building in deliberate skepticism about the problem statement — even after the team feels they have it right — is a structural safeguard against the most expensive kind of innovation failure.** ([source](Episode 259 — Practicing Innovation with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Institutionalize a 'problem statement challenge' step: after a team has agreed on their problem framing, assign one person the role of interrogating it. Have them argue that the real problem is different, that the framing contains hidden assumptions, or that solving this problem would not actually produce the outcome the team wants.

**Utley observes that most brainstorming meetings implicitly begin with a problem statement that was written by one person, under time pressure, before the meeting started — and then treated as fixed throughout the session. This single structural habit is responsible for a large fraction of innovation failures, because it means the team never had permission to question whether they were working on the right problem in the first place.** ([source](The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley — podcast))

**Implication:** Make the opening of every brainstorm a problem-statement workshop, not a problem-statement presentation. The question should be co-created or at minimum interrogated by the full group before any solution generation begins. The ten minutes spent doing this will save hours of misaligned ideation.

**Utley teaches that narrow problem framing is often a form of hidden risk aversion.** When a team frames a problem very tightly — constraining the scope, the user, the acceptable solution types — they are often unconsciously managing anxiety about openness rather than accurately describing the problem. Recognizing this pattern is the first step toward asking genuinely ambitious questions. ([source](Zero to One: The Myth That Keeps Individuals, Teams, and Organizations from Breakthrough Innovation — video))

**Implication:** When a problem statement feels 'safe' or immediately actionable, treat that as a signal that it may be too narrow. Ask the team: what are we ruling out by framing it this way, and are we ruling it out because we know it's irrelevant or because it feels uncomfortable to include?

**Utley frequently uses the language of 'flaring' and 'focusing' to describe the two modes of creative work — and problem finding is fundamentally a flaring activity. Before narrowing in on a single problem definition, teams should generate multiple candidate problem statements, explore adjacent framings, and allow the question space to expand before it contracts. The discipline is not premature focus but disciplined expansion followed by deliberate convergence.** ([source](Episode 248: Jeremy Utley — unSILOed Podcast))

**Implication:** Treat the problem-finding phase with the same diverge-then-converge structure you would apply to ideation. Generate ten possible problem framings before committing to one. The version you started with may not survive contact with the alternatives — and that is the point.

**Utley connects the scarcity of good problem-finders to the way creativity is typically taught — or not taught.** Because most educational and professional environments reward people for having right answers quickly, the skill of dwelling productively in uncertainty, holding open questions, and resisting premature resolution is systematically undertrained. Great problem-finders are not geniuses; they are people who have built the tolerance for ambiguity that others abandoned early. ([source](Jeremy Utley: AI Ignites Human Creativity — video))

**Implication:** Build ambiguity tolerance explicitly into innovation programs. Assign problems with no clear answer and reward the quality of the questions asked, not the speed of resolution. This is the competency that compounds over a career and cannot be downloaded from a framework.

**Utley points out that asking AI to help interrogate a problem framing — to steelman the question, identify its hidden assumptions, and propose alternative framings — is one of the highest-ROI uses of AI in the innovation process. Most people ask AI to help them once they have already decided what the problem is; the greater leverage comes from using AI before that decision is made, as a rigorous thinking partner on the question itself.** ([source](AI as Your New Strategic Thought Partner with Jeremy Utley, Stanford, Ideaflow, & Beyond the Prompt — video))

**Implication:** Before finalizing any problem statement, feed it to an AI system and ask it to: (1) identify assumptions embedded in the framing, (2) restate the problem from three different stakeholder perspectives, and (3) propose a more ambitious version of the same question. Use its output not as the answer but as a pressure-test on your framing.

**Utley observes that the organizations most likely to solve the wrong problem are those with the most capable execution cultures. High-competence organizations are rewarded so consistently for delivering against defined briefs that they develop a structural bias against questioning the brief itself. The very capabilities that make them excellent executors make them poor problem-finders — and the switch from one mode to the other requires deliberate structural intervention, not just cultural encouragement.** ([source](Maximizing the Flow of Ideas for Your Organization — interview))

**Implication:** In high-performance organizations, create protected spaces — specific teams, specific sessions, specific leadership postures — where the normal execution norms are explicitly suspended, and questioning the problem is not just permitted but required. Without structural permission, cultural capability will always default to execution.

**Most innovation failures are not execution failures — they are framing failures.** Teams invest enormous energy solving the wrong problem with great precision. The highest-leverage moment in any innovation process is not when you pick the best solution, but when you decide which question is actually worth answering. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Before any ideation session, teams should invest deliberate time auditing whether they are working on the right problem. A well-framed question is not overhead — it is the most valuable work product the team will produce.

**The ability to find the right problem is rarer and more valuable than the ability to solve problems.** Solvers are abundant; problem finders are scarce. Most professional training — in business schools, engineering programs, and corporate development — focuses almost entirely on solution generation, leaving the prior and more critical skill of problem identification largely unaddressed. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Organizations that want genuine innovation should invest in training people to find better problems, not just generate more solutions. Problem-finding workshops, reframing exercises, and structured question audits are as important as any ideation methodology.

**The 'Wonder Wander' — a deliberate practice of taking a problem out into the physical world and colliding it with unrelated stimuli — is partly a problem-reframing tool. When you carry a question into a new environment, you are forced to see it through different lenses: a nail salon becomes a model for upselling, a traffic light becomes a scarcity signal. The environment reshapes the problem, not just the solutions.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Assign a 'Wonder Wander' before finalizing a problem statement — not just to generate ideas, but to test whether the framing holds up when viewed from radically different contexts. If the problem looks different from every angle, you may not yet have found its stable core.

**Utley connects great question design to the idea that creativity is fundamentally about making connections across frames of reference. A well-designed question is one that deliberately creates productive tension between two frames — the user's experience and the business constraint, the current reality and the desired future, the technical limit and the human need. That tension is not a problem with the question; it is the generative engine inside it.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** When crafting a problem statement, explicitly name the tension the question holds — who or what is in conflict, and what makes this hard. A question with no embedded tension is likely a question whose answer is already known, which means it does not need innovation; it needs execution.

**The d.school's 'How Might We' question format is more than a linguistic trick — it is a precision instrument for reframing problems at exactly the right level of abstraction. Too narrow and the HMW forecloses creative options; too broad and it offers no traction. Utley has identified crafting the right HMW as the highest-leverage skill in the entire innovation toolkit, because the quality of a question determines the quality of the solution space it opens.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Teams should spend substantive time — not minutes but sessions — iterating on the framing of their challenge before generating solutions. Treat the HMW as a design artifact in its own right, not as a warm-up exercise.

**Utley has noted that most innovation failures are not execution failures — they are framing failures that happened long before any execution began. Teams spend enormous energy solving the wrong problem beautifully because they never invested in reframing the challenge from multiple angles before converging. The d.school's insistence on a deep define phase before ideation is structurally designed to catch framing errors while they are still cheap to fix.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Add a formal 'reframe audit' to your innovation process: before any ideation, require the team to generate at least five alternative framings of the problem and consciously choose which one to pursue. The choice itself is a strategic decision.

**A core insight from d.school methodology that Utley repeatedly surfaces is that innovation is not primarily about generating solutions — it is about finding the right problem. Most organizations are systematically over-invested in solution generation and under-invested in problem finding. This imbalance is structural, not accidental: solution generation feels productive and legible; problem finding feels ambiguous and uncomfortable.** ([source](Jeremy Utley and Perry Klebahn | Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** Leaders should audit their innovation process for the ratio of time spent in problem exploration versus solution generation. If the ratio heavily favors solution work, the organization is likely operating with a false economy — moving fast toward destinations that may not be worth reaching.

**Utley consistently emphasizes that the most valuable input to the innovation process is not raw ideas — it is correctly identified problems. The innovation pipeline is not an idea funnel; it is a question funnel. Organizations that deliberately curate and refine their problem statements before opening them to ideation produce systematically better output from the same creative talent than organizations that skip directly to idea generation.** ([source](Jeremy Utley and Perry Klebahn | Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** Create an explicit problem portfolio alongside your idea portfolio. Invest in maintaining a curated library of well-framed problems — questions that have been stress-tested for assumptions, abstraction level, and scope. A richly populated problem portfolio is a strategic asset that makes every future ideation session more productive.

**Utley teaches that the quality of a question is often a direct proxy for the quality of the thinking that produced it.** Vague questions generate vague ideas; precise, human-centered, tension-filled questions generate ideas that are specific enough to actually test. Learning to craft a great question is therefore a metacognitive skill — it forces you to articulate what you know, what you don't know, and what tension you are trying to resolve. ([source](Jeremy Utley and Perry Klebahn | Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters | Talks at Google — video))

**Implication:** Make question quality a visible, reviewable artifact. Before a brainstorm, write the problem statement on a whiteboard and invite the room to improve it. Treat a better question as a meaningful deliverable — not just a warm-up.

**The 'How Might We' framing is not a warm-up exercise — it is a precision instrument.** Crafting the right question before generating any solutions is the single highest-leverage skill in the innovation toolkit. A narrow question produces incremental answers; a reframed question can open entirely new solution spaces that the original framing made invisible. ([source](Stanford Webinar — Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Facilitators and team leads should treat question-crafting as a standalone work session, not a preamble to brainstorming. Spend as much time sharpening the 'How Might We' as you plan to spend on generating answers to it.

**Reframing a problem from multiple angles before committing to a solution direction is not indecision — it is the creative act itself. Utley teaches that a problem stated from a different vantage point often reveals solutions that were entirely invisible from the original framing. The skill of holding multiple framings simultaneously, rather than locking onto the first one, is what distinguishes expert innovators from novices.** ([source](Stanford Webinar — Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Build a 'reframe round' into every problem-solving session: before generating solutions, require the team to articulate the problem from at least three different perspectives — the customer's, the competitor's, and the skeptic's — and notice where the framings diverge.

**Design Thinking, as taught at the Stanford d.school, places empathy and problem definition ahead of ideation for a structural reason: you cannot generate solutions to a problem you do not yet understand. The 'define' phase — where teams synthesize observations into a precise, human-centered problem statement — is not a bureaucratic checkpoint but the creative core of the entire process.** ([source](Stanford Webinar — Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Teams that skip directly to brainstorming after a brief user research phase are likely optimizing a problem statement that was never stress-tested. Build in explicit synthesis sessions where raw observations are turned into crisp problem statements before any solution generation begins.

**The 'How Might We' construction is deliberately optimistic in its grammar.** 'how' assumes a solution exists, 'might' removes the pressure of finding the only solution, and 'we' signals that it is a collective challenge. Each word in the formulation does work. Changing any one of them — to 'how can we' or 'how should we' — subtly narrows the creative aperture in ways that matter at scale. ([source](Stanford Webinar — Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Train teams on the anatomy of a 'How Might We' question so they can diagnose when their question is inadvertently constraining the solution space. The linguistic precision of the question is not pedantry — it is creative architecture.

**Utley draws a direct line between the quality of empathy research and the quality of problem definition.** Teams that spend more time genuinely understanding users — observing behavior, listening for unarticulated needs, noticing what people do rather than what they say — consistently produce sharper, more generative problem statements. Surface-level research produces surface-level questions; deep human understanding produces questions that open unexpected doors. ([source](Stanford Webinar — Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Do not allow a problem statement to be finalized until the team has direct contact with the people experiencing the problem. Secondhand research, internal assumptions, and market data alone produce problem statements that reflect what the team already believed — not what the user actually needs.

**One of Utley's recurring teaching moves is to distinguish between the problem as presented and the problem worth solving. Users, clients, and stakeholders present problems through the lens of their existing mental models, which means the stated problem is always filtered through assumptions about what kinds of solutions are possible. The innovation practitioner's job is to look through the stated problem to the underlying need it reflects, then ask whether there is a better way to frame that need as a design challenge.** ([source](Stanford Webinar - Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** In client or stakeholder briefings, treat the initial problem statement as raw material for problem-finding, not as a mandate. Develop a standard reframing protocol — a set of questions you ask in every briefing — that systematically surfaces the underlying need behind the stated problem and opens the conversation to alternative framings before any solution work begins.

**Posing multiple questions — rather than a single question — is a foundational creative workflow principle.** Many people fail to innovate because they are solving the wrong problem; asking only one question locks you in if that question is incorrect. Exploring a range of questions and generating multiple answers reduces that risk and surfaces the breadth of ideas needed for genuine innovation. ([source](Creativity in the Age of AI: A Conversation with Jeremy Utley))

> *"One of the most important things is to pose more than one question. Seek more than one answer. Try more than one thing... Often, people are seeking to address the wrong problem. If you ask only one question, and it's the wrong one, you're stuck. But if you ask more than one, you minimize that risk."*

**Implication:** Creative workflows should be designed with deliberate question-divergence phases before converging on a problem definition. AI makes this low-cost — use it to rapidly explore multiple problem framings before committing to one.

**Problem articulation is itself a meaningful and non-trivial innovation output.** Drawing on John Dewey's principle that 'a problem well put is half solved,' Utley argues that investing in problem framing often makes solutions nearly self-evident — yet organizations systematically undervalue this work. ([source](Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley))

> *"John Dewey, who's an American educational philosopher, he said, a problem well put is half solved. And a lot of times what we find is when you frame the problem properly, the solution almost handles itself."*

**Implication:** Organizations should formally recognize and resource problem-definition work as a deliverable in its own right, not just a precursor to 'real' solution work.

**Utley identifies the bias toward solution-finding as one of the most persistent obstacles to genuine innovation.** Organizations prize problem-solving so highly that they shortcut the problem-definition phase, often solving the wrong problem efficiently. ([source](Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley))

> *"There's kind of a default bias because we prize problem solving so much. We almost always put a premium on solution finding. And yet, oftentimes, focusing on the problem... a problem well put is half solved."*

**Implication:** High-performing innovation teams should deliberately slow down before the solution phase and invest disproportionate time in problem articulation — counterintuitive but consistently more productive.

---

## Leadership & Enabling Innovation

**Vision is a function of imagination, and imagination is a function of experience.** Leaders who are not personally experimenting with AI cannot develop a rich enough imagination to cast a compelling vision for what AI makes possible. The failure to inspire is upstream of strategic failure. ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

**Implication:** Asking leaders to set AI strategy before they have personal AI experience is backwards — the prerequisite for bold AI vision is hands-on imaginative exposure, not strategic frameworks.

**Most organizations have dramatically over-invested in AI governance and compliance policies while under-investing in inspiring vision. When 90% of CEOs have a governance policy but only 10% have written an inspiring manifesto about AI's possibilities, the organizational signal is clear: people have been told what not to do but not what to hope for.** ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

**Implication:** The highest-leverage AI leadership action for most organizations is not another policy but an inspiring articulation of possibility — the asymmetry between compliance investment and vision investment is itself the bottleneck.

**The question 'Did you work with AI on this?' functions as a permission structure, not an interrogation.** When people say no, the follow-up 'Why didn't you?' or 'Why don't you do a second pass?' opens creative possibilities rather than assigning blame — normalizing AI use as the professional default. ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

> *"Did you work with AI on this? I think is another way to give permission because when people say no, then you can say, why didn't you? Or then even better, why don't you do a second pass and see how AI can help you punch it up."*

**Implication:** Leaders can shift organizational AI culture through two diagnostic questions asked consistently — 'Have you tried AI?' and 'Did you work with AI on this?' — both of which give permission, surface ideas, and generate follow-up stories worth celebrating.

**Asking 'Have you tried AI?' accomplishes three things simultaneously.** it gives the person an idea they hadn't considered, it gives explicit permission to use AI (signaling it's not cheating), and it earns the leader the right to follow up and collect success stories worth sharing broadly. ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

> *"The first thing is most of the time that gives the person you ask an idea. Second, asking them 'have you tried AI' gives permission. And then the third thing that it does is it gives you as a leader the opportunity to follow up with someone and ask how did AI help, which then stocks your repository of stories you can share."*

**Implication:** This single repeatable question is a compounding leadership tool — each deployment generates ideas, permission, and stories that multiply AI adoption far beyond what training programs achieve.

**Leaders who celebrate AI experimentation publicly — sharing their screen at all-hands, posting Loom videos of cool workflows, spotlighting team members' AI wins — create the cultural permission structure that no policy can manufacture. What leaders celebrate becomes what the organization normalizes.** ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

> *"All of the best leaders I know have some version of the following: either on every all-hands they're sharing their screen and showing something cool they've seen, or in the team Slack they're posting a one-minute video. People pay attention to what leaders celebrate. So the question of what do you celebrate really matters."*

**Implication:** Public celebration of AI experimentation is the highest-leverage cultural intervention available to leaders — it simultaneously normalizes use, signals permission, and generates the shared vocabulary organizations need to accelerate adoption.

**Board members and senior leaders who do not spend meaningful time in AI every day are the organizational bottleneck — not because they block initiatives but because they lack the imaginative basis to hold their organizations to a high standard of AI ambition. The board that doesn't know what's possible can't demand it.** ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

> *"If you all individually are not spending at least an hour in AI every day, you are the bottleneck for this organization. If the board has no concept of what's possible, they cannot possibly hold the CEO to a high standard of excellence and a high standard of vision."*

**Implication:** AI transformation programs that target front-line employees while leaving boards and C-suites uninstructed will stall — the approval and ambition ceiling is set by the people at the top, and that ceiling is constrained by their personal AI imagination.

**When a leader knows what's possible with AI, they can compress timelines and budgets by an order of magnitude — transforming an eight-week, $50K prototype estimate into an eight-hour, $500 proof of concept. This only happens when leaders have the imaginative baseline to challenge assumptions rather than approve them.** ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

> *"The average response was 50K and eight weeks to build a prototype. Me and my partner built one of the ideas during lunch. If a senior leader goes, 'Yeah right, I'll give you eight hours and $500,' that same dynamic has to take place at the board level. If they know what's possible, they start demanding more."*

**Implication:** A leader's personal AI fluency directly translates to organizational speed and resource efficiency — the gap between 'eight weeks' and 'built during lunch' is entirely a function of whether the approving leader knows what's actually possible.

**Leaders cannot enforce on others what they are not practicing themselves.** People are highly attuned to hypocrisy, and the reason leaders hesitate to hold their teams to AI standards is often that they are not meeting those standards themselves. Modeling must precede mandating. ([source](linkedin_live:jeremy_utley_ai_fatigue))

**Implication:** Any leader experiencing resistance to their AI expectations should start by auditing their own practice — the credibility gap between what is demanded and what is modeled is usually the root cause of team non-compliance.

**Leaders who demonstrate AI use publicly on their highest-stakes work — not just low-effort tactical tasks — give everyone in the organization permission to use AI strategically. The example a leader models calibrates the aspiration level of the entire organization.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"If you want them to be strategic and to look for really impactful use cases, then you have to demonstrate how impactfully it can be used for you in your role. Which means if you're the CEO, the most important thing you can be doing is updating your board on your progress and your problems and your priorities and you're showing your team how you're using AI to prepare for that conversation."*

**Implication:** Leaders who only share AI wins on email drafts inadvertently signal that AI is for trivial work; sharing AI use on board prep, strategic planning, and difficult conversations raises the organizational ambition for AI application.

**Leaders who model AI learning publicly — sharing their screen, making Loom videos of their own AI use, refusing to level up privately while expecting their teams to level up publicly — create the cultural permission and practical templates that accelerate organizational adoption.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

**Implication:** Leadership modeling of AI learning should be visible and narrated, not just practiced in private — the act of sharing the messy learning process is itself a powerful cultural signal.

**Senior executives often have no one to talk to about genuine strategic anxieties — they can't speak openly to their boards or their employees. Peer CEO collectives that provide a psychologically safe space for candid exchange fill a structural gap in leadership support.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"I can't talk to my board about this stuff and I can't talk to my employees about this stuff... I asked another CEO that I was talking with later, a different industry, hey, who else do you talk to about this stuff? And they said, 'You. That's it. I can't talk to my board. I can't talk to my employees.'"*

**Implication:** Organizations and networks serving senior leaders should explicitly design for candid peer dialogue on strategic uncertainty — the isolation at the top is a real phenomenon that limits decision quality.

**The top-left quadrant — high-value work done with low-quality AI collaboration — is arguably the most costly failure mode. Leaders who accept a single AI answer on a major strategic decision, or who dismiss AI after getting one bad answer, are applying high stakes to low sophistication in a way that compounds rather than reduces risk.** ([source](youtube:The 10-Second Test That Shows If You're Behind on AI))

> *"The top left is dangerous in a different way. High value work done with low quality AI collaboration. This is the executive who asks AI a single strategic question, takes the first answer, and applies it to a major strategic decision. Or even worse, the executive who asks a single strategic question, knows that the first answer is bogus, and therefore decides AI is not relevant to this process. High stakes, low sophistication is arguably the most costly quadrant."*

**Implication:** Senior leaders are uniquely exposed to this failure mode because their work is inherently high-stakes — a single unsophisticated AI interaction can produce both bad decisions and lasting dismissal of AI's potential.

**Leaders cast an enormous shadow on AI adoption.** When a senior leader publicly and honestly shares their own AI experiments — including failures and modifications — they destigmatize AI use and call out experimenters across the organization who would otherwise remain hidden. ([source](youtube:Stanford-Jeremy-Utley-AI-brainstorming))

> *"Folks give attention to that leaders cast an enormous shadow and if as a leader you are forthright about your own experiments and your own interest you're going to call folks out of the corners of the organization who are similarly experimental."*

**Implication:** The highest-leverage AI adoption intervention for leaders is not policy or training — it is personal, public, candid disclosure of their own AI practice, including what doesn't work.

**AI should be framed as an abundance tool — asking 'how much more could we accomplish with turbocharged people?' — rather than an efficiency tool asking 'how many fewer people do we need?' The scarcity/efficiency orientation misses the transformative potential that an abundance orientation unlocks.** ([source](youtube:Stanford-Jeremy-Utley-AI-brainstorming))

> *"There's one way to think about the world with generative AI which is how many less people can we eek the same performance out of... then there's a totally different perspective which is how much more could we accomplish with our people now that they have these turbocharged abilities."*

**Implication:** Leaders who frame AI as a headcount reduction tool will extract a fraction of its value compared to leaders who frame it as a capability amplifier — the framing shapes every downstream decision about investment and deployment.

**A leader can prime continued ideation between meetings by explicitly stating a belief that the best ideas haven't occurred yet and that all whiteboard work so far is merely priming for better ideas to come. This reframe turns closing a session into an opening act rather than a conclusion.** ([source](youtube:AI_in_Learning_Ep1_JeremyUtley))

> *"I believe that our best ideas haven't occurred to us yet. All of the work that we've been doing on the whiteboard here is just priming us to individually think of better stuff between now and the next meeting."*

**Implication:** A single sentence from a leader at the close of a brainstorm can meaningfully extend the creative lifespan of a session by repositioning it as the beginning of a generative interval.

**Leaders who have conviction about AI should be loudly and publicly sharing use cases and spotlighting examples.** Leaders who feel curiosity rather than conviction should honestly declare they are on a learning journey. Both stances are legitimate and productive — the failure mode is silence in either direction. ([source](youtube:AI_in_Learning_Ep1_JeremyUtley))

> *"The leaders who have conviction should be boldly sharing use cases, examples, spotlighting, highlighting, giving shoutouts at every chance they can. The leaders who feel a sense of curiosity should be honestly expressing that they're on the learning journey. Both are fine and both are great. The question is recognizing it is a learning curve."*

**Implication:** Leadership AI communication strategy should be differentiated by genuine conviction level — authentic vulnerability about learning is more effective than performed certainty that erodes credibility.

**The defining cultural shift from the Ballmer era to the Nadella era at Microsoft has been characterized as moving from 'know-it-all' to 'learn-it-all.' In the current environment, being a learn-it-all has never been more important.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"The big shift from the Ballmer era of Microsoft to the Satya Nadella era has been described as this: from know-it-all to learn-it-all. It has never been more important than it is right now to be a learn-it-all."*

**Implication:** The competitive advantage has shifted from depth of existing knowledge to speed of acquiring new knowledge — leaders and organizations that optimize for learning rate will outpace those that optimize for expertise display.

**When there is pressure to be the expert, you cannot admit what you don't know — and this creates a ceiling on both personal learning and team psychological safety. The shift to 'learn-it-all' culture requires leaders to model not-knowing publicly.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"There's so much pressure to know. There's so much pressure to be the expert. And here's the problem. When you're expected to know, you can't admit that you don't know."*

**Implication:** Expert-identity pressure is an innovation tax — leaders who tie status to knowing rather than learning suppress both their own growth and their team's willingness to surface uncertainty.

**Leaders who ask 'show me three' before giving feedback — requiring multiple alternatives before evaluating any single solution — create conditions where ideation and alternatives-generation are structurally valued. This is a simple management tactic with profound implications for team creative output.** ([source](youtube:talks-at-google-ideaflow))

> *"Bob McKim would always respond with, show me three. I want to see options before I can give you feedback. That's a really, really great management and leadership tactic. And every CEO should have that ingrained in their minds."*

**Implication:** 'Show me three' is a concrete, immediately implementable leadership behavior that shifts team norms from presenting conclusions to generating options — any manager at any level can adopt it today.

**Rather than trying to change organizational culture from the bottom up — a largely futile effort — individuals should focus on 'managing down': creating conditions for ideation and experimentation within their own sphere of influence, with the people who report to them.** ([source](youtube:talks-at-google-ideaflow))

> *"You can't control what your boss says or what the CEO says about what's allowed. You do have control over how people roll up to you. Trying to change things upwards is a fool's errand. Trying to change things downward is a bold, high-agency act of courage."*

**Implication:** Every manager, regardless of seniority, has the authority to build a creative micro-culture within their team — waiting for organizational permission to innovate is a choice, not a constraint.

**Jeremy's primary focus as a Stanford educator has shifted toward equipping student leaders not just to use design thinking techniques themselves, but to impart those principles to others and lead teams in a design thinking manner.** ([source](youtube:Stanford Instructor - Meet Jeremy Utley))

> *"I'm primarily focused on equipping student leaders not just to engage the techniques of design thinking, but really to enable them and equip them to impart those principles to other people and to lead teams in a design thinking manner."*

**Implication:** The highest-leverage design thinking education builds multipliers — people who can spread the capability through organizations — rather than individual practitioners.

**Conviction built through direct user empathy gives innovators the confidence to persist past organizational resistance.** Bill Pacheco continued developing his treadmill innovation despite explicit rejection from his boss because his firsthand knowledge of users' fear was stronger than his boss's opinion. ([source](stanford-webinar-design-thinking-what-is-it))

> *"What happened for Bill is he got conviction that people were afraid. People felt unsafe. And as he's watching and interacting with people in gyms, that conviction grew. There's a need for a perception of safety here that I feel is a real human need, a real human pain that deserves to be met."*

**Implication:** Deep user empathy is not just a research tool — it is a source of personal conviction that enables innovators to withstand organizational pressure to abandon promising ideas.

**To create space for innovation ownership, leaders must also specify what the person will stop doing.** Assigning new exploratory work without removing existing obligations is a reliable way to ensure the new work never happens. ([source](stanford-webinar-design-thinking-what-is-it))

> *"The other thing they'd say is what are they going to stop doing? You can't give somebody something new without also kind of drawing back some level of responsibility. So which two or three standing meetings do they not need to attend in the next week so that they can create that data?"*

**Implication:** Innovation capacity is a resource allocation problem as much as a creative problem — leaders who add without subtracting are signaling that innovation is optional.

**When a boss rejects an idea, they are reacting to the idea with no information — only their past experience.** Prototyping and testing creates new information that bypasses opinion and makes organizational resistance harder to sustain. ([source](stanford-webinar-design-thinking-what-is-it))

> *"His boss is reacting to an idea, but he has no information. He doesn't understand. There's no way to contextualize the idea other than according to his own past history. And what Bill realized and what's really magical is part of the design mindset is saying, really quickly I can actually create data."*

**Implication:** The strategic response to organizational resistance is not argumentation — it is rapid evidence generation that transforms a debate about opinions into a conversation about facts.

**Utley notes that the organizational incentive structure typically punishes problem-finding and rewards solution-presenting, which creates a systemic bias toward premature solution generation. Presenting a well-defined problem without a solution attached reads as incompetence in most corporate environments, so people skip the definition work and go straight to proposals. This social dynamic is one of the primary structural causes of innovation failure that is almost never named.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on How to Build an AI Culture to Lead for the Future — video))

**Implication:** Leaders who want better innovation outcomes need to actively reward and celebrate rigorous problem-framing work, even when it arrives without a solution attached. Create explicit recognition moments for teams that identify a better question, reframe a problem, or discover that the original challenge was pointing in the wrong direction.

**The common leadership mistake in AI adoption is treating it as a technical rollout rather than a cultural shift.** Leaders who deploy AI tools without addressing the psychological experience of using them — the anxiety of not knowing, the social risk of being seen experimenting — find low adoption regardless of how good the technology is. The human layer is the implementation challenge. ([source](Jeremy Utley on How to Build an AI Culture to Lead for the Future))

**Implication:** AI rollout strategy should include explicit attention to the emotional and social dynamics of adoption: normalizing early confusion, protecting psychological safety for experimentation, and ensuring that early adopters are recognized rather than resented for their competency advantage.

**Utley has observed that the best AI practitioners inside organizations are often invisible — they quietly do better work but don't broadcast their methods because research shows that AI-assisted work is perceived by peers as less competent and less effortful. This creates a paradox: the people most capable of spreading AI literacy have the strongest social incentives to hide it.** ([source](The AI Fatigue Myth & What Leaders Can Actually Do About It))

**Implication:** Leaders who want AI capability to diffuse across their organizations must actively create social cover for skilled practitioners to share their methods. Recognition programs, protected learning time, and explicit normalization from the top are required because market forces alone will suppress knowledge sharing.

**Utley describes a characteristic pattern he observes with skeptical executives.** they believe AI is hype or limited until they witness it build meaningful work product — with their own strategic questions — in real time in front of them. Imagination about what is possible expands through witnessing, not through argument. This has significant implications for how leaders should introduce AI capability to resistant peers and boards. ([source](AI as Your New Strategic Thought Partner with Jeremy Utley, Stanford, Ideaflow, & Beyond the Prompt))

**Implication:** When making the case for AI investment to skeptical senior leaders, skip the ROI slides and run a live demonstration using their actual current challenge. Ten minutes of watching AI engage with a real problem they care about does more persuasive work than any deck could accomplish. The goal is imagination expansion, not rational argument.

**Utley frames the leader's relationship to AI adoption within their organization as an environmental design challenge: the question is not which tools to mandate but which norms, structures, and signals will produce genuine daily experimentation across the team. Mandates produce compliance; environments produce learning. The difference shows up in whether people use AI to check a box or to actually think differently.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on How to Build an AI Culture to Lead for the Future))

**Implication:** Resist the temptation to create AI mandates or usage quotas as the primary adoption lever. Instead, design for voluntary daily use: protect experimentation time, normalize failure, share interesting use cases in team meetings, and let leaders demonstrate their own ongoing AI practice visibly.

**Utley has pointed out that what leaders call 'AI fatigue' inside their organizations is frequently a cover story for something more specific: their people are anxious about falling behind, and that anxiety makes engagement feel threatening rather than appealing. The leader's job is to reframe the entry point — not 'you need to catch up' but 'you're not behind yet, and here's how to begin.'** ([source](The AI Fatigue Myth & What Leaders Can Actually Do About It))

**Implication:** AI change management language matters enormously. Urgency framing ('everyone is ahead of you') increases avoidance. Access framing ('here is a low-stakes place to start') increases engagement. Leaders should audit their internal AI communications for whether they are triggering anxiety or inviting curiosity.

**Utley has argued that when leaders skip the 'should we' question about AI and go straight to selling capability, they generate the very backlash they then dismiss as fear or resistance. People are often making a genuine values objection — about authorship, effort, or human judgment — not a capability complaint. Leaders who recognize this distinction respond with dialogue rather than evangelism, and achieve far better adoption outcomes.** ([source](The AI Fatigue Myth & What Leaders Can Actually Do About It))

**Implication:** Before launching an AI capability program, surface and explicitly address the 'should we' questions your team is carrying: concerns about authenticity, credit, skill atrophy, or job displacement. Teams whose values concerns are heard first become far more willing to engage with the capability questions afterward.

**Utley has noted that the daily AI use habit is more important than sophisticated AI use, and this principle has a direct leadership implication: the organization's AI capability compounds fastest when leaders build structures that create daily touchpoints with the technology for everyone, not when they invest in advanced training for a small technical cohort. Consistency across the team beats depth in a few individuals.** ([source](How to Get Ahead of 99% of People (with AI)))

**Implication:** Structure AI exposure as a daily ambient habit rather than an occasional skill-building event. Brief daily prompts in team standups, weekly use-case shares, and integration of AI into existing workflows at low stakes all produce more compounded capability growth than quarterly intensive workshops.

**Psychological safety is not a soft prerequisite to innovation — it is the mechanism by which innovation happens.** Without it, team members self-censor the unconventional ideas that are statistically most likely to contain breakthroughs. Safe environments don't produce comfortable mediocrity; they produce the strange ideas that, at high volume, yield outlier results. ([source](Episode 259 – Practicing Innovation with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Leaders who dismiss psychological safety as HR language are misunderstanding its function in the innovation pipeline. Safety is not about feelings — it is about ensuring that the full distribution of ideas, including the weird ones at the tail, actually makes it into the room.

**Leaders model the norms that govern creative risk in their organizations.** When a leader visibly shares an unformed idea, admits uncertainty, or builds on a subordinate's half-baked concept, they are broadcasting that creative vulnerability is acceptable. No training program or poster campaign can substitute for this direct behavioral signal from the top. ([source](The Science of Idea Flow with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** The most effective innovation culture intervention available to any leader is free: publicly take creative risk yourself in front of your team. Share an idea before it's ready, say 'I don't know' about a strategic question, or ask a naive question in a meeting you're supposed to own.

**Utley observes that leaders frequently confuse a generation problem for an evaluation problem when it comes to innovation. They assume they need better criteria for selecting ideas when the real deficit is that the team is not generating enough ideas in the first place. Volume must come before selection; a thin pipeline is not improved by a sharper filter.** ([source](Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas with Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** When innovation results disappoint, resist the reflex to build more rigorous evaluation frameworks. Instead, ask whether your team is generating ideas at a rate that gives randomness and variety a chance to surface something novel. The diagnostic question is quantity, not quality of selection.

**Utley has consistently emphasized that the highest-leverage innovation leadership skill is protecting the divergent phase of creative work from premature convergence. This is harder than it sounds because premature evaluation feels productive — it looks like rigor. The discipline of holding the question open longer than is comfortable is what distinguishes innovation-enabling leaders from those who confuse decisiveness with wisdom.** ([source](Episode 248: Jeremy Utley — unSILOed Podcast))

**Implication:** Train yourself and your leadership team to notice when the group is converging prematurely — the telltale sign is evaluative language entering a generative conversation. Call it out explicitly: 'We're moving to evaluation mode before we've finished generating — let's hold judgment a few more minutes.'

**Utley has observed that the zero-to-one myth — the belief that breakthroughs require starting from nothing — paralyzes both individuals and organizations. Innovation almost always builds on existing assets, recombined in new ways. Leaders who frame innovation as requiring blank-slate reinvention inadvertently raise the creative stakes so high that teams freeze; leaders who frame it as recombination of existing strengths lower the barrier enough that generation actually begins.** ([source](Zero to One: The Myth That Keeps Individuals, Teams, and Organizations from Breakthrough Innovation))

**Implication:** Reframe your team's innovation challenge away from 'invent something new' toward 'what existing capability, asset, or insight could we apply in an adjacent context?' This framing activates connection-making rather than demanding revelation, which is both more accurate and more generative.

**The leader is the single biggest variable in whether a team feels safe to generate and share ideas.** When a leader signals — even subtly — that only polished, defensible ideas are welcome, the team stops generating anything strange or early-stage. The environment the leader creates either multiplies or suffocates creative output across the entire organization. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Leaders who want more innovation from their teams should audit their own reactions to bad ideas first. The meeting culture, the facial expressions, the first responses to rough pitches — these are the actual innovation policy, regardless of what the org chart or strategy deck says.

**Leaders who hoard ideation — who position themselves as the source of strategic insight or creative direction — become the bottleneck in their own organizations. Every idea must pass through one node, and that node has finite bandwidth. Distributing the generative function across the team multiplies creative capacity in proportion to team size.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** If your team consistently waits for the leader's take before generating options, the organization has an ideation bottleneck that looks like a talent problem but is actually a structural one. The fix is delegation of the generative function, not better hiring.

**The reward signals inside an organization communicate more about what leadership actually values than any stated innovation priority. If only polished presentations get promoted, if early-stage thinking is penalized in performance reviews, if failure is quietly held against people — then the organization's creative capacity will contract regardless of how many innovation labs are funded.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Leaders should audit the reward and recognition signals in their system before launching any innovation initiative. If the incentive structure punishes creative risk, the initiative will fail not because the people lack creativity but because the system is rationally discouraging it.

**Utley argues that leaders should treat ideaflow — the volume and velocity of ideas generated across a team — as a measurable, manageable leading indicator rather than hoping innovation culture will emerge organically. When leaders track this metric regularly, they gain visibility into creative health before it shows up (or fails to show up) in revenue or product launches.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Add a simple ideaflow count to team retrospectives and innovation sprints. Tracking the number is less important than the discipline of asking the question: are we generating more or fewer ideas than last quarter? The act of measuring the metric signals that generation is a leadership priority.

**Utley argues that idea quotas — explicit, uncomfortably high targets like fifty or a hundred ideas — are not motivational gimmicks but structural mechanisms that change the social contract of a meeting. When the goal is a large number, the group shifts from 'say something impressive' to 'help us hit the target,' which is the only condition that makes it socially safe for unconventional ideas to survive long enough to be heard.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Leaders running innovation sessions should set numerical idea targets that feel absurdly high, and make hitting the number a shared team objective. This reframes the session away from evaluation and competition toward collective generation — and the strange ideas that wouldn't survive individual exposure often make it through under the protection of the quota.

**A leader's job in innovation is not to be the best idea generator in the room — it is to design the conditions under which ideas can emerge from everyone. This reframe shifts the leader's role from performer to architect: the relevant skill set is meeting structure, reward signals, and modeling creative risk, not creative brilliance.** ([source](Jeremy Utley and Perry Klebahn | Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** Leaders who see themselves as the chief ideator are structurally limiting their organization. The intervention is to stop contributing ideas first in meetings and start building the room conditions that allow others to contribute — which requires ego discipline more than creative talent.

**Meeting structure is a leadership intervention with enormous leverage on creative output.** Most organizations inadvertently blend divergent thinking and convergent evaluation within the same meeting, which means evaluation always dominates and the strangest — often most valuable — ideas are killed before anyone can hear them. Separating these phases is a structural decision leaders must make deliberately. ([source](Jeremy Utley and Perry Klebahn | Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** Before the next brainstorm, leaders should explicitly announce which phase the meeting is in, protect the generative phase from early evaluation, and schedule a separate session for selection. This single structural change yields more usable ideas than any creativity workshop.

**Most organizations have a generation problem masquerading as an evaluation problem.** Leaders invest heavily in frameworks for selecting winning ideas — stage gates, portfolio processes, venture boards — while the pipeline feeding those frameworks remains thin. A sophisticated filter applied to a weak idea pool still produces mediocrity; the leverage is almost always upstream of the funnel. ([source](Jeremy Utley and Perry Klebahn | Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** Before improving your innovation selection process, measure how many ideas are actually entering the pipeline. If the answer is small relative to team size, the investment priority should be increasing generative volume, not refining evaluation criteria.

**When leaders default to providing answers rather than asking questions, they inadvertently signal that the team's generative function is not needed. Teams that are consistently told what to think stop practicing thinking, and their ideaflow declines over time. The leader who asks genuine questions — ones they don't already know the answer to — activates the creative capacity of the entire room.** ([source](Jeremy Utley and Perry Klebahn | Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** Replace at least one answer per meeting with a question. Not a rhetorical question, but a genuine one: 'What are we not seeing here?' or 'What would have to be true for this not to work?' The discipline of staying in question mode longer than is comfortable is a direct investment in team creative capacity.

**Utley and Klebahn's work at Stanford's d.school demonstrates that innovation is teachable at scale — not just to naturally creative individuals but to entire organizations with the right process design. The implication for leadership is that creative output is an organizational design problem, not a talent acquisition problem. You cannot hire your way to an innovative culture; you have to build the conditions for it.** ([source](Stanford Webinar - Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Leaders who believe their organization lacks creative talent should examine whether their processes and environment are designed to produce creative output. The d.school model shows that virtually any group, given the right structure, can generate high-quality innovative ideas — talent is far less limiting than process.

**Utley challenges the leadership assumption that innovation culture is built through inspiration — keynotes, away days, culture campaigns. His position, grounded in the d.school methodology, is that culture is built through repeated practice of specific behaviors in structured environments. You do not inspire a team into creative confidence; you run them through deliberate exercises until the behavior becomes habitual.** ([source](Stanford Instructor - Meet Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Redirect innovation culture investment from inspirational events toward regular, structured creative practice. Brief weekly ideaflow exercises, consistent meeting facilitation protocols, and monthly framing workshops compound into durable behavioral change in a way that annual innovation summits do not.

**Utley positions the leader as the chief question-asker rather than the chief answer-provider in an innovation context.** The practical expression of this is that leaders should invest disproportionate time upstream — in crafting the right 'How Might We' framing of the problem — before the team generates any solutions. The quality of the question determines the ceiling on the quality of the answers. ([source](Stanford Webinar - Design Thinking: What is it and why should I care?))

**Implication:** Before any innovation sprint, leaders should spend significant time — more than feels proportionate — on problem framing. Bring multiple reframings of the challenge to the team, test which framing generates the most productive generative energy, and only then open the floor to solutions.

**Every organizational communication about AI — every all-hands, every change management memo, every enthusiastic demo — is an appraisal event. If the framing triggers replacement-fear rather than curiosity, the brain's threat response suppresses the very learning the organization is trying to produce.** ([source](Not Behind Enough to Panic. Too Afraid to Learn.))

> *"Every all-hands presentation, every change management communication, every enthusiastic demo from your most AI-forward leader — each of those is an appraisal event. Your people's brains are running a threat-assessment in real time. If the framing triggers replacement-fear rather than curiosity, the dopamine doesn't fire."*

**Implication:** Leaders must audit the implicit framing of every AI communication. 'Here's what's possible' and 'here's what's coming for you' are categorically different messages with categorically different neurological and learning outcomes.

**Even the most well-intentioned AI enthusiasts in an organization may be the biggest threat-triggers in the room.** The more impressive the demo and the more fluent the prompts, the higher the appraisal threat generated in people who aren't yet fluent — potentially suppressing their curiosity and learning. ([source](Not Behind Enough to Panic. Too Afraid to Learn.))

> *"You're probably already enthusiastic about AI. Which means you're probably the most well-intentioned threat-trigger in the room. The more impressive your demo, the more fluent your prompts, the more it demonstrates what's possible — the higher the appraisal threat you may be generating in someone who isn't yet fluent."*

**Implication:** AI champions and early adopters need to actively modulate how they demonstrate capability. Leading with vulnerability and process, not just polished outputs, may be more effective at enabling peer adoption.

---

## Design Thinking & Stanford d.school

**MacKinnon's most creative architects were far more comfortable leaving decisions unmade — they delayed closure and sat with ambiguity longer than their less creative colleagues. This tolerance for open questions is not indecision; it is a deliberate strategy for activating deeper creative processing.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"The most creative architects weren't any smarter than their peers, but there was something else that distinguished them. They were far more comfortable leaving decisions unmade. They delayed closure. They sat with ambiguity far longer than their less creative colleagues did."*

**Implication:** Organizations that reward fast closure and penalize ambiguity tolerance are systematically eliminating the conditions in which breakthrough ideas are born.

**Jeremy's work exists at the intersection of organizational application and student education — each context continuously informs and improves the other. Teaching student leaders and working with corporations creates a reinforcing feedback loop that deepens his understanding of design thinking in both domains.** ([source](youtube:Stanford Instructor - Meet Jeremy Utley))

> *"I find that my work with organizations really informs how I approach students and my work with students really informs how I then approach organizational challenges."*

**Implication:** Practitioners who operate across both academic and applied settings develop richer, more tested frameworks than those confined to only one context.

**Design thinking's primary goal is to understand an end user — whether an external customer or internal constituent — better than they understand themselves. This depth of empathy enables teams to address latent, implicit needs the user may not even be aware of.** ([source](youtube:Stanford Instructor - Meet Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Surface-level user research is insufficient; the discipline demands going deeper than stated needs to uncover what people cannot yet articulate.

**The ultimate aim of deep user understanding is to produce innovations that are truly disruptive and breakthrough — ones that delight users and exceed their expectations rather than merely satisfying stated requirements.** ([source](youtube:Stanford Instructor - Meet Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Teams that stay at the surface of user needs will produce incremental improvements; only deep empathy unlocks the possibility of genuinely surprising and delighting people.

**Design thinking is no less rigorous than finance, consulting, or other traditionally analytical disciplines.** The perception of it as soft or unstructured is a misconception that dissolves on close examination. ([source](youtube:Stanford Instructor - Meet Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Resistance from analytically trained professionals toward design thinking is often based on a false premise; framing it as a rigorous discipline can reduce adoption friction among skeptical audiences.

**The three-step question generation process — (1) identify an area of life, (2) surface a struggle or uncertainty in that area, (3) reframe it into a behavioral 'how can I get someone to do something' question — is a teachable, repeatable method for producing design-ready challenges. This transforms amorphous frustration into structured creative prompts.** ([source](special_dschool_mission))

**Implication:** Giving people a structured scaffold for question generation removes the blank-page paralysis that often prevents practitioners from engaging meaningfully with design thinking methodology.

**Design thinking is most powerful when applied to a challenge that genuinely matters to the practitioner.** The pre-work assignment is designed not just to prepare questions but to ensure that the session addresses real, personally relevant struggles rather than hypothetical or assigned problems. ([source](special_dschool_mission))

> *"we're going to have a really great productive session about using design thinking on a challenge that matters to you"*

**Implication:** Intrinsic motivation in the problem space dramatically increases participant engagement and the practical applicability of design thinking outputs.

**Modeling the question-generation process with a personal, concrete example — rather than an abstract explanation — is a pedagogical choice that makes the method immediately accessible and replicable. Jeremy's 'students don't create, they only consume' example demonstrates exactly how to move from struggle to question.** ([source](special_dschool_mission))

**Implication:** Facilitators and teachers accelerate adoption of new frameworks by demonstrating the process with real personal examples rather than generic or hypothetical scenarios.
